Why Creation Is Not a Separate Act: Action as Essence
Maturidi argues that Allah's action is identical to His essence, making questions about the reason for creation invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah's action of creation is identical to His essence, making the question of 'why creation' as meaningless as asking why Allah is Lord.

Core Idea
Allah's action is His essence; asking why He created is like asking why He is generous.

This lesson explores a profound Maturidi reply to the question of why Allah created. Instead of giving a purpose-driven answer, Imam al-Maturidi redirects the inquiry by asserting that the action of creation is not separate from Allah's essence. Understanding this principle resolves many theological puzzles about divine motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the flow: Maturidi gives a possible answer, then declares the basis of the question corrupt.
The passage ends with a traditional formula.
2
Recognize the analogy: action = essence
Maturidi says we consider the action to be the essence, and Allah is described by it in pre-eternity. This means the act of creation is not a separate event but an essential attribute.
This is a key Maturidi doctrine.
3
Reflect on the implication
If action is essence, then asking 'why did He create?' is like asking 'why is He Lord?' Both are essential and uncaused. The question is thus invalid.
This protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جائز أن يقال: إذ هو بذاته جواد، وبذاته قادر، وبذاته عالم فجاد بخلقه على خلقه؛ إذ هو خلقه، إذ هو قادر على أن يجود ويُظهر مواهبه. وأصل هذا السؤال عندنا فاسد، لأنا نجعل الفعل هو الذات، وهو به موصوف في الأزل والسؤال عن ذلك كالسؤال على أنه لم كان رباً عالما . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then it is possible to say: Since He is by His essence Generous, and by His essence Capable, and by His essence Knowing, He was generous with His creation to His creatures; because He is their Creator, and He is able to be generous and manifest His gifts. And the basis of this question with us is corrupt, because we consider the action to be the essence, and He is described by it in pre-eternity; and the question about that is like the question about why He is Lord and Knowing. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
Imam al-Maturidi provides an additional argument: since Allah is generous, powerful, and knowing by His very essence, His act of creation is an act of generosity from His essential attributes. Therefore, the question about why He created reduces to asking why He is generous—i.e., why He is what He is. In Maturidi's theological framework, the attributes (including action) are not separate from the essence; the action of creation is the same as the essence in the sense that it is an eternal attribute. Thus the question is as invalid as asking why Allah is Lord or Knowing.

Key Terms
ذات
جواد
فعل
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation is a separate act that can be questioned as to why it occurred, as if Allah had a motive or reason.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that the action of creation is identical to Allah's essence, so asking 'why' is as invalid as asking why Allah is Lord—these are essential attributes without cause.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Maturidi, why is asking about the reason for creation like asking why Allah is Lord?
A) Because creation is separate from Allah
B) Because the action is the essence and He is described by it pre-eternally
C) Because creation is not an act of generosity
D) Because Allah's lordship is temporary

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi teaches that Allah's action is identical to His eternal essence, so the question 'why did He create?' is as invalid as asking why He is Lord.

Maturidi's Response to Objection about Unintelligible Commands
Maturidi answers an objection about God commanding what cannot be understood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God could address the servant with utterly unintelligible commands.

Core Idea
God always provides a way to understand every command, whether through intellect or revelation.

This passage presents a theological objection and Maturidi's careful response. The objection attempts to draw a parallelism between intellectual and revealed commands. Maturidi explains that while at first glance there seems to be no difference, in reality God always ensures understanding is possible. The answer highlights the role of intellect and revelation in grasping divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage which contains the objection. Note the structure: 'If it is said...' (فإن قيل). Then read the translation.
The objection tries to create a dilemma: if God can command through intellect without understanding, why not through revelation?
2
Identify Maturidi's key moves in the answer
Read the answer starting from 'قيل' (It is said). Notice how Maturidi first acknowledges the apparent similarity ('no difference in the first glance') but then rejects the blanket statement. Identify the two ways commands are given: through intellect (بعث العقل) or revelation (خطاب السمع).
Maturidi emphasizes that in every case, God provides a way for understanding.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider the principle: understanding is always possible for those capable. If someone's understanding falls short, they are exempt from the command. Also note the reference to 'aspects of principles' (جهات الأصول) and how reflection (النظر والفكر) determines the type.
This passage guards against the misconception that God might hold people accountable for unintelligible commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذ جاز أن يأمر الله العبد في عقله بما لا يفهم، لم لا جاز أن يخاطبه بما لا يفهمه؟ قيل : لا فرق بينهما في النظرة الأولى، ولكن لا يجوز الإطلاق عليه بالذي ذكرت وما من شيء يأمر الله به، إما ببعث العقل عليه أو بخطاب السمع، إلا وقد جعل الله لفهم ذلك سبيلاً. ومن قصر فهمه عن احتماله فهو خارج عن الأمر . لكن جهات الأصول مختلفة، تُعلم بالنظر والفكر أن ذلك من أي نوع. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If it is said: Since it is possible that God commands the servant in his intellect with what he does not understand, why is it not possible that He addresses him with what he does not understand? It is said: There is no difference between them in the first glance, but it is not permissible to make a general statement about Him with what you mentioned. There is nothing that God commands, whether by prompting the intellect to it or by the address of revelation, except that God has made a way for understanding it. And whoever's understanding falls short of bearing it is outside the command. But the aspects of the principles differ; it is known by reflection and thought which type it is. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues: if it is permissible for God to command something in the intellect that the servant does not understand, then why cannot God address the servant with something he does not understand at all? The answer distinguishes between initial appearance and reality. Maturidi replies that at first glance there is no difference, but one cannot make a blanket statement. In fact, whenever God commands something—whether through intellectual prompting (بعث العقل) or through revealed address (خطاب السمع)—He always provides a way to understand it. If someone's understanding is too limited to bear the command, that person is exempt from the command. However, the various sources of principles (جهات الأصول) differ, and one must use reflection and thought to determine which type a given command belongs to. The passage ends with the formula of reliance on Allah.

Key Terms
بعث العقل
خطاب السمع
جهات الأصول
النظر والفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God might hold people accountable for commands they cannot possibly understand.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that God always provides a way for understanding every command, either through intellect or revelation. If understanding is impossible, the command is not binding.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does God always provide for every command He issues?
A) A clear text without ambiguity.
B) A way for understanding it, either through intellect or revelation.
C) Immediate intuitive knowledge.
D) A prophet to explain it.

Key Takeaway
God's commands are always accompanied by a means of understanding, whether through the intellect or revealed scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command of Allah that you find difficult or confusing, do you assume the problem is with the command—or with your own effort to understand? Are you actively seeking the way He has provided, whether through intellect or scripture, or do you dismiss it as unintelligible?
?
Maturidi says that those whose understanding falls short are outside the command. Does this comfort you—or challenge you? Are you content to remain in ignorance of a command, or does this spur you to use reflection and thought to grasp what Allah requires of you?
?
The objection assumes that if one command is not understood, others might also be unintelligible. Is there any area of your faith where you have assumed that a ruling is arbitrary or meaningless, rather than trusting that Allah has provided a way for you to understand it? What would it take for you to seek that understanding?

Allah Creates All Actions, Good and Evil
Maturidi affirms Allah creates all actions and distinguishes creation from created act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi affirms that Allah creates both good and evil actions while remaining transcendent and not described by evil.

Core Idea
Allah creates all actions, but His act of creation is not tainted by the act's good or evil.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi addresses a delicate question: if Allah creates everything, does He create evil? And if so, does that make Him evil? The answer lies in a precise distinction between the act of creation and the created act. Maturidi uses this distinction to uphold divine transcendence while affirming Allah's sole lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Notice the key terms: خالق (Creator), جوهر (substance), خلق (creation), فعل (act).
The passage is dense but precise.
2
Identify the two main affirmations
First, Allah creates all actions, both good and evil. Second, there is a necessary distinction between creation and the created act.
These are the pillars of Maturidi's position.
3
Understand the refutation of partners
Maturidi argues that if anything existed without Allah's creation, that would imply a partner or equal in His dominion. This refutes dualism and Mu'tazila.
This is a logical argument from divine sovereignty.
4
Grasp the theological nuance
Allah's act of creation is not the same as the creature's act, and His action is not described as good or evil. Only the creature's act carries moral description.
This preserves Allah's transcendence.
5
Reflect on the implication
Allah is not called 'good' or 'evil' based on the actions He creates; rather, the doer is named by the deed.
This maintains divine justice and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عندنا فنحن نقول بأن الله جل جلاله خالق جوهر الشر والخير وخالق فعل الخلق شراً وخيرا. ولا يجوز كون شيء في سلطانه لم يخلقه، فيكون له شريك في سلطانه وعديل في خلق عالمه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ونقول بأن خلق الخلق ليس هو ذلك الخلق، وكذلك فعله؛ ولا يوصف فعله بالشر والخير، ولا يوصف بأن فعله خير وشر، لأنه موصوف بفعله. ولم نقل هو خير ولا شرير؛ ومن فِعْله ذلك في الحقيقة فهو مسمى به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And as for us, we say that Allah, majestic is His glory, is the Creator of the substance of evil and good and the Creator of the actions of creation, evil and good. And it is not permissible that anything exists in His dominion that He did not create, for then there would be for Him a partner in His dominion and an equal in the creation of His world – exalted is Allah above that and high. And we say that the creation of creation is not that creation, and likewise His act; and His action is not described by evil and good, nor is it described that His action is good or evil, because He is described by His action. And we do not say that He is good or evil; and the one whose actual deed is that in reality, he is named by it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the Maturidi creed regarding the creation of actions. The author affirms that Allah is the Creator of the substance (jawhar) of both evil and good, and He creates the actions of creatures – whether evil or good. This is a direct refutation of the dualists and a correction of the Mu'tazila. Then he asserts that nothing exists in Allah's dominion that He did not create, otherwise there would be a partner with Him. He then makes a crucial distinction: the creation (khalq) of creation is not identical to the created thing (makhluk); similarly, Allah's act (fi'l) is not the same as the act of the creature. Consequently, Allah's own act (i.e., His creating) is not described as good or evil, because He is described by His act (as Creator, etc.), not by the moral qualities of the created actions. Therefore, we do not say that Allah is 'good' or 'evil' in the sense of being characterized by the moral value of the actions He creates; rather, a creature whose deed is actually evil is called evil. The passage underscores divine transcendence and the rejection of anthropomorphism, while affirming that Allah is the sole creator of all actions.

Key Terms
خالق (Creator)
جوهر (substance)
خلق (creation)
فعل (act)
سلطان (dominion)
شريك (partner)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah creates evil actions, then He must be evil.
Correction: Maturidi explains that Allah's act of creation is distinct from the created act, and His action is not described by the moral qualities of what He creates. Thus, creating evil does not make Him evil.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is Allah not described as 'evil' despite creating evil actions?
A) Because Allah does not actually create evil
B) Because Allah's act of creation is not the same as the created act, and His action is not described by good or evil
C) Because evil is only metaphorical
D) Because Allah only creates good, and evil comes from another source

Key Takeaway
Allah creates all actions without being described by their moral qualities, because His act of creation is categorically different from the created act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever blame your circumstances or your own weakness, forgetting that Allah created that action? How does this lesson make you see your sin as fully your own while still affirming that nothing happens outside Allah's will? Will you now take full responsibility without despairing of His mercy?
?
If Allah creates both good and evil, how does that change your view of the trials you face? Are you willing to accept that even the evil that befalls you is from Him, and yet He is not evil? What would it take for you to truly submit to His decree without questioning its goodness?
?
This passage says Allah's act of creation is not described by good or evil. Does your heart sometimes judge Allah's actions by your own moral standards? How can you train yourself to trust that His wisdom is beyond your categories of good and evil, especially when life feels unfair?

The Wisdom in Creating Harmful Things
Maturidi explains the divine wisdom in creating harmful substances and sensory defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's creation of harmful and ugly things serves an epistemological purpose: enabling humans to analogize sensory experiences to understand the unseen.

Core Idea
Sensory contrasts are the ladder to knowledge of the unseen.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a common theological question: why does God create things that cause harm or appear ugly? Rather than being arbitrary or punitive, Maturidi explains that these phenomena are essential for human cognition. By experiencing opposites like pleasure and pain, beauty and ugliness, we develop the conceptual tools needed to reason about realities beyond our senses. Let's explore this profound argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the repeated contrast between 'qabīḥ' (ugly) and 'ḥasan' (beautiful).
2
Identify the main purpose
Look for the phrase 'li-yumaththilū' (so that they may analogize). This is the core purpose: to enable analogical reasoning from the visible to the invisible.
3
Trace the logical flow
Maturidi argues: Without sensory contrasts (harmful vs. beneficial, ugly vs. beautiful), humans would not know moral and practical distinctions. Therefore, God created them to facilitate reasoning about the unseen.
This is a 'if not... then...' argument.
4
Understand key term: tamthīl
Tamthīl (تمثيل) means drawing an analogy or comparison. Here it means using sensory experiences as a model for understanding what lies beyond the senses.
This word is central to the Maturidi epistemology.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
This argument shows that the existence of evil and ugliness is not a flaw in creation but a necessary feature for human knowledge and spiritual development.
Contrast this with views that see such things as pointless or punitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يجب في الحكمة من خلق الجواهر المؤذية والمناظر القبيحة وخلق الآفات في الحواس فهو بسبب أن البشر كلهم قد اعتقدوا شيئاً غاب عن حواسهم إما نفياً أو إثباتاً؛ منهم من دانوا، ومنهم من تجاهل وحصل على الشهوات. فإذا لم يخلق فيما يقع على الحواس ما ذكرنا لم يعرفوا القبيح من الحسن ولا المؤذى من النافع؛ وإذا لم يعقلوا ذلك لم يحتمل عقولهم درك القبيح من الحسن ولا المؤذى من الملذ. فخلق كذلك ليمثلوا بما تقع عليه الحواس ما لا تقع عليه، ليصير كل معتقد غاب عن البصر على ما عليه معروفاً بما يشاهد. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And what is necessitated by wisdom regarding the creation of harmful substances (jawahir) and ugly sights and the creation of defects in the senses is because all humans have believed in something absent from their senses, either by denial or affirmation; some of them became religious, and some feigned ignorance and sought pleasures. If He had not created in what falls upon the senses what we mentioned, they would not know the ugly from the beautiful nor the harmful from the beneficial; and if they did not comprehend that, their intellects would not bear the perception of the ugly from the beautiful nor the harmful from the pleasant. So He created thus so that they might analogize (yumaththilu) by what falls upon the senses to what does not fall upon them, so that every belief absent from sight becomes known as it is through what is witnessed. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the divine wisdom behind the creation of harmful things and sensory defects. It argues that humans universally hold beliefs about the unseen, and sensory experiences of harm and beauty serve as analogies for understanding the unseen. Without such sensory contrasts, humans would lack the conceptual framework to distinguish between good and evil. Therefore, God created these phenomena to facilitate analogical reasoning, allowing humans to infer truths about the unseen from what they observe.

Key Terms
حكمة
جوهر
حواس
تمثيل
قبيح
حسن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the creation of harmful things is arbitrary or without purpose.
Correction: The passage shows that harmful substances and sensory defects serve an epistemological function: they enable humans to analogize and understand the unseen, which is essential for moral and spiritual knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of creating harmful substances and ugly sights according to this passage?
A) To punish humans for their sins.
B) To enable humans to analogize and understand the unseen.
C) To test human patience.
D) To show that God is both good and evil.

Key Takeaway
The creation of harmful and ugly things is not purposeless, but serves to equip humans with the sensory contrasts needed for analogical reasoning about the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience something harmful or ugly in your life—an illness, a loss, or a difficult person—do you see it as a mere inconvenience or as a lesson from God about something deeper? What specific unseen truth might you be missing because you haven't analogized from that experience? Pledge today to pause and ask: 'What can this teach me about the unseen?'
?
Do you ever complain about the existence of evil or hardship in the world, treating it as pointless? Maturidi argues it is necessary for knowledge. How does your habitual response to difficulties reveal a lack of trust in this wisdom? What one harmful situation right now can you intentionally use as a ladder to understand a truth about God or the afterlife? Write down your commitment.
?
The passage says some people use sensory pleasures to feign ignorance. Are you using any worldly comfort to avoid thinking about the unseen? If so, what discomfort—physical or emotional—has God placed before you to awaken your intellect? Will you let it drive you to deeper belief, or will you ignore it? Be honest with yourself.

Harmful Things Also Benefit: Refuting Dualism
Maturidi argues that harmful substances also benefit, disproving a separate evil origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that all harmful substances also provide benefit, which refutes the claim of a separate originator of evil.

Core Idea
Every harmful thing benefits some creature, proving one Wise Creator.

This compact argument from Imam Maturidi tackles a perennial theological challenge: if harmful things exist, does that imply a separate evil creator? Maturidi points to a simple observation — harmful substances also benefit some creatures — to dismantle dualist assumptions. Reading this passage will show you how a single empirical fact can uphold tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: a premise about harmful substances, then a conclusion, then an implication.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the three key terms: جواهر مؤذية (harmful substances), مدبر حكيم عليم (Wise Knowing Manager), and منشئ الشر (originator of evil). Understand how they connect.
3
Follow the argument's logic
Trace Maturidi's reasoning: harmful things benefit some → they are not purely harmful → they must be from a single Wise Creator → the idea of a separate evil origin is false.
This is a concise refutation of dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا شيء من تلك الجواهر المؤذية إلا وهو ينفع خلقا، فلم تكن مؤذية، ولكن بمدبر حكيم عليم جعلها بحيث تؤذى أحدا وتنفع آخر. ثبت أن القول بانفراد منشئ الشر بعيد.
Then nothing of those harmful substances is except that it benefits some creation, so it is not harmful, but rather with a wise, knowing manager who made it such that it harms one and benefits another. It is established that the statement of singling out the originator of evil is far [from truth].
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a direct argument against the dualist claim that evil has a separate origin. Maturidi points out that all harmful things also provide benefit to some creatures. For example, a poisonous substance might harm one creature but be medicine for another. Therefore, these substances are not purely evil; they are created by a single Wise and Knowing Manager (مدبر حكيم عليم) who assigns them different effects. This disproves the idea of a separate originator of evil (منشئ الشر).

Key Terms
جواهر مؤذية
مدبر حكيم عليم
منشئ الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that harmful substances are purely evil and must originate from a separate evil source.
Correction: Maturidi shows that all harmful things also benefit, so they are not purely evil and do not require a separate origin.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about harmful substances?
A) They are purely evil
B) They are beneficial to some
C) They have no creator
D) They are from a separate evil source

Key Takeaway
Harmful things also benefit, so they point to one Wise Creator, not two origins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a painful situation or a harmful event, do you instinctively label it as purely evil, or do you pause to consider how it might benefit you or someone else? How can you train your heart to look for the hidden wisdom in trials, as this lesson teaches?
?
Think of a person who has caused you harm. Can you see how that harm might have been a means of benefit for others, even if you cannot see it for yourself? How does this perspective shift your willingness to forgive and see the situation through the eyes of the Wise Manager?
?
If you truly believe that every harmful thing also benefits some creation, what specific difficulty are you currently facing that you need to re-evaluate? What would it mean for you this week to actively seek the benefit in that difficulty, and what step can you take to act on that belief?

The Wisdom of Creating Diverse Substances for Human Testing
Maturidi explains the divine wisdom in creating diverse substances, including evil, as a test for humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God created both good and evil substances according to Maturidi, and how this serves as a test for humans to exercise gratitude and discernment.

Core Idea
God creates diversity, including evil, as a test for human wisdom and gratitude.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's *Kitab al-Tawhid* addresses a profound theological question: Why does God create evil? Maturidi argues that the very diversity of substances—both good and evil—is a manifestation of divine wisdom. By creating a world with moral opposites, God provides a context for humans to be tested, to learn gratitude, and to develop moral discernment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument step by step.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claims
Maturidi states that God's action is not describable by human standards of good and bad. Wisdom means placing everything in its proper place. Identify these key points.
3
Understand the purpose of creation
Maturidi explains that God created not for His own needs, but so that humans—endowed with intellect—may be tested. How does the diversity of substances serve this test?
Consider the role of enmity and friendship.
4
Reflect on human response
Humans learn caution, fear, and initiative by witnessing different substances and conditions. How does this lead to praiseworthy outcomes?
Think about theodicy and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا وجه الحكمة في خلق الجواهر المختلفة، وأن فعل الله لا يوصف بذلك، وأن إنشاءها على ما عليه من قبح القبيح وحسن الحسن هو معنى الحكمة ووضع كل شيء موضعه، وأن الله تعالى إذ لم يخلق لحاجات نفسه وإنما خلق بذاته. إنه خالق ليكون الخلق الذي ركب فيهم العقول وجعلهم أهل المعرفة بالنعم والبلايا يمتحنون بوضع كل شيء موضعه والقيام بالشكر لما أنعم عليهم؛ بأن جعل لهم جميع الخلائق على اختلاف جواهرهم أدلة وعبرا ومحنة وابتلاء بمعاداة جواهر وموالاة أخرى؛ وليعرفوا كيفية الاتقاء ووجه الحذر وما فيه الرعب ووجوه المبادرة في ذلك للعواقب المحمودة في العقول واتقاء الآخرين لمكروهة فيها؛ بما عاينوا من مختلف الجواهر والأحوال في حق الترغيب
And we have clarified the aspect of wisdom in the creation of the different substances, and that the act of God is not described by that, and that their creation [i.e., of substances] upon what they are of the ugliness of the ugly and the beauty of the beautiful is the meaning of wisdom and putting everything in its place, and that God Almighty did not create for His own needs but rather He created by His essence. He is a Creator so that the creation in whom He placed intellects and made them possessors of knowledge of blessings and trials may be tested by putting everything in its place and performing gratitude for what He has bestowed upon them; by making for them all creatures, despite the difference of their substances, as proofs, lessons, trial, and test, by enmity towards some substances and friendship towards others; and so that they may know the manner of protection and the aspect of caution and what is in it of fear and the aspects of initiative in that for the praiseworthy outcomes in the intellects and the avoidance of others for what is disliked in them; by what they witness of different substances and conditions in the aspect of encouragement.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage explains the divine wisdom behind creating both good and evil substances. Maturidi states that God's actions are not describable in human terms of good and bad; rather, the creation of things with their moral qualities (ugliness and beauty) is precisely what wisdom means: placing everything in its proper place. God did not create for His own needs but by His essence. He created so that humans, who are endowed with intellect and knowledge of blessings and trials, may be tested by putting everything in its proper place and showing gratitude. God made all creatures, with their different substances, as proofs, lessons, and tests, so that humans experience enmity and friendship toward different things, learn caution and fear, and take initiative for praiseworthy outcomes while avoiding what is disliked, based on their observation of the diverse substances and conditions.

Correction: Maturidi shows that God's creation of both good and evil substances serves a wise purpose: to test humans and guide them to gratitude and moral discernment.

Why did God create both good and evil substances according to Maturidi?
A) For His own need
B) To test humans and provide lessons
C) Because He could not avoid it
D) To make some people suffer

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom in creating both good and evil substances is to test humans, teach them gratitude, and guide them through experiences of enmity and friendship.

How am I supposed to put this in its proper place and show gratitude?' Will you commit to asking that question every time difficulty arises, starting today?
?

Name one relationship you will re-frame starting now.
?

Are you truly using your intellect to discern the wisdom behind the diverse substances in your life, or are you sleepwalking through your days, reacting without reflection? What is one specific way you will intentionally 'put everything in its place' today as an act of gratitude?

The Wisdom of Divine Commands
Maturidi argues that denying commands negates wisdom and that the world's proofs indicate a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying divine commands and promises leads to a lack of wisdom, and how the wisdom of the Creator proves His wise nature.

Core Idea
Denying divine commands denies wisdom in creation itself.

Maturidi begins by addressing those who reject the necessity of God's commands and prohibitions. He argues that such a rejection leads to a failure to see wisdom in God's act of creating and annihilating. The very existence of order and proofs in the world points to a wise Maker. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with a conditional statement.
The passage is concise; each sentence builds the argument.
2
Identify the consequence of denying commands
What does Maturidi say happens to someone who denies commands and promises? He says they do not obtain wisdom in creation.
The denial leads to a lack of wisdom, not just a practical loss.
3
Understand how wisdom in creation indicates God's wisdom
The wisdom of the Maker is evident in the proofs of oneness and power in the world. This shows that God is wise, contrary to the denier's view.
This is a theological argument from design.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من أنكر الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد لم يحصل [عنده] للإنشاء حكمة، وإنما حصل منه على الإنشاء ثم الإفناء. ثم معلوم أن كل من ذلك عاقبة فعله ليس بحكيم. فدلت حكمة صانع العالم - بما جعل فيه من الأدلة على وحدانيته وعظيم سلطانه ـ على أنه حكيم. والله الموفق. مع ما كان الله سبحانه - إذ هو غني بذاته حكيم في فعله ـ خلق الخلق للبقاء إلى [ نهاية ] قدرة جعلها لهم ؛ ثم لم يجعل البقاء إلا بالأغذية، وقد حبّب إليهم البقاء ودوام الحياة. فلو لم يجعل عليهم الأمر والنهي لبادر كل إلى ما يطمع فيه البقاء ودوام الحياة، مع ما له من اللذة والشهوة؛ ثم يفعل أقرانه بذلك الشيء نحو فعله، فيحدث بينهم التنازع والتجاذب، ويحملهم ذلك على التدافع؛ وفي ذلك خوف الفناء بما به جعل البقاء. فلزم جعل الحرمات والحل والأمر والنهي بما فيه من الوعد والوعيد ؛ ليعلم كل ما له مما ليس له؛ فيسلم [من] كل عداوة وتبقى له روحه.
Then whoever denies the command and prohibition and promise and threat does not obtain wisdom in creation; rather he obtains from it creation then annihilation. Then it is known that everyone who does that is not wise as a consequence of his action. So the wisdom of the Maker of the world—by what He placed in it of proofs of His oneness and mighty dominion—indicates that He is wise. And Allah is the giver of success. Along with that, Allah—since He is self-sufficient in essence, wise in His action—created creatures for remaining until a limit of power He made for them; then He did not make remaining except through nutrition, and He made them love remaining and continuance of life. So had He not imposed upon them command and prohibition, each would hasten to what he covets for remaining and continuance of life, along with the pleasure and desire he has; then his peers would do the same thing as his action, and conflict and contention would arise among them, and that would drive them to push each other; and in that is fear of annihilation by that through which remaining is made. So it became necessary to establish sanctities and permissibility and command and prohibition containing promise and threat, so that each may know what is his and what is not his, and thus he is safe from all enmity and his soul remains.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine commands and prohibitions are necessary for human survival. Maturidi begins by stating that anyone who denies commands and prohibitions, as well as promise and threat, fails to see wisdom in God's act of creation and annihilation. Such a person is not wise in his own reasoning. The wisdom of the Creator, evident in the proofs of His oneness and power, shows that He is wise. Maturidi then presents a practical argument: God created humans for a limited lifespan and made their survival dependent on food, while instilling in them a love for life. If there were no commands and prohibitions, everyone would aggressively pursue whatever ensures their survival, driven by pleasure and desire. This would lead to conflict and mutual destruction, endangering the very life they seek to preserve. Therefore, it was necessary to establish sanctities (ḥurumāt), permissible things (ḥalāl), commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats, so that each person knows his rights and duties, thereby avoiding enmity and preserving his life.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
الوعد والوعيد
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine commands are arbitrary and unnecessary.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denying commands leads to a lack of wisdom, and the world's order proves God's wisdom, making commands inherently wise.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to someone who denies commands and promises?
A) They become wise
B) They do not obtain wisdom in creation
C) They are excused
D) They are rewarded

Key Takeaway
Denying divine commands negates wisdom, but the universe's order proves God's wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see God's commands as arbitrary restrictions or as expressions of His perfect wisdom? How does your daily obedience reflect your answer?
?
When you look at the world, do you truly see proofs of a wise Creator, or have you become numb to the signs? What will you do today to actively seek that wisdom?
?
If you deny the wisdom behind even one of God's commands, what does that imply about your view of the entire creation? Will you commit to trusting His wisdom even when you don't understand?

Commands as a Necessity for Survival
Maturidi argues that divine commands are necessary to prevent conflict and preserve life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical necessity of divine commands and prohibitions for preserving human life and preventing societal conflict.

Core Idea
Without divine commands, human survival would be impossible due to conflict.

Maturidi now presents a pragmatic argument: God created humans with a love for life and made survival dependent on food. Without a framework of commands and prohibitions, people would fight ruthlessly for resources, leading to mutual destruction. Hence, divine laws are essential for preserving life and peace.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice it starts with God's attributes and then describes human nature.
2
Understand the natural love for life and need for food
God created humans for a limited period, made survival dependent on food, and instilled a love for life.
This sets the stage for the problem.
3
See the consequence of absent commands: conflict
Without commands, each person would pursue survival aggressively, leading to conflict and mutual destruction.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Recognize the solution: divine legislation
Therefore, it was necessary to establish sanctities, permissible things, commands, and prohibitions to ensure peace and survival.
The conclusion shows the necessity of divine law.

Why God's Commands Are Not for His Benefit
Maturidi refutes the objection that God's self-sufficiency negates trial and commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that God's self-sufficiency makes trial and commands purposeless.

Core Idea
God's commands are not for personal gain; He is wise and self-sufficient.

This lesson presents a common objection against divine commands and trials: that they are meaningless for a self-sufficient God who already knows all. Maturidi's answer is sharp and foundational for Islamic theology. Prepare to see how he defends the wisdom of divine legislation without attributing neediness to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the opening lines describing why someone might deny trial and commands. Notice the reasoning based on human analogies.
The objector uses 'shahid' (witnessed world) as a starting point.
2
Identify Maturidi's response
After the objector's claim, the faqih responds. Find the key statement that contrasts God's actions with those of needy creatures.
Look for the phrase 'He does not act for benefit nor for repulsion'.
3
Understand the comparison
Maturidi explains how needy creatures act for gain or harm. Compare that with God's attributes of wisdom and self-sufficiency.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends by stating the objector's claim is error. Notice the final point about evil contradicting divine wisdom.
The conclusion reinforces that God's actions are perfect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من أنكر الأمر والنهي والمحنة ذهب إلى معنى المحنة في الشاهد؛ إنما هو لظهور ما خفى وتجلّي ما استتر، والأمر والنهي لمنفعة ينالها الآمر والناهي أو مكروه يدفعه ، فإذ كان الله غنيا بذاته عليمًا بالسرائر والخفيات ذهب معنى المحنة والأمر والنهي. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : ،نقول وبالله التوفيق: إن أمره ونهيه ومحنته على ما يذكر، فإن كان] فعله لذلك لكان لمكروه يدفع أو محبوب يجلب أو عيب عنه يتخلى. والله سبحانه أنشأ العالم لا للذي يذكر، فمثله الأمر والنهي والمحنة. مع ما كان / ذلك التقدير إنما هو فعل المحتاجين مما تعلو درجاتهم وتجلّ أقدارهم، ولو فعلوا غير ذلك كان عليهم في فعل ذلك ضرر عاجل وشر آجل. فأما من هو حكيم بذاته، غني، فهو لا يفعل لنفع ولا لدفع ، فمثله الأمر والنهي . مع ما بينا من اختلاف الممتحنين في الغنى والحكمة لم يجز تقدير أحدهما بالآخر. ولا يحتمل الحكيم [فعل] الشر [الذي يخالف] حكمة الربوبية، فتكلّفه الذي ذكر خطأ.
And whoever denies the command, prohibition, and trial goes to the meaning of trial in the witnessed world: it is only for the manifestation of what is hidden and the disclosure of what is concealed, and command and prohibition are for a benefit the commander and prohibiter obtain or a disliked thing they repel. So since Allah is self-sufficient in essence and knows the innermost secrets and hidden things, the meaning of trial and command and prohibition disappears. The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah is success: His command, prohibition, and trial are as mentioned. For if He did that, it would be for a disliked thing to repel or a beloved thing to attract or a defect to abandon. But Allah, exalted, created the world not for what is mentioned; so likewise are command, prohibition, and trial. Along with that, such estimation is only the act of the needy whose degrees rise and whose ranks become exalted, and if they did otherwise, there would be in that action immediate harm and delayed evil. As for He who is wise in His essence, self-sufficient, He does not act for benefit nor for repulsion; so likewise are command and prohibition. Along with what we have explained of the difference between the one tested and the tester in wealth and wisdom, it is not permissible to judge one by the other. Nor does the wise one do evil that contradicts the wisdom of lordship, so his claiming what was mentioned is error.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the denial of divine commands, prohibitions, and trials. The objector argues that in human experience, trial (miḥna) is meant to reveal hidden matters, and commands are for the benefit or harm of the commander. Since God knows all hidden things and is self-sufficient, these purposes are inapplicable to Him. The answer (introduced by 'al-faqīh raḥimahu Allāh') states that God's command, prohibition, and trial are not for His own benefit or harm; He does not act for gain or repulsion. Unlike needy creatures who act to elevate their status or avoid harm, God is wise and self-sufficient. Therefore, comparing God to humans is invalid. Moreover, a wise being does not do evil that contradicts divine wisdom, so the objector's claim is erroneous.

Key Terms
المحنة
الأمر والنهي
الغنى بالذات
الحكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's commands and trials must serve a purpose similar to human commands (e.g., to learn something or gain benefit).
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God is self-sufficient and wise, so His commands are not for personal benefit or harm, but for the wisdom of His creation.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the claim that God's self-sufficiency negates the purpose of trial and commands?
A) He agrees and says commands are unnecessary
B) He says God acts for His own benefit
C) He argues that God's actions are not for benefit or harm, unlike humans
D) He says trial is only for humans

Key Takeaway
God's commands and trials are not for His own benefit but are expressions of His wisdom, unlike human actions driven by need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God, do you ever feel that you are somehow benefiting God or that He needs your obedience? How does this lesson's teaching about God's self-sufficiency challenge such a mindset? Reflect on a recent act of worship and ask yourself: was it driven by love and reverence, or by a subtle sense that God requires your service? Let this truth reshape your intention so that you worship Him as the Self-Sufficient, not as one in need.
?
If you truly believe that God's commands are not for His own benefit but are rooted in wisdom, how should that change the way you respond to difficult trials? Are you willing to accept that even when you do not see the benefit, God's wisdom is sufficient? Examine your heart: when hardship strikes, do you grumble as if God is being unfair, or do you submit to His wisdom, trusting that His command is good simply because it comes from Him? Resolve today to embrace trials with trust in divine wisdom.
?
Consider the difference between how you act (often for your own benefit or to avoid harm) and how God acts (not for benefit). In what areas of your life are you still operating like God needs your achievements or that you can add to Him? How can you shift your perspective to see your own actions as responses to His worth, not as contributions to His glory? Let this lesson move you to a place of pure servitude, where you obey simply because He is worthy, not because you think He needs it.

Necessity of Warnings and Promises in Creation
Maturidi argues that since creation includes harm and benefit, warnings and promises are necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues from the existence of harm/benefit and pain/pleasure in creation to the necessity of divine warnings and promises.

Core Idea
Pain and pleasure in creation imply divine threats and rewards.

This passage presents Maturidi's concise proof that the very structure of creation — containing both harmful and beneficial things, and beings capable of pain and pleasure — logically requires that God provide warnings and promises. Without them, human motivation would be incomplete. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; every word is deliberate.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage begins with two premises: (1) creation is divided into harmful and beneficial, (2) every substance is capable of pain and pleasure. Understand why these are foundational.
These premises are presented as given facts about the world.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, Maturidi deduces that God must have created consequences (warnings and promises) to direct behaviour. This is not arbitrary but logically necessary.
The conclusion is that desire and fear are perfected through these consequences.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument matters: it ties divine attributes (justice, wisdom) to the observable world. It also shows that human motivation requires divine guidance.
This connects to broader topics in kalam about God's actions and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه إذ جعل الخلق قسمين ضارًا ونافعا ، وجعل كل جوهر محتملاً للألم واللذة، لم يحتمل أن يجعلهم كذلك إلا لعواقب، يُحذرهم بها ويُرغبهم فيها من الوعيد بالشدائد والوعد بالملاذ، وبذلك تتم الرغبة والرهبة. والله الموفق.
And moreover, since He made creation two kinds: harmful and beneficial, and made every substance capable of pain and pleasure, it is not plausible that He made them thus except for consequences, by which He warns them and makes them desire, of threat with hardships and promise with delights, and by that desire and fear are perfected. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof for the necessity of divine warning and promise. Maturidi argues that since God created the world comprising both harmful and beneficial things, and every substance is susceptible to pain and pleasure, it is inconceivable that He would create them in this way without corresponding consequences. Those consequences are the warnings (waʿīd) of hardships and promises (waʿd) of delights, which serve to perfect human desire (raghba) for good and fear (rahba) of evil. Thus, the very structure of creation implies the need for divine threats and promises.

Key Terms
الوعيد
الوعد
الرغبة
الرهبة
الألم واللذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pain and pleasure are random and lack any divine purpose.
Correction: Maturidi shows that pain and pleasure are not random; they are part of a designed system where warnings and promises guide human behavior toward perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why are warnings and promises necessary?
A) Because God likes to threaten
B) Because creation includes pain and pleasure, requiring consequences to motivate
C) Because humans are naturally good
D) Because God changes His mind

Key Takeaway
The presence of pain and pleasure in creation logically necessitates divine warnings and promises to perfect human desire and fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pleasure, do you recognize it as a foretaste of the delights promised by Allah, or do you consume it as if there is no final reckoning?
?
When you face hardship, do you see it as a warning from Allah calling you to return to Him, or do you dismiss it as meaningless suffering?
?
How does the fact that your very capacity for pain and pleasure is not random but purposeful change how you respond to the joys and sorrows of your daily life? Will you begin to see each moment of ease or difficulty as a signpost pointing to the Hereafter?

God's Commands Align with Created Benefits and Harms
Maturidi argues that God's commands align with created benefits and harms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi demonstrates that God's commands and prohibitions are consistent with the beneficial and harmful natures He created, and that this coherence reflects His self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's commands mirror the beneficial and harmful order He created, showing His wisdom and self-sufficiency.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid builds on the idea that creation is structured with mutual benefits and harms. Since God is self-sufficient, He gains nothing from this arrangement, yet His commands direct creatures to benefit one another and avoid harm, aligning with the very nature He instilled in creation. Understanding this coherence helps us see the wisdom behind divine law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi connects creation (خلق) with command (أمر) and prohibition (نهي).
Pay attention to the word 'فمثله' which links the two ideas.
2
Identify key elements
Identify the terms 'منافع' (benefits), 'مضار' (harms), and 'الأمر والنهي' (command and prohibition). Observe how Maturidi relates them to God's self-sufficiency (الغنى).
3
Reflect on divine self-sufficiency
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes that God has no need for these benefits. This underscores that His commands are for our benefit, not His.
This refutes anthropomorphic views that God commands for His own gain.
4
Apply the insight
Think about how this argument shows the harmony between God's creative act and His legislative act. How does this strengthen the case for divine wisdom?
This coherence is a sign of God's perfect design.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإذ خلق الخلق، وجعل البعض منافع ببعض ، وإن لم يكن له في ذلك نفع لغناه، وكذلك المضار، فمثله يأمر وينهى بمنافع بعض ببعض واتقاء المضار؛ مع ما يأمر بما ينفعهم، كما خلقهم وجعل لهم ذلك، وينهى عما يضرهم. والله الموفق.
And moreover, since He created creation and made some of them benefits for others, even though there is no benefit for Him in that due to His self-sufficiency, and likewise harms, so likewise He commands and forbids regarding benefits of some for others and avoidance of harms; along with that He commands what benefits them, just as He created them and made that for them, and forbids what harms them. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage extends the previous argument by noting that God created some beings as means of benefit for others, and similarly some as causes of harm. Since God is self-sufficient (ghanī), He gains no benefit from this arrangement. Nevertheless, He commands and prohibits in a manner that directs creatures to benefit one another and avoid harm. His commands are thus consistent with His creative act: He both creates beneficial things and commands their use, and He creates harmful things and forbids them. This shows the coherence between divine creation and divine law.

Key Terms
منافع
مضار
الأمر والنهي
الغنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's commands are unrelated to His creation or that He commands arbitrarily.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's commands align with the beneficial and harmful natures He created, demonstrating coherence between creation and legislation.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say God commands and forbids regarding benefits and harms?
A) To gain benefit for Himself
B) To align with the created order of mutual benefit and harm
C) To test humans arbitrarily
D) To increase confusion

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that God's commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary but align with the beneficial and harmful nature He created, reflecting His wisdom and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God commands what benefits you and forbids what harms you, even though He is completely self-sufficient. In what area of your life do you still resist a command because you think you know better than the Creator? What would it take to fully surrender that to Him?
?
Reflect on a specific prohibition (e.g., lying, backbiting, wasting time). Can you see how it aligns with the harm it creates in your life and others? How does seeing God's command as rooted in that created reality change your motivation to obey?
?
If God's commands are not arbitrary but perfectly matched to the nature He established, what does that mean for how you approach His law? Are you treating it as a loving guide or a burdensome restriction? What needs to shift in your heart?

Gratitude for Blessings Necessitates Commands
Maturidi argues that God's abundant blessings require humans to know and thank the Giver, necessitating commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that God's unmerited blessings logically require humans to know and thank Him, thereby necessitating divine commands.

Core Idea
Unearned blessings demand recognition—God's commands are the rational expression of gratitude.

This passage presents Maturidi's reasoning that divine commands are not arbitrary but rooted in the very nature of creation and blessings. He begins with the fact that God created humans in the best form and provided countless blessings without any prior merit, and argues that reason itself demands that such gifts be acknowledged. Therefore, knowledge of the Giver and gratitude become obligatory, which in turn requires commands, prohibitions, and the framework of reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact but logical structure of the argument.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace Maturidi's argument: blessings (without merit) → reason demands recognition → knowledge of Benefactor becomes incumbent → leads to trial and commands/promises/threats.
This chain is the core of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Maturidi sees commands as necessary. How does this connect to the concept of wisdom (ḥikma) in divine actions?
This step cements the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن في الحكمة الأمر والنهي؛ لأن الله خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم، وسخر لهم جميع ما على وجه الأرض وبركاتها وبركات السماء، من غير أن سبق منهم ما خرج [به] ذلك مخرج المكافأة أو مخرج حق قضاه . فلا يجوز في العقل إسداء مثل هذه النعم إلى من لا يعرفها ، لما فيه تضييع وظلم النعم، فلزمهم به معرفة المنعم ليعلموا من يستحق المحبة ويستوجب الشكر، وفي ذلك لزوم المحنة ؛ ووَصل بذلك الوعد والوعيد لتتم الرغبة والرهبة. وبالله التوفيق.
And also, in wisdom are command and prohibition, because Allah created humans in the best stature, and subjected to them all that is on the face of the earth and its blessings and the blessings of the sky, without any prior deed from them that would make that come out as compensation or as a right He decreed. So it is not permissible in reason to bestow such blessings upon one who does not know them, because that would involve wasting and oppressing the blessings. Therefore, knowledge of the Benefactor became incumbent upon them so that they may know who deserves love and merits gratitude. And in that is the necessity of trial; and He connected with that promise and threat to perfect desire and fear. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a proof that commands and prohibitions are inherently wise. Maturidi argues that God created humans in the best form (aḥsan taqwīm) and subjugated to them all earthly and heavenly blessings without any prior merit or compensation from them. Reason dictates that such unearned blessings cannot be given to someone who does not recognize them, for that would be wasteful and unjust. Therefore, it becomes obligatory for humans to know the Benefactor (al-munʿim) so that they may love Him and thank Him. This knowledge requires trial (miḥna) to distinguish the grateful from the ungrateful, and God attached promises and threats to complete the motivation of desire and fear. Thus, commands and prohibitions are necessary for the proper reception of blessings.

Key Terms
أحسن تقويم
النعم
المعرفة
الشكر
المحنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's blessings require no response from humans, or that commands are arbitrary.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason itself demands gratitude and knowledge of the Giver; otherwise, blessings would be wasted and oppressed. Commands are the necessary framework for this gratitude and trial.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that knowledge of the Benefactor is necessary?
A) Because God needs recognition
B) To avoid wasting blessings by giving them to the ungrateful
C) Because humans deserve blessings
D) To increase worldly wealth

Key Takeaway
God's unearned blessings make knowledge of Him and gratitude a rational duty, which necessarily requires commands, prohibitions, and the framework of reward and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What unearned blessing in your life have you treated as a right, and how does that reveal your ingratitude toward the Giver?
?
This passage says it is not permissible in reason to bestow blessings on one who does not know the Giver. How have you been receiving God's blessings while living as if He does not exist?
?
If gratitude to God necessarily requires commands and prohibitions, what command of God are you currently resisting because it demands you to acknowledge Him?

The logical problem of a 'part' becoming a son
Maturidi questions how one part became a son and shows the absurd consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if a part of God became a son, then every smaller part of the universe would also be a son, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
If one part can be a son, then all parts can be sons — absurd.

Maturidi begins his refutation by questioning the very premise that a 'part' of God could become a son. He shows that if we accept that, we must also accept that every smaller part of the universe is a son of the larger, which leads to an infinite regress of sons. This lesson examines that initial logical move.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by a hypothetical answer and its consequence.
The passage is a single logical reductio.
2
Identify key terms
Identify الروح القديمة (eternal spirit) and جزء (part). Understand that Christians claim Christ has a part of God dwelling in him.
3
Trace the logical consequence
If the Christian responds that this part became a son because it is smallest, then every smaller part of the world would also be a son. Think about why this is absurd.
This shows the doctrine leads to an infinite number of sons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم: إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة وهي بعض، كيف صار ابنا ولم تصل غيره من الأبعاض؟ فإن قيل: لأنه أقل، لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم بنين للأكبر منها، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك، فيصير بكليته بنين. ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر من الأب، كيف صارا قديمين؟ وإن جعل الكل في البدن، قيل له: أي شيء منه الابن؟ فإن قال: الكل صيّر الكل ابنا وأبًا، وفي ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه. فإن قيل: هو جزء فيه، من غير أن كان عن كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يُؤخذ من السراج، فيبطل قوله في قدم الروح، وهو الابن. وإن زعم أنه منقول من الله كالمأخوذ [من السراج] حل عليه ما سلف. وبعد، فما يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقصه؟ فإن قيل: معاينتنا إياه كذلك، قيل: لعل الله أحدثه، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج، وأيهما كان فهو حادث، والحادث مخلوق، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا؟ قال: من أن الله أظهر منه عجائب. قيل: وقد أظهر من موسى، فقولوا: هو ابن آخر. فإن زعمتم أن ذلك كان بدعاء وتضرع فمثله أمر غيره.
So it is said to them: Since the spirit that is in Him is eternal and is a part, how did He become a son while no other parts reached Him? If it is said: Because it is the smallest, then it would be necessary to make every part of the world a son to the largest of them, and it would be necessary to make every part of the rest likewise, so the whole would become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father, so how are both eternal? And if He makes the whole in the body, it is said to him: Which thing of Him is the son? If he says: The whole, then he makes the whole both son and father, and in that he makes the father a son of himself. If it is said: It is a part in Him without the whole original having deficiency, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected that if the part taken is originated as occurs in what is taken from a lamp, then his statement about the eternity of the spirit is invalidated, and it is the son. And if he claims that it is transferred from God like what is taken [from a lamp], then what preceded applies to him. Moreover, what tells him that what is taken from the lamp does not diminish it? If it is said: Our observation of it is so, it is said: Perhaps God originated it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is originated, and the originated is created, so why is it permissible for it to be a son? He said: Because God manifested wonders through him. It is said: And He manifested from Moses, so say: He is another son. And if you claim that that was through supplication and humility, then similar is the matter of others.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of Christian doctrines regarding Christ being the Son of God through a part of God dwelling in him. Maturidi uses logical arguments to expose inconsistencies: (1) If only one part became a son, why not other parts? If because it is smaller, then all smaller parts of the universe would be sons of larger ones. (2) If the son is eternal, how can he be younger than the eternal Father? (3) If the whole divine presence is in the body, then the whole is both father and son, making the father son of himself. (4) If it is like a part taken from a lamp without diminishing the source, then that part is originated (as the flame is newly lit), contradicting its eternity. (5) If it is like taking a flame from a lamp, observation suggests no diminishment, but that could be God's creation or like fire from stone. In any case, the part is created, not eternal, so cannot be divine son. Finally, if they claim Jesus performed wonders, so did Moses, so why not call him son too? If they say Moses did it through supplication, then others also can.

Key Terms
الروح القديمة
جزء
ابن
أب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue would happen if Christians claim Christ became a son because the part in him is the smallest?
A) Then the whole would become sons
B) Then no part would be a son
C) Then only the largest part would be a son
D) Then the spirit would not be eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that claiming one part of God became a son leads to the absurdity that every part of the universe becomes a son.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when pushed to its logical conclusion, becomes absurd? How does Maturidi's method challenge you to examine the consistency of your own religious convictions?
?
If you were to claim that something or someone has a special divine status because it contains a 'part' of God, how would you answer Maturidi's question: why that part and not another? What does this teach you about the danger of arbitrary reasoning in matters of faith?
?
Consider a time you accepted a religious teaching without thinking through its implications. What would it look like for you to apply Maturidi's rigorous questioning to that teaching today? Will you commit to doing so?

beginner
5 min
The contradiction of an eternal son and younger father
Maturidi points out the age contradiction and the problem of the whole being in Christ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if the son is eternal, he cannot be younger than the father, and if the whole divine presence is in Christ, then the father becomes son of himself.

Core Idea
An eternal son cannot be younger than an eternal father; if the whole is in Christ, God becomes his own son.

Maturidi continues by pointing out two more logical inconsistencies. First, the natural relationship of father-son implies the son is younger, but here both are claimed eternal. Second, if the entirety of God's presence is in the body of Christ, then the distinction between father and son collapses entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on age contradiction
Read the Arabic and translation for the first part about the son being younger than the father.
2
Read the passage on the whole in the body
Read the second part about the whole being in the body and the question 'which thing of Him is the son?'
3
Consider the implications
Reflect on why an eternal son cannot be younger, and how claiming the whole is both father and son leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

The lamp analogy: why the part cannot be eternal
Maturidi uses the lamp analogy to argue the part in Christ is originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of a lamp's flame to argue that the supposed part of God in Christ must be originated (created), not eternal.

Core Idea
If the part of God in Christ is like a flame taken from a lamp, it is originated, not eternal.

Christians sometimes argue that the part of God in Christ is like a flame taken from a lamp — it does not diminish the source. Maturidi counters that the taken flame is originated (newly lit), so it cannot be eternal. This lesson explores that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic and translation for the lamp analogy. Identify the comparison being made.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage considers two interpretations: the part is like a taken flame (originated) or like fire in stone (latent).
3
Analyze the conclusion
In both cases, the part is originated/created, so it cannot be eternal or divine. Understand why this refutes the Christian claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال لهم: إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة وهي بعض، كيف صار ابنا ولم تصل غيره من الأبعاض؟ فإن قيل: لأنه أقل، لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم بنين للأكبر منها، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك، فيصير بكليته بنين. ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر من الأب، كيف صارا قديمين؟ وإن جعل الكل في البدن، قيل له: أي شيء منه الابن؟ فإن قال: الكل صيّر الكل ابنا وأبًا، وفي ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه. فإن قيل: هو جزء فيه، من غير أن كان عن كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يُؤخذ من السراج، فيبطل قوله في قدم الروح، وهو الابن. وإن زعم أنه منقول من الله كالمأخوذ [من السراج] حل عليه ما سلف. وبعد، فما يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقصه؟ فإن قيل: معاينتنا إياه كذلك، قيل: لعل الله أحدثه، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج، وأيهما كان فهو حادث، والحادث مخلوق، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا؟ قال: من أن الله أظهر منه عجائب. قيل: وقد أظهر من موسى، فقولوا: هو ابن آخر. فإن زعمتم أن ذلك كان بدعاء وتضرع فمثله أمر غيره.
So it is said to them: Since the spirit that is in Him is eternal and is a part, how did He become a son while no other parts reached Him? If it is said: Because it is the smallest, then it would be necessary to make every part of the world a son to the largest of them, and it would be necessary to make every part of the rest likewise, so the whole would become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father, so how are both eternal? And if He makes the whole in the body, it is said to him: Which thing of Him is the son? If he says: The whole, then he makes the whole both son and father, and in that he makes the father a son of himself. If it is said: It is a part in Him without the whole original having deficiency, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected that if the part taken is originated as occurs in what is taken from a lamp, then his statement about the eternity of the spirit is invalidated, and it is the son. And if he claims that it is transferred from God like what is taken [from a lamp], then what preceded applies to him. Moreover, what tells him that what is taken from the lamp does not diminish it? If it is said: Our observation of it is so, it is said: Perhaps God originated it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is originated, and the originated is created, so why is it permissible for it to be a son? He said: Because God manifested wonders through him. It is said: And He manifested from Moses, so say: He is another son. And if you claim that that was through supplication and humility, then similar is the matter of others.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of Christian doctrines regarding Christ being the Son of God through a part of God dwelling in him. Maturidi uses logical arguments to expose inconsistencies: (1) If only one part became a son, why not other parts? If because it is smaller, then all smaller parts of the universe would be sons of larger ones. (2) If the son is eternal, how can he be younger than the eternal Father? (3) If the whole divine presence is in the body, then the whole is both father and son, making the father son of himself. (4) If it is like a part taken from a lamp without diminishing the source, then that part is originated (as the flame is newly lit), contradicting its eternity. (5) If it is like taking a flame from a lamp, observation suggests no diminishment, but that could be God's creation or like fire from stone. In any case, the part is created, not eternal, so cannot be divine son. Finally, if they claim Jesus performed wonders, so did Moses, so why not call him son too? If they say Moses did it through supplication, then others also can.

Key Terms
جزء
سراج
حادث
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the problem with comparing the part of God in Christ to a flame taken from a lamp?
A) The flame is eternal
B) The flame is originated
C) The flame diminishes the lamp
D) The flame is part of the lamp

Key Takeaway
The lamp analogy backfires: if the part is like a taken flame, it is originated and created, thus not eternal or divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe something that seems to have no beginning, like the flame of a lamp, do you automatically assume it is eternal? In what areas of your life do you rely on observation rather than revelation to judge what is eternal?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God that, upon closer inspection, turned out to be based on a flawed analogy? How willing are you to abandon such beliefs when faced with clear reasoning?
?
If you were to argue with a Christian about the divinity of Christ using the lamp analogy, would you be able to present Maturidi's objection as precisely as it is here? What does your current understanding of this argument reveal about your own preparation to defend tawhid?

Miracles do not prove sonship: the example of Moses
Maturidi refutes the argument from miracles by citing Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Jesus' miracles do not prove he is the Son of God, since other prophets like Moses also performed wonders.

Core Idea
If miracles prove sonship, then Moses would also be a son of God.

Christians often cite Jesus' miracles as evidence of his divine sonship. Maturidi counters by pointing out that Moses also performed mighty wonders through God's power. If that makes someone a son, then Moses would have to be considered a son as well. This lesson examines that final argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about wonders
Read the Arabic and translation for the final argument about wonders.
2
Compare to Moses
Notice that Maturidi directly compares Jesus' wonders to those of Moses.
3
Consider the rebuttal about supplication
If Christians say Moses performed wonders through supplication, Maturidi says others can do the same, so the argument does not uniquely support Jesus.
