Refuting the Claim of Divine Sonship Through Logical Implications
Maturidi presents three logical objections to the Christian claim that a part of God is Jesus's son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the inconsistency of the claim that a part of God is a son to demonstrate its impossibility.

Core Idea
If a part of God is a son, then everything would be a son.

The Christian claim that Jesus is the son of God because a divine part entered him raises immediate logical questions. Maturidi begins by asking: if the soul in Jesus is an eternal part of God, why is it uniquely called a son? This lesson explores his first series of objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian claim and the naming aside
Read the opening lines where the claim is stated and Ibn Shabib's comment about the term 'son'. Note that the naming aside is not part of the main argument but a linguistic observation.
2
Identify the first objection about parts
Maturidi asks: if the soul is an eternal part and is called the son, why aren't other parts also sons? Consider why this is a problem.
This points to an inconsistency in the Christian position.
3
Analyze the second objection about relative size
If the Christian responds that the soul is a smaller part, Maturidi argues that then every smaller part of the universe would be a son to a larger part, leading to absurdity.
This shows the claim leads to infinite regress of sonships.
4
Understand the third objection about age
Maturidi notes that a son is normally younger than the father, but here both are eternal, which is contradictory.
This undermines the very concept of sonship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيهم من يقول : يصل إليه جزء من الله تعالى، ويفصل جزء آخر . قال ابن شبيب : سمعت من مولديهم أنه كان ابن التبنى لا ابن الولاد، كما سميت أزواج محمد عليه السلام أمهات، وكما يقول الرجل لآخر : يا بني. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فيقال لهم : إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة وهي بعض، كيف صار ابنا ولم تصل غيره من الأبعاض ؟ فإن قيل : لأنه أقل، لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم البنين للأكبر منها، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك، فيصير بكليته بنين. ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر من الأب، كيف صارا قديمين؟ وإن جعل الكل في البدن ، قيل له : أي شيء منه الابن ؟ فإن قال : الكل صيّر الكل ابنا وأبًا، وفي ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه. فإن قيل : هو جزء فيه، من غير أن كان عن كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يُؤخذ من السراج، فيبطل قوله في قدم الروح، وهو الابن. وإن زعم أنه منقول من الله كالمأخوذ [ من السراج ] حل عليه ما سلف. وبعد، فما يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقصه ؟ فإن قيل: معاينتنا إياه كذلك، قيل : لعل الله أحدثه، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج، وأيهما كان فهو حادث، والحادث مخلوق، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا؟
And among them is one who says: a part of God reaches him, and another part separates. Ibn Shabib said: I heard from their birth-claimants that he was Ibn al-Tubna, not Ibn al-Walad, just as the wives of Muhammad (peace be upon him) are called mothers, and as a man says to another: O my son. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said to them: Since the soul that is in him is eternal and it is a part, how did he become a son while no other part reached him? If it is said: Because it is less, then it would be necessary to make every part of the world sons for the greater part of them, and it would be necessary to make every part of the remainder likewise, so that everything would become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father, how can both be eternal? And if he makes the whole in the body, it is said to him: Which thing of him is the son? If he says: The whole, he makes the whole a son and a father, and in that he makes the father a son to himself. If it is said: It is a part in him, without there being any diminution from the whole of the original, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected that if the taken part were originated as happens in what is taken from the lamp, then his statement about the eternity of the soul—which is the son—is invalidated. And if he claims that it is transferred from God like what is taken [from the lamp], what has preceded applies to him. Moreover, how does he know that what is taken from the lamp does not diminish it? If it is said: Our observation of it is thus, it is said: Perhaps God originated it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is originated, and the originated is created, so how is it permissible for it to be a son?
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Christian claim that Jesus is the son of God because a part of God (the soul) entered his body while another part remained separate. The passage opens by stating the claim, then an aside about naming (Ibn Shabib's comment), then Maturidi's refutation. The refutation proceeds by questioning how the soul, being a part of God, can be called a son while no other parts are sons. If the Christian responds that it is because this part is smaller, Maturidi argues that then every smaller part of the world would be a son to a larger part, leading to an infinite regress of sons. Also, the son must be younger, but both are eternal if the soul is eternal. If the Christian says the whole divine substance is in the body, then the whole would be both father and son, making God a son to Himself. If the Christian says it is a part without diminishing the whole, like taking a flame from a lamp, Maturidi counters that the taken flame is originated, not eternal, contradicting the claim of eternal soul. If the Christian says it is transferred like a flame, the same problem arises. Finally, Maturidi questions how we know the lamp is not diminished; if observation is cited, he suggests the flame might be originated like fire from stone, which is created and originated, thus cannot be a son. The argument is a multi-layered logical dismantling of the Christian position.

Key Terms
جزء (part)
الروح (the soul)
الابن (the son)
الأب (the father)
قديم (eternal)
البعض (some/part)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Christian belief that Jesus is the son of God because a part of God entered him is logically coherent.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this belief leads to logical contradictions: it implies all parts of God are sons, leads to infinite regress, and violates the age relationship.

Checkpoint
What is one of Maturidi's objections to calling a part of God 'son'?
A) The soul cannot be eternal.
B) A son must be younger than the father, but both are eternal.
C) The part is too small to be considered a son.
D) The soul is not a part of God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first objections show that the claim of divine sonship through a part leads to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious claim that seems emotionally comforting (like 'God has a son'), do you ever pause to examine its logical consistency? How might this lesson's method of testing claims protect you from error in your own beliefs?
?
The Christians used a familiar human relationship (father-son) to describe God, but it led to absurdities. Are there any ways you unknowingly describe Allah using human categories that might distort your understanding of His oneness? What will you do to correct that?
?
This passage shows that even a small inconsistency in theology can unravel into total absurdity. Where in your own understanding of tawheed might you be tolerating a small inconsistency? How will you investigate it today?

The Lamp Analogy and the Originated Nature of the Divine Part
Maturidi refutes the lamp analogy and argues that the claimed divine part must be originated and created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the analogy of the lamp and demonstrates that the claimed divine part must be created.

Core Idea
What is taken from a lamp is originated, not eternal — the same applies to the claimed divine part.

After showing contradictions in the concept of sonship, Maturidi addresses a defense from Christians: that the divine part is like a flame taken from a lamp without diminishing the source. This lesson examines his response and final refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the defence and the whole/father-son confusion
Read the part where Maturidi considers the possibility that the whole divine substance is in Jesus' body, leading to God being both father and son.
This is a separate argument before the lamp analogy.
2
Analyze the lamp analogy objection
The Christian says the part is like a flame from a lamp — no diminution. Maturidi counters that the taken flame is originated (حادث) not eternal.
This invalidates the eternity of the soul.
3
Consider the transfer argument
If the Christian claims the part is transferred from God like a flame from a lamp, Maturidi says the same problem applies: the taken part is originated.
4
Examine the observation rebuttal
To the claim that observation shows the lamp is not diminished, Maturidi suggests the flame might be originated like fire from stone, which is created, so it cannot be a son.
This final point ties everything together: the divine part must be created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيهم من يقول : يصل إليه جزء من الله تعالى، ويفصل جزء آخر . قال ابن شبيب : سمعت من مولديهم أنه كان ابن التبنى لا ابن الولاد، كما سميت أزواج محمد عليه السلام أمهات، وكما يقول الرجل لآخر : يا بني. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فيقال لهم : إذ كانت الروح التي فيه قديمة وهي بعض، كيف صار ابنا ولم تصل غيره من الأبعاض ؟ فإن قيل : لأنه أقل، لزمه جعل كل أبعاض العالم البنين للأكبر منها، ويلزمه أن يجعل كل بعض من البقية كذلك، فيصير بكليته بنين. ثم المعروف أن الابن يكون أصغر من الأب، كيف صارا قديمين؟ وإن جعل الكل في البدن ، قيل له : أي شيء منه الابن ؟ فإن قال : الكل صيّر الكل ابنا وأبًا، وفي ذلك جعل الأب ابنا لنفسه. فإن قيل : هو جزء فيه، من غير أن كان عن كلية الأصل نقصان نحو الجزء المأخوذ من السراج، عورض بما لو كان الجزء المأخوذ حادثا كما حدث في الذي يُؤخذ من السراج، فيبطل قوله في قدم الروح، وهو الابن. وإن زعم أنه منقول من الله كالمأخوذ [ من السراج ] حل عليه ما سلف. وبعد، فما يدريه أن المأخوذ من السراج لا ينتقصه ؟ فإن قيل: معاينتنا إياه كذلك، قيل : لعل الله أحدثه، أو يكون كالنار في الحجر فيخرج، وأيهما كان فهو حادث، والحادث مخلوق، فلم جاز أن يكون ابنا؟
And among them is one who says: a part of God reaches him, and another part separates. Ibn Shabib said: I heard from their birth-claimants that he was Ibn al-Tubna, not Ibn al-Walad, just as the wives of Muhammad (peace be upon him) are called mothers, and as a man says to another: O my son. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So it is said to them: Since the soul that is in him is eternal and it is a part, how did he become a son while no other part reached him? If it is said: Because it is less, then it would be necessary to make every part of the world sons for the greater part of them, and it would be necessary to make every part of the remainder likewise, so that everything would become sons. Then it is known that the son is younger than the father, how can both be eternal? And if he makes the whole in the body, it is said to him: Which thing of him is the son? If he says: The whole, he makes the whole a son and a father, and in that he makes the father a son to himself. If it is said: It is a part in him, without there being any diminution from the whole of the original, like the part taken from a lamp, it is objected that if the taken part were originated as happens in what is taken from the lamp, then his statement about the eternity of the soul—which is the son—is invalidated. And if he claims that it is transferred from God like what is taken [from the lamp], what has preceded applies to him. Moreover, how does he know that what is taken from the lamp does not diminish it? If it is said: Our observation of it is thus, it is said: Perhaps God originated it, or it is like fire in stone that comes out, and whichever it is, it is originated, and the originated is created, so how is it permissible for it to be a son?
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Christian claim that Jesus is the son of God because a part of God (the soul) entered his body while another part remained separate. The passage opens by stating the claim, then an aside about naming (Ibn Shabib's comment), then Maturidi's refutation. The refutation proceeds by questioning how the soul, being a part of God, can be called a son while no other parts are sons. If the Christian responds that it is because this part is smaller, Maturidi argues that then every smaller part of the world would be a son to a larger part, leading to an infinite regress of sons. Also, the son must be younger, but both are eternal if the soul is eternal. If the Christian says the whole divine substance is in the body, then the whole would be both father and son, making God a son to Himself. If the Christian says it is a part without diminishing the whole, like taking a flame from a lamp, Maturidi counters that the taken flame is originated, not eternal, contradicting the claim of eternal soul. If the Christian says it is transferred like a flame, the same problem arises. Finally, Maturidi questions how we know the lamp is not diminished; if observation is cited, he suggests the flame might be originated like fire from stone, which is created and originated, thus cannot be a son. The argument is a multi-layered logical dismantling of the Christian position.

Key Terms
السراج (the lamp)
نقصان (diminution)
حادث (originated)
مخلوق (created)
الكل (the whole)
منقول (transferred)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy of the lamp demonstrates that a part can be taken from God without diminishing Him and without the part being created.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the taken part is originated, not eternal, and thus created. Therefore it cannot be a divine son.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main objection to the lamp analogy?
A) The lamp is diminished when a flame is taken.
B) The taken flame is originated, not eternal.
C) The flame is not a part of the lamp.
D) The lamp is not a good analogy for God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of the lamp analogy shows that the claimed divine part must be originated and created, making it impossible to be an eternal son.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In Maturidi's argument, he challenges the apparent evidence of observation ('perhaps God originated it'). Are there aspects of your belief about God that you hold simply because 'it seems obvious' or 'everyone says so,' rather than grounding them firmly in revelation? Will you commit today to examining one such belief with the same critical spirit Maturidi applies to the lamp analogy?
?
The Christian insistence on a 'part' of God becoming human reflects a desire to make God tangible and manageable. In what ways do you try to reduce God to human categories—through your expectations of how He should act, or through demands for signs? Will you surrender your own attempts to confine God and accept His complete otherness as taught in this lesson?

Human Limitations Refute Divinity
Maturidi summarizes the dispute into two aspects: lordship and creaturely limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the human attributes of Christ (eating, aging, worship) to disprove claims of his divinity.

Core Idea
Human limitations refute divinity: eating, aging, and worship all point to creaturehood.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi distills the Christian controversy into two fundamental aspects. The first concerns lordship (al-rububiyya). He demonstrates that the very scriptural descriptions of Christ—his eating, aging, and worship—prove he cannot be divine. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Aspects
Read the passage and note that Maturidi says the disagreement reduces to two aspects. The first is lordship. What is the second? (It will be covered later.)
This lesson focuses only on the first aspect.
2
List the Human Traits of Christ
Maturidi lists several traits that are incompatible with divinity. Write down each one: eating, drinking, relieving needs, childhood, old age, worship, supplication, humility, and calling others to worship Allah.
These are all actions of creatures, not the Creator.
3
Reflect on the Argument
For each trait, ask why it cannot apply to God. For example, eating implies need and digestion—God is free of need. Aging implies change—God is unchanging. Worship implies a higher being—God is the highest.
4
Connect to the Broader Purpose
Understand that Maturidi is providing a rational and scriptural rebuttal to the Christian claim that Christ is divine. The evidence is drawn from the very scriptures Christians accept.
This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology against any form of anthropomorphism or association.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذا عندنا أن الاختلاف رجع إلى وجهين. أحدهما الربوبية. والله تعالى جل ثناؤه قد بين إحالة ذلك بأكله وشربه ودفع الحاجات إلى مكان الأقذار، ووصفه بالصغر والكهولة، وعبادته الله تعالى وتضرعه له وخضوعه ، ودعائه الخلق إلى عبادة الله
And the principle in this, according to us, is that the disagreement returns to two aspects: one is lordship. And Allah Almighty, glorified be His praise, has shown the impossibility of that by his (Christ's) eating and drinking and relieving needs to the place of filth, and his description with childhood and old age, and his worship of Allah Almighty and his supplication to Him and his humility, and his calling of creation to the worship of Allah.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion by stating the foundational principle: the dispute about Christ's nature reduces to two aspects, one of which is lordship (الربوبية). Maturidi asserts that God Himself has demonstrated the impossibility of Christ being divine by describing in revelation Christ's human acts: eating, drinking, relieving oneself (implying biological waste), growing from childhood to old age, worshipping God, supplicating, being humble, and calling people to worship Allah. All these are incompatible with divinity. Thus, the Christian claim is refuted by scriptural evidence.

C) Being described as a child.
D) Calling people to worship God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's key move is to use scriptural descriptions of Christ's humanity as direct evidence against his divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Christians claim Jesus is divine despite his human attributes. Maturidi responds using their own scriptures. What false beliefs do you hold about Allah that you might be ignoring from the same Quran you recite? Will you examine one of your assumptions today?

Refuting the Doctrine of al-Aslah
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is best for His creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God's wisdom requires Him to always do what is most beneficial for His creatures.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not bound by human standards of benefit and harm.

Maturidi presents the Mu'tazilite argument that a wise agent always acts for a benefit, and since God does not benefit from His actions, He must act for the benefit of others. This leads them to the doctrine of al-aslah (the best). Maturidi then refutes this by showing that such reasoning improperly analogizes God to human beings and restricts His freedom. This passage is key to understanding the Sunni rejection of imposing human rational expectations on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation side by side. Note that the passage is incomplete due to the page break, but the argument is clear up to that point.
The Arabic has OCR uncertainties, focus on the overall meaning.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find the statement that a rational agent does not act without benefit, and how the Mu'tazila apply this to God to conclude He must always do what is best for others.
They say: 'since everyone who possesses reason does not act for no benefit, he is not wise'.
3
Analyze Maturidi's critique
Notice how Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila analogize God's actions to human actions in a way that is not permissible, for example by comparing wisdom and lying.
They set up examples like lying cannot be wisdom, but then apply the same logic to God.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this refutation means for God's freedom: the Mu'tazilite view limits God to only acting for others' benefit, which Maturidi rejects.
The passage ends before Maturidi fully concludes, but his position is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث
مسائل القضاء والقدر
مسألة
[ في الحكمة والسفه ]
زعم قوم من أهل التوحيد أن أكثر منتحليه خرج منه من وجهين : إما جهلاً بواجبه ، وإما عجزا عن تلخيص القول فيه من مذهب الثنوية وسائر الملحدين فزعم قوم أنه إذ كان كل ممن يعقل [يفعل ] لغير نفع فليس بحكيم، ومن فعل فعلاً لغير علة فهو عابث. فظنوا أن لا يجوز الله أن يبتدأ فعل ضرر بأحد، وأن ذلك يزيل الحكمة عنه ، فألزموه في كل فعل يفعله الأصلح لغيره في الدين والأحسن لغيره في العاقبة؛ إذ هو متعال عن فعل ينفعه أو عن أن يضره شيء، فلم يروا له الفعل إلا بما ينفع غيره، أو يدفع به الضرر عن غيره، فيكون ذلك أيضاً علة فعله على ما كان علة فعل كل حكيم منا ما تأمل من نفع عاجل أو آجل أو دفع [ ضرر ] لزم به، فيجر بذلك حسن الثناء مع جزيل الثواب وضربوا لتقدير فعله بفعل غيره مثلاً بما لا يجوز أن يكون منه الكذب حكمة أو الجور، أو يكون منه الحركة [ من "] غير زوال، أو السكون [من ] غير قرار ، فثبت أن تقدير فعله على فعل الحكماء في الشاهد لازم. إلا أنهم دفعوا عنه الارتفاع بالفعل، والانحطاط بترك فعل ما ، فأوجبوا بذلك أنه بفعله لا يجر إلى نفسه النفع ولا يدفع عنها الضرر. فيجب أن يكون فعله حكمة" بما ينفع غيره أو
Chapter Three: Issues of al-Qada and al-Qadr (divine decree and predestination). Issue: [On Wisdom and Foolishness]. A group from among the people of tawhid claimed that most of its adherents deviated from it in two ways: either out of ignorance of its obligatory aspect, or out of inability to summarize the speech concerning it from the school of the dualists and other atheists. So they claimed that since everyone who possesses reason does not act for no benefit, he is not wise, and whoever does an act without a cause is frivolous. Thus they thought that it is not permissible for God to initiate an act that harms anyone, and that this would remove wisdom from Him. So they necessitated for Him in every act He does what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for others in religion and what is best for others in the outcome; since He is exalted above an act that benefits Him or that anything harms Him. So they did not see for Him any act except that which benefits others, or by which He repels harm from others. So that also becomes the cause of His act, just as the cause of every wise one among us is what he considers of immediate or deferred benefit, or repelling a harm that follows from it. Thus he incurs thereby good praise along with abundant reward. And they analogized His action to the action of others by an example: that it is not permissible that lying be wisdom or injustice, or that movement [be] without displacement, or stillness [be] without stability. So it is established that measuring His action by the action of the wise in the witnessed world is necessary. Except that they denied Him elevation by action, and lowliness by refraining from some action. So they thereby necessitated that by His action He does not draw benefit to Himself nor repel harm from Himself. So it must follow that His action is wisdom in what benefits others or
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on divine wisdom and the doctrine of al-aslah (the best). The author begins by identifying the group as 'people of tawhid' (a term the Mu'tazila used for themselves) and states that they deviated in two ways: ignorance or inability to articulate their view clearly. Then he presents their argument: a rational agent does not act without benefit, so God must act for benefit; since God does not benefit from actions, He must act for the benefit of others, and thus must always do what is best for them. Maturidi then critiques this by noting that they analogize God's actions to human actions in a way that is not permissible—comparing God's wisdom to human wisdom in examples like lying not being wisdom, or movement without displacement. He points out that they deny God the ability to elevate by acting or lower by refraining, and thus they restrict God's actions to only benefiting others, which limits divine freedom. The passage ends mid-sentence on this page, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazilite reasoning forces the conclusion that God's action is only wisdom insofar as it benefits others.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's wisdom requires Him to always do what is most beneficial for His creatures in the same way a human wise person would.
Correction: God's wisdom is not bound by human concepts of benefit and harm; He is free to act according to His own wisdom, which may include trials and hardships for His creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Mu'tazila restrict God's action?
A) They say God can only act for His own benefit.
B) They say God must always do what is beneficial for others.
C) They say God's actions are arbitrary.
D) They say God does not act at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah by showing that it wrongly restricts God's freedom through a flawed analogy to human wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship or loss, do you ever catch yourself thinking that God should have done something different—something that seems 'better' for you? ?
The Mu'tazila thought God must always act for the benefit of others, just as a wise human would. Is there an area in your life where you secretly believe God owes you fairness or goodness based on your own standards? What would it look like to trust that His wisdom is infinitely beyond your calculations, even when it brings pain?
?
If you truly internalized that God is not obligated to do what you consider 'best' for you, how would that change your reaction to unanswered prayers or unexpected trials? The Dualist and Mu'tazila Condition of Benefit for Divine Action
Maturidi presents the Dualist and Mu'tazila condition of benefit and the Tawhid response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Dualists and some Mu'tazila required that God's action bring benefit to Himself, and how the People of Tawhid opposed this.

Core Idea
God's actions are wise by essence, not by benefit to Himself.

Imam Maturidi begins by tackling a subtle theological error: the idea that God's actions cannot be wise unless they benefit Him. This view, shared by Dualists and early Mu'tazila, reduces divine wisdom to a human standard. The passage shows how the People of Tawhid rejected this condition, upholding God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully, then its translation. Notice the technical terms like 'علة' (cause) and 'عبث' (futility).
2
Identify the Dualist position
Find where the Dualists are described: they refused to consider an action wise unless it benefits the agent. Note their reasoning based on analogy from the visible world (shahid).
3
Understand the Mu'tazila agreement
See how the Mu'tazila agreed with the Dualists on requiring benefit for the agent, but differed by making God's repelling of harm from others the cause (illa) of His action.
4
Grasp the Tawhid opposition
Observe that the People of Tawhid opposed both the Dualists and the Mu'tazila in two respects: requiring benefit for the agent, and the claim that the world must have an originating cause.
These two points are summarized by 'فخالفهم أهل التوحيد في هذين'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدفع عن غيره الضرر، وجعلوا ذلك علة فعله ليخرج عندهم فعله عن معنى العبث. وبهذا الوجه خالفوا الثنوية؛ إذ هم أبوا الفعل بغير نفع للفاعل فيه أن يكون من الحكمة، فأثبتوا الفعل بجوهر الحكمة عند المزاج، ليكون في خلاصه من جنس جوهر السفه، فيصير فعله حكمة على التقدير بالشاهد مع ما كان كون شيء لا من شيء ممتنعا في الشاهد، فأوجبوا لكلية العالم أصلاً منه جعل وأنشئ؛ لأنه لا فصل بين خروج الفعل عن حق الوجود في الشاهد وبين خروجه عن العدم إلا بأصل فيلزم دفعه أن يكون ذلك من حكيم. فخالفهم أهل التوحيد في هذين ثم ألزم فريق منهم إياه بما في الفعل علة لم يكن له الفعل دونها، لما وجدوا فعل مثله في الشاهد عبثًا؛ وألزموا في فعل المضار لو كان لغير نفع يُعقب سفها؛ على ما ذكرت الثنوية في فعل لا ينتفع به الفاعل. ثم تفرقوا، فزعم قوم أنه لا ضرر في الحقيقة على المفعول به وإن سمع منه التضرع والشكوى، وزعم قوم أن عليه في الحقيقة ضررًا، لكن عليه أن يعوضه عن ليصير الفعل به حكمة، كالموجود في الشاهد من تحمل المؤن العظام وشرب الأدوية الكريهة القصد، وفصد الجراح لتقع العواقب؛ وليس له فعل الضار بغير إلا بعوض.
He repels harm from others, and they made that the cause of His action so that His action in their view exits from the meaning of futility. And in this respect they opposed the Dualists; for they refused that an action without benefit for the agent in it could be from wisdom, so they affirmed action by the essence of wisdom in mixture, in order that in his salvation from the genus of the essence of folly, his action becomes wisdom according to analogy from the visible world, given that a thing coming from nothing is impossible in the visible world. Thus they required for the totality of the world an origin from which it is made and created; because there is no distinction between an action's exit from the right of existence in the visible world and its exit from non-existence except by an origin, so its repudiation necessitates that this is from a wise one. So the People of Tawhid opposed them in these two matters. Then one group among them [the Mu'tazila] imposed on him [another group] that the action has a cause without which he would not have the action, because they found a similar action in the visible world to be futile; and they imposed in the case of a harmful action that if it were for no benefit it would entail folly, according to what the Dualists mentioned concerning an action from which the agent receives no benefit. Then they diverged: some claimed that there is no real harm to the one acted upon, even if complaint and lamentation are heard from him; and some claimed that there is real harm upon him, but he [the agent] must compensate him so that the action becomes wise by it, like what exists in the visible world of bearing great burdens, drinking unpleasant medicines with purpose, and lancing wounds for the sake of good outcomes; and he has no power to do a harmful action except with compensation.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist and Mu'tazila views on divine action. Maturidi begins by describing a view (likely of the Mu'tazila) that God's action of repelling harm from others is made the cause (ʿilla) of His action, so that His action is not futile. He notes that in this respect they agreed with the Dualists (al-thanawiyya), who refused to consider an action wise unless it brings benefit to the agent. The Dualists instead attributed action to an essence of wisdom (ja whar al-ḥikma) arising from mixture (al-mizāj), so that the agent's action becomes wise only after being saved from folly. They also held that something coming from nothing is impossible in the visible world, so the world must have an originating cause. The People of Tawhid (ahl al-tawḥīd) opposed both the Dualists' requirement of benefit for the agent and their claim that the world must have an origin. Then within the Mu'tazila, differences emerged: some held that every action must have a cause without which it would be futile; others argued that a harmful action without benefit is folly. Finally, the Mu'tazila split on whether harm to the recipient is real or merely apparent: some denied real harm, others affirmed real harm but insisted on compensation to make the action wise, analogous to medical procedures in the visible world. The passage concludes that God cannot do a harmful action without compensation (ʿiwaḍ). This sets up Maturidi's own position that God's actions are wise without needing benefit or compensation.

Key Terms
الثنوية
العبث
الحكمة
العلة
الشاهد
أهل التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's actions must have a purpose that benefits Him, otherwise they would be futile.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this view is shared by Dualists and Mu'tazila but is rejected by Ahl al-Tawhid. God's actions are inherently wise and do not require benefit to Himself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Dualists require that an action bring benefit to the agent?
A) Because they denied God's existence
B) Because they considered actions without benefit to be futile
C) Because they believed in multiple creators
D) Because they rejected divine wisdom

Key Takeaway
God's actions are wise in themselves; requiring benefit for the agent is a condition from the visible world that does not apply to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself assuming that God must act in a certain way because it seems 'beneficial' to you or to others? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's actions are wise by essence, not by your standard of benefit? Commit to one thought you will actively reject this week.
?
The Dualists required benefit for the agent to call an action wise. In your own life, how often do you evaluate your own actions based on what you gain? Could you perform an act of worship purely because it is wise in itself, even if you see no personal benefit? Resolve to perform one such action in secret before the end of this week.
?
The Mu'tazila required that God's actions bring benefit to others to avoid futility. When you pray for others or do good deeds, are you secretly expecting some return? How can you purify your intention to act solely because it is wise and good, not because of any benefit to yourself or even to others? Write down one specific intention you need to correct and a practical step to do so today.

Mu'tazila Internal Dispute on Harm and Compensation
Maturidi reports and critiques the Mu'tazila internal divisions on harm and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal Mu'tazila disagreements on whether God's actions cause real harm and whether compensation is necessary for divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila split on harm and compensation, but all limit God's action by human analogies.

After establishing the general Mu'tazila position requiring a cause for divine action, Maturidi reports their internal divisions. Some denied real harm, others affirmed it and required compensation. This lesson traces those arguments and highlights how they stray from tawhid by imposing human conditions on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the second half of the source text. Focus on the phrases 'ثم تفرقوا' and the two groups described.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazila groups
Find the two opinions: one group denies real harm, another affirms real harm but requires compensation. Note the examples they use from the visible world.
The examples include bearing burdens, taking medicine, and lancing wounds.
3
Understand the compensation argument
See how the second group uses medical analogies to argue that compensation makes a harmful action wise. Then note the final line: 'وليس له فعل الضار بغير إلا بعوض'.
This implies that some Mu'tazila limit God's ability to cause harm without compensation.
4
Reflect on the Tawhid critique
Although not explicit here, the overall context shows that the People of Tawhid reject these restrictions on God's action. Consider why imposing such conditions violates divine transcendence.

So the People of Tawhid opposed them in these two matters. Then one group among them [the Mu'tazila] imposed on him [another group] that the action has a cause without which he would not have the action, because they found a similar action in the visible world to be futile; and they imposed in the case of a harmful action that if it were for no benefit it would entail folly, according to what the Dualists mentioned concerning an action from which the agent receives no benefit. Then they diverged: some claimed that there is no real harm to the one acted upon, even if complaint and lamentation are heard from him; and some claimed that there is real harm upon him, but he [the agent] must compensate him so that the action becomes wise by it, like what exists in the visible world of bearing great burdens, drinking unpleasant medicines with purpose, and lancing wounds for the sake of good outcomes; and he has no power to do a harmful action except with compensation.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist and Mu'tazila views on divine action. Maturidi begins by describing a view (likely of the Mu'tazila) that God's action of repelling harm from others is made the cause (ʿilla) of His action, so that His action is not futile. He notes that in this respect they agreed with the Dualists (al-thanawiyya), who refused to consider an action wise unless it brings benefit to the agent. The Dualists instead attributed action to an essence of wisdom (ja whar al-ḥikma) arising from mixture (al-mizāj), so that the agent's action becomes wise only after being saved from folly. They also held that something coming from nothing is impossible in the visible world, so the world must have an originating cause. The People of Tawhid (ahl al-tawḥīd) opposed both the Dualists' requirement of benefit for the agent and their claim that the world must have an origin. Then within the Mu'tazila, differences emerged: some held that every action must have a cause without which it would be futile; others argued that a harmful action without benefit is folly. Finally, the Mu'tazila split on whether harm to the recipient is real or merely apparent: some denied real harm, others affirmed real harm but insisted on compensation to make the action wise, analogous to medical procedures in the visible world. The passage concludes that God cannot do a harmful action without compensation (ʿiwaḍ). This sets up Maturidi's own position that God's actions are wise without needing benefit or compensation.

Key Terms
الضرر
العوض
التضرع
الشكوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God causes harm, He must compensate the victim to remain just and wise.
Correction: Maturidi presents this as a Mu'tazila view that limits God's absolute freedom. The People of Tawhid hold that God's actions are wise regardless of human concepts of compensation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what analogy do the Mu'tazila who require compensation use?
A) A king rewarding his subjects
B) Bearing burdens, drinking medicine, and lancing wounds
C) A father punishing his child
D) A merchant trading goods

Key Takeaway
Requiring compensation for divine actions measures God by human standards; true tawhid transcends such limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself demanding that God explain His actions to you, as if He must compensate you for your hardships? How does this lesson challenge that attitude and call you to trust His wisdom beyond your own reasoning?
?
This passage shows scholars arguing about God's actions based on human analogies. In your own life, are there areas where you judge God's actions by what seems 'fair' or 'wise' by human standards? What would it mean for you to surrender that judgment and submit to His absolute freedom and wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila debated whether God causes real harm. When you experience pain or loss, do you believe God is truly harming you, or do you find it difficult to reconcile that with His goodness? How does Maturidi's critique of measuring God by human standards push you to rethink your concept of divine justice?

God's Actions Are Necessarily Wise
Maturidi presents the fundamental argument that God's actions are necessarily wise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's actions are always wise because He is self-sufficient and all-knowing, and the causes of unwise action (ignorance and need) are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency and knowledge guarantee all His actions are wise.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: anyone who truly knows God's attributes of self-sufficiency, power, and sovereignty will recognize that His actions cannot depart from wisdom. This lesson unpacks that core argument, showing how the two causes of unwise action in creatures—ignorance and need—are absent in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi uses a conditional 'whoever knows... knows that' structure.
The phrase 'له الخلق والأمر' is a Quranic reference.
2
Identify the key attributes of God mentioned
Maturidi lists self-sufficiency (ghinā), sovereignty (sulṭān), power (qudra), and dominion (mulk). Ask yourself why these specific attributes are foundational to the argument.
These attributes collectively establish God's absolute independence.
3
Spot the two causes of unwise action
In the visible world, departure from wisdom is caused by ignorance or need. Confirm that both are negated from God. Then see how the conclusion follows: God's actions are never unwisely done.
This is a simple syllogism: if cause absent, effect absent.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'ḥakīm bi-dhātihī'
Maturidi says God is wise by essence, not by acquisition. This means wisdom is intrinsic, not dependent on anything external. Consider why this is important.
This reinforces the idea that God's actions are inherently wise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ: من عرف الله حق المعرفة وعلم غناه وسلطانه ثم قدرته وملكه في أنه «له الخلق والأمر» عرف أن فعله لا يجوز أن يخرج عن الحكمة؛ إذ هو حكيم بذاته، غنى عليم. والذي به الخروج عن الحكمة في الشاهد ويبعث صاحبه عليه جهله أو حاجته، وهما منفيان عن الله، فثبت أن فعله غير خارج عن الحكمة. وعلى ما ذكرت يبطل أن يكون فعله في الحركة أو السكون، إذ هما حاجتان يحلان في صاحبهما فيبلغه أحدهما إلى ما تأمل لنفسه من الراحة والسلوة، والآخر إلى ما يبلغه الهمة والرغبة، إذ لا سبيل له إلى مقصوده إلا بالتحرك والزوال، ولا إلى دفع الإعياء والتعب إلا بالقرار والسكون فأما الله سبحانه إذ ثبت غناه وقدرته بطل أن يعتريه حاجة أو يعتريه همة. وعلى ذلك لما ثبتت قدرته وسلطانه وعلمه بطل وصفه بأن لا يقدر على فعل شيء ابتداء لا عن شيء، إذ ذلك علم الحاجة وآية الضعف. وحاجة جميع ما يُحس ويبلغه علم البشر هي الدلالة على تقدير العالم، والله عالم به قدير غني، لم يجز إضافة ذلك إلى الذي عرف غناه وقدرته وحكمته وعلمه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فلذلك لزم القول بضرورة العقل لجواز كون العالم لا عن شيء وخروج فعله على الحكمة، وإن عجزت عقول حكماء العالم عن إدراكها لخروج وجه الحكمة عن نهاية قوة عقولهم، على ما بيّنا من كون شيء لا عن شيء، ومن جواز فعل ممن لا ينتفع به، وبذلك تظهر حقيقة الأمر له، و «أن له الخلق والأمر». ولكل ذي ملك أن يفعل في ملكه على قدر ما ملك منه ما شاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The Shaykh said: Whoever knows God with true knowledge and knows His self-sufficiency and His sovereignty, then His power and His dominion, in that 'His is the creation and the command,' knows that His action cannot depart from wisdom; for He is wise by essence, self-sufficient, knowing. That by which departure from wisdom occurs in the visible world and drives its possessor to it is his ignorance or his need, and both are negated from God. So it is established that His action does not depart from wisdom. And based on what I mentioned, it is invalid that His action be in motion or rest, for they are two needs that settle in their possessor, one bringing him to what he aims for himself of comfort and solace, and the other to what brings him aspiration and desire, for he has no way to his goal except through motion and movement, nor to repel fatigue and exhaustion except through stillness and rest. As for God, exalted, when His self-sufficiency and power are established, it is invalid that need befall Him or that aspiration befall Him. And upon that, when His power, sovereignty, and knowledge are established, His description as being unable to do anything initially from nothing is invalid, for that is a mark of need and a sign of weakness. And the need of all that is sensed and attained by human knowledge is the indication of the measure of the world, and God is knowing of it, powerful, self-sufficient. It is not permissible to attribute that to one who knows His self-sufficiency, power, wisdom, and knowledge. And there is no power except through God. Therefore, it is necessary to affirm by rational necessity the permissibility of the world's existence from nothing and the emergence of His action upon wisdom, even if the minds of the world's sages are incapable of comprehending it because the aspect of wisdom exceeds the limit of the strength of their minds, according to what we have explained about a thing coming from nothing, and about the permissibility of an action from one who does not benefit from it. And by that the reality of the matter becomes clear to him, and 'His is the creation and the command.' And every possessor of dominion may do in his dominion according to the measure of what he possesses of it whatever he wills. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a definitive proof from Maturidi that God's actions are always wise. He starts with a conditional statement: whoever truly knows God's self-sufficiency (ghinā), sovereignty (sulṭān), power (qudra), and dominion (mulk) as expressed in the Quranic verse 'His is the creation and the command' will know that His action cannot depart from wisdom. The reason is that God is wise by essence (ḥakīm bi-dhātihī), self-sufficient, and all-knowing. Maturidi then identifies the two causes of unwise action in the visible world: ignorance (jahl) and need (ḥāja). Both are impossible for God. Hence His actions are necessarily wise. He then expands the argument to show that God cannot be described with motion or rest, because these entail need—either to attain something or to avoid fatigue. Since God is free from need, He is beyond such attributes. Maturidi further argues that because God's power, sovereignty, and knowledge are established, it is invalid to say He cannot create something directly from nothing, as that would imply need and weakness. Human knowledge of the world indicates its measure (taqdīr), but God knows it fully and is powerful and self-sufficient. Therefore, it is rationally necessary to affirm that the world can come from nothing and that God's actions are wise, even if human intellects cannot grasp the specific wisdom. The passage concludes by reaffirming God's absolute sovereignty: every possessor of dominion may act in his dominion as he wills.

Key Terms
الحكمة بذاته
الغنى
الجهل
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what two things in creatures cause departure from wisdom in action?
A) Power and knowledge
B) Ignorance and need
C) Motion and rest
D) Desire and aversion

Key Takeaway
God's actions are always wise because He is free from ignorance and need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or disappointment, do you ever suspect that God's action toward you might not be wise? What does Maturidi's argument say about that suspicion, and how should it change your response?
?
If all your unwise decisions come from ignorance or need, what does that reveal about your constant dependence on God's guidance? Will you today confess a specific area where you have acted as if you were self-sufficient?
?
Are you truly convinced that every single event in your life—including the painful ones—is the act of a wise God who lacks ignorance and need? If not, what attribute of God do you need to study more deeply? Make a plan to do that today.

Divine Attributes
intermediate

God Is Beyond Motion, Rest, and Need
Maturidi draws corollaries about God's transcendence of need and affirms rational truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi denies motion, rest, and any inability in God, and why reason affirms creation from nothing and the wisdom of God's actions even when humans cannot grasp it.

Core Idea
God's power and self-sufficiency mean He acts without motion, rest, or need.

Having established that God's actions are wise, Maturidi draws striking implications. He refutes the idea that God acts through motion or rest, since those imply need. He goes further to dismiss any suggestion that God cannot create from nothing, as that would indicate weakness. This lesson explores these corollaries and concludes with the rational necessity of affirming God's absolute sovereignty, even if human minds cannot always perceive the wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how Maturidi builds on the previous argument and extends it to new implications.
This part contains several distinct points; read slowly.
2
Understand why motion and rest are denied
Maturidi argues that motion and rest are needs (ḥājatān) that affect creatures: motion to attain comfort, rest to avoid exhaustion. Since God is free from need, He is beyond such attributes.
This is an indirect refutation of anthropomorphism.
3
Grasp the argument against inability to create from nothing
Claiming God cannot create directly from nothing would imply need and weakness. Maturidi asserts that God's power and knowledge negate such inability.
This defends the doctrine of creation ex nihilo.
4
Appreciate the conclusion on rational necessity and limits of reason
Maturidi says reason necessarily affirms the world's origin from nothing and God's wise actions, even if human sages cannot comprehend the specific wisdom. Recognise that this is a humble acknowledgment of human limitations.
This balances rational theology with intellectual humility.

Source Text

?

?

?

Divine Attributes

Contextual Nature of Justice and Wisdom
Maturidi establishes a principle and illustrates contextual variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that justice and wisdom are good and their opposites evil in general, but a single action can be considered just or unjust, wise or foolish depending on its context.

Core Idea
The moral value of an action shifts with context, not the action itself.

Maturidi begins by laying down a fundamental principle: injustice and folly are ugly, while justice and wisdom are good. But he immediately adds a crucial nuance: the same act can be wise in one situation and foolish in another, just or unjust depending on circumstances. Let's explore how he explains this contextual variation with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Read the opening sentence. What does Maturidi state about injustice/folly and justice/wisdom? Note that he uses the phrase 'in general' (في الجملة).
This principle is the foundation for the whole discussion.
2
Examine the contextual qualification
Notice how Maturidi says 'but one single thing may be wisdom in one state and folly in another'. This is the key idea. He is not saying justice and wisdom are relative; rather, their application depends on the situation.
3
Analyze the examples
He gives examples: drinking medicine, eating/drinking, and destroying or preserving substances. Think about how these can be wise or foolish, just or unjust based on need, rights, or requital.
These examples show that the moral judgment is not inherent in the act but in its relation to the context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الجور والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة؛ لكن شيئا واحدا قد يكون حكمة في حال سفها في حال، جورا في حال عدلا في حال، نحو ما ذكرت من شرب الأدوية. ثم أكل الأشياء وشربها، ثم إتلاف الأشياء وإبقاؤها من أنواع الجواهر قد يكون للحاجات أو للمجازات أو لحقوق أو لنحو ذلك. وإذا ثبت حسن الحكمة في الجملة والعدل وقبح السفه والجور، ولزم وصف الله تعالى في كل فعل خلقه في أقل ما يوصف أنه حكمة وعدل أو فضل وإحسان من حيث ثبت أنه جواد كريم غنى عليم وبطل أن يلحقه وصف الجور والسفه لما كان سببهما الجهل والحاجة، قد ثبت انقسام الشيء الواحد على الجور والعدل وعلى الحكمة والسفه؛ لما كان سببهما الجهل، وجائز خفاء.
Then the principle is that injustice and folly are ugly, and that justice and wisdom are good in general; but one single thing may be wisdom in one state and folly in another, injustice in one state and justice in another, like what I mentioned of drinking medicines. Then eating things and drinking them, then destroying things and preserving them from types of substances, may be for needs or for requitals or for rights or for something like that. And when the goodness of wisdom and justice in general and the ugliness of folly and injustice are established, it becomes necessary to describe God, exalted, in every action He creates, at the very least, as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence, because it is established that He is Generous, Kind, Self-sufficient, Knowing; and it is invalid that the description of injustice and folly should attach to Him, because their cause is ignorance and need. It has been established that one thing can be divided between injustice and justice and between wisdom and folly; because their cause was ignorance, and concealment is possible.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moral categories and their application to God. Maturidi begins by stating a fundamental principle: injustice (jawr) and folly (safah) are ugly (qabīḥān), while justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma) are good (ḥasanān) in general (fī al-jumla). However, he immediately qualifies this: a single action can be wise in one context and foolish in another, just in one context and unjust in another. He gives examples: drinking medicine (wise for health, foolish without reason); eating and drinking; destroying or preserving substances—these can be for needs, requitals, rights, etc. Therefore, the moral evaluation depends on context. But when it comes to God, since He is established as Generous (jawād), Kind (karīm), Self-sufficient (ghanī), Knowing (ʿalīm), every action of His must be described as wisdom, justice, bounty, and beneficence. The opposite qualities (injustice and folly) cannot attach to God because their cause is ignorance and need, both absent in Him. Maturidi then reaffirms that a single action can be subject to division between justice and injustice, wisdom and folly, because the cause (ignorance) can vary, and concealment (of the true nature) is possible (i.e., humans may not perceive the wisdom). This guards against anthropomorphic or simplistic attributions to God.

Key Terms
الجور
السفه
العدل
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an action is called 'justice' or 'wisdom', it is always good regardless of context.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same act (e.g., destroying something) can be just in one context (e.g., punishment) and unjust in another (e.g., without cause). The moral label depends on the state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of an action that can be wisdom in one state and folly in another?
A) Drinking medicine
B) Sleeping
C) Walking
D) Breathing

Key Takeaway
A single action is not inherently just or unjust; its moral value depends on the context, such as needs, rights, or requitals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you judged someone's action as wrong without knowing their full context. How might that same action have been just or wise in their circumstances? What does this reveal about your own tendency to rush to moral judgment? Will you commit to seeking context before condemning?
?
The lesson teaches that moral value depends on purpose and need. Now examine your own life: are there actions you consider 'good' that you do without a real purpose, merely out of habit or social approval? Are they truly wise in your actual context? Challenge yourself to align your actions with genuine needs and rights, not just convention.
?
You believe God is all-wise and all-just. This lesson says that wisdom and justice are contextual. How does that affect your trust in God's decree, especially when you face events that seem unjust or foolish? Can you accept that there is a hidden context (needs, requitals, rights) that makes God's act just and wise? What would it take for you to surrender your limited judgment to His perfect wisdom?

God's Actions Are Necessarily Just and Wise
Maturidi applies contextual principle to God, concluding His actions are always just and wise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, despite the contextual variability of human actions, every action of God is necessarily just and wise, based on His attributes of generosity, self-sufficiency, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Because God is free from ignorance and need, His actions are always perfectly just and wise.

After establishing that actions can be just or unjust depending on context, Maturidi applies this framework to God. He argues that since God is Generous, Kind, Self-sufficient, and Knowing, His actions cannot be unjust or foolish. Let's follow his reasoning and see how he links God's attributes to the necessity of divine justice and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion about God's actions
Read the second part of the passage. What does Maturidi say must be true about every action of God? He says they must be described at least as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence.
This is the main conclusion.
2
Understand the basis for that conclusion
Maturidi bases this on God's attributes: He is Generous, Kind, Self-sufficient, and Knowing. Why do these attributes guarantee that His actions are just and wise?
Self-sufficiency and knowledge rule out the causes of injustice and folly (ignorance and need).
3
Trace the reasoning about causes
Injustice and folly arise from ignorance and need. Since God has neither, He cannot commit unjust or foolish acts. This is the key link.
Maturidi also notes that the same act can be divided between justice and injustice, wisdom and folly, but the cause of the negative side is ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الجور والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة؛ لكن شيئا واحدا قد يكون حكمة في حال سفها في حال، جورا في حال عدلا في حال، نحو ما ذكرت من شرب الأدوية. ثم أكل الأشياء وشربها، ثم إتلاف الأشياء وإبقاؤها من أنواع الجواهر قد يكون للحاجات أو للمجازات أو لحقوق أو لنحو ذلك. وإذا ثبت حسن الحكمة في الجملة والعدل وقبح السفه والجور، ولزم وصف الله تعالى في كل فعل خلقه في أقل ما يوصف أنه حكمة وعدل أو فضل وإحسان من حيث ثبت أنه جواد كريم غنى عليم وبطل أن يلحقه وصف الجور والسفه لما كان سببهما الجهل والحاجة، قد ثبت انقسام الشيء الواحد على الجور والعدل وعلى الحكمة والسفه؛ لما كان سببهما الجهل، وجائز خفاء.
Then the principle is that injustice and folly are ugly, and that justice and wisdom are good in general; but one single thing may be wisdom in one state and folly in another, injustice in one state and justice in another, like what I mentioned of drinking medicines. Then eating things and drinking them, then destroying things and preserving them from types of substances, may be for needs or for requitals or for rights or for something like that. And when the goodness of wisdom and justice in general and the ugliness of folly and injustice are established, it becomes necessary to describe God, exalted, in every action He creates, at the very least, as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence, because it is established that He is Generous, Kind, Self-sufficient, Knowing; and it is invalid that the description of injustice and folly should attach to Him, because their cause is ignorance and need. It has been established that one thing can be divided between injustice and justice and between wisdom and folly; because their cause was ignorance, and concealment is possible.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification of moral categories and their application to God. Maturidi begins by stating a fundamental principle: injustice (jawr) and folly (safah) are ugly (qabīḥān), while justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma) are good (ḥasanān) in general (fī al-jumla). However, he immediately qualifies this: a single action can be wise in one context and foolish in another, just in one context and unjust in another. He gives examples: drinking medicine (wise for health, foolish without reason); eating and drinking; destroying or preserving substances—these can be for needs, requitals, rights, etc. Therefore, the moral evaluation depends on context. But when it comes to God, since He is established as Generous (jawād), Kind (karīm), Self-sufficient (ghanī), Knowing (ʿalīm), every action of His must be described as wisdom, justice, bounty, and beneficence. The opposite qualities (injustice and folly) cannot attach to God because their cause is ignorance and need, both absent in Him. Maturidi then reaffirms that a single action can be subject to division between justice and injustice, wisdom and folly, because the cause (ignorance) can vary, and concealment (of the true nature) is possible (i.e., humans may not perceive the wisdom). This guards against anthropomorphic or simplistic attributions to God.

Key Terms
الجواد الكريم
الغنى العليم
الجور
السفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God could, if He willed, act unjustly or foolishly, since humans can.
Correction: Maturidi argues that injustice and folly arise from ignorance and need, which God lacks. Therefore, it is impossible (invalid) for God to act unjustly or foolishly.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the cause of injustice and folly in actions?
A) Power and might
B) Ignorance and need
C) Will and desire
D) Creation and sustenance

Key Takeaway
Because God is free from ignorance and need, every action of His is necessarily just, wise, and beneficent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that you do not understand, do you truly believe that God's action is still wise and just, even though you cannot see the wisdom? What would change if you fully internalised that your lack of understanding does not negate God's perfection? Will you commit to recalling this truth when you next feel confused about God's decree?
?
If you ever catch yourself thinking that a divine command is unfair or foolish, pause and examine that thought. Is it not your own limited perspective that makes it seem so? How would your obedience improve if you trusted that every single command from God is ultimately wise? Can you today submit to a command you have been resisting, even if you do not see the wisdom yet?

Moral Judgments Depend on Context
Maturidi introduces the principle that actions are not inherently just/unjust but are judged by context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the moral categories of justice and wisdom are not intrinsic to actions but depend on the circumstances and purposes.

Core Idea
Context determines whether an action is just or foolish.

Maturidi begins by stating a general principle: injustice and foolishness are ugly, justice and wisdom are good. However, he immediately refines this by showing that one and the same action, like drinking medicine, can be wise in one context and foolish in another. This lesson explores that foundational idea.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the general principle before the qualification.
Notice the phrase 'في الجملة' meaning 'in general'.
2
Identify the example
Find the example of drinking medicine and see how it illustrates the contextual nature.
Think of other examples where the same action could be good or bad.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this principle affects our understanding of moral judgments about human actions.
This will be applied to divine actions in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الجور والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة؛ لكن شيئًا واحدا قد يكون حكمة في حال سفها في حال، جورًا في حال عدلاً في حال، نحو ما ذكرتُ من شرب الأدوية. ثم أكل الأشياء وشربها، ثم إتلاف الأشياء وإبقاؤها من أنواع الجواهر قد يكون للحاجات أو للمجازات أو لحقوق أو لنحو ذلك. الحكمة وإذا ثبت حسن في الجملة والعدل وقبح السفه والجور، ولزم وصف الله تعالى في كل فعل خلقه في أقل ما يوصف أنه حكمة وعدل أو فضل وإحسان من حيث ثبت أنه جواد كريم غنى عليم وبطل أن يلحقه وصف الجور والسفه لما كان سببهما الجهل والحاجة، قد ثبت انقسام الشيء الواحد على الجور والعدل وعلى الحكمة والسفه؛ لما كان سببهما الجهل، وجائز خفاء وجه ذلك على الناظر المتأمل، أو هو بالحس يريد الاطلاع على العلم به. وقد ثبت احتمال الوجهين، لا يقع على واحد منهما الحس وعلم المتأمل ذلك، فبطل قضاؤه في شريعته على الإشارة إليه بالحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور فلزم بهذا جهل كل من البشر المعرفة حقيقة الأمرين في الشيء بالتأمل فيه مثل أن يعرف جميع الأسباب التي بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس.
Then the principle is that injustice and foolishness are ugly, and that justice and wisdom are good in general; but one thing may be wisdom in a situation and foolishness in another, injustice in a situation and justice in another, such as what I mentioned about drinking medicines. Then eating and drinking things, then destroying and preserving things from types of substances may be for needs or for recompenses or for rights or the like. Wisdom and when it is established that justice is good in general and injustice and foolishness are ugly, and it is necessary to describe Allah, the Exalted, in every action He creates in the least thing that is described, that it is wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence, because it is established that He is generous, bountiful, self-sufficient, knowing, and it is invalid that the description of injustice and foolishness should attach to Him because their cause is ignorance and need. It has been established that one thing can be divided into injustice and justice and into wisdom and foolishness; because their cause is ignorance, and it is possible that the aspect of this is hidden from the reflective observer, or he seeks by sense experience to obtain knowledge of it. And it has been established that two aspects are possible, so neither sense experience nor the knowledge of the observer concerning that applies to either one, so his judgment in his [the observer's] law based on indicating it as wisdom and foolishness and justice and injustice becomes invalid. Thus, by this, every human's knowledge of the true nature of the two matters in a thing by reflecting on it, such as knowing all the causes by which the states of sensed things change with respect to the senses, is made impossible.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage classifies actions and establishes a key principle: the moral qualities of justice/injustice and wisdom/foolishness are not intrinsic but depend on circumstances. Maturidi begins by stating the general rule that injustice and foolishness are ugly, while justice and wisdom are good. However, he immediately qualifies: the same action can be wise in one situation and foolish in another, just in one and unjust in another. The example is drinking medicine. He extends this to eating, drinking, and destroying or preserving objects, which can be for needs, recompenses, or rights. Then he applies this to God: since God is established as generous, bountiful, self-sufficient, and knowing, every act of His must be described as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence; the descriptions of injustice and foolishness cannot apply to Him because their cause is ignorance and need, which are absent in God. Maturidi then notes that a single action can have two aspects (just and unjust, wise and foolish) depending on the context, and this is due to ignorance (of the observer). It is possible that the true aspect is hidden from the observer, or he tries to know it through sense perception but cannot. Since both aspects are possible, sense perception and the observer's knowledge cannot decisively attribute wisdom or foolishness, justice or injustice to an action. Therefore, any human judgment in his own law (shari'atihi) based on indicating the action as wise or foolish is invalid. Consequently, humans cannot know the true nature of the two matters (wisdom vs foolishness, justice vs injustice) in a thing through mere reflection, because they lack knowledge of all the causes that change the states of sensed things.

Key Terms
الجور
السفه
العدل
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that an action is always either just or unjust regardless of situation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same action can be just or unjust depending on the context, such as drinking medicine.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi give to show that one action can be both wise and foolish depending on circumstances?
A) Sleeping and waking
B) Drinking medicines
C) Building and destroying
D) Giving and taking

Key Takeaway
Moral judgments of actions are not fixed but depend on context and purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a recent situation where you immediately labeled someone's action as 'wrong' or 'stupid'. Without reading this lesson, that judgment might have been absolute. Now, can you think of a possible context that would make that action wise or just? What would it take for you to pause and consider context before judging others? Will you commit to doing that this week?
?
When you make a decision—like spending money or speaking harshly—do you evaluate the context thoroughly, or do you rely on a rule of thumb? This lesson teaches that the same act can be wise or foolish based on purpose. Think of a habit of yours that might be 'foolish' in one context but 'wise' in another. Are you aware of which context you are actually in? How can you realign your actions to be consistently wise?
?
If you truly believe that justice and wisdom are defined by context, then is your own life currently reflecting that? Are there areas where you have rigid standards that may actually be unjust or foolish in your unique situation? What is one area where you need to reevaluate using the principle of this lesson, and what is the first step you will take to change?

Why All Divine Actions Are Wise and Just
Maturidi argues that Allah's actions are all wise and just because He lacks ignorance and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every act of Allah must be described as wisdom and justice, and cannot be described as injustice or foolishness.

Core Idea
Because Allah is free from ignorance and need, all His actions are wise and just.

After establishing that actions can be judged differently based on context, Maturidi applies this principle to Allah. Since Allah is known to be generous, bountiful, self-sufficient, and all-knowing, He cannot be associated with injustice or foolishness, whose causes are ignorance and need. This lesson explains that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the divine application
Read the passage where Maturidi connects the general principle to divine actions.
Notice how he uses the attributes of Allah to argue for the necessity of wisdom and justice.
2

Maturidi says the cause of injustice and foolishness is ignorance and need. Check that you understand why.
These are absent in Allah.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this means that every action of Allah is good and just, even if we don't understand it.
This is a key point in Islamic theology.

Why Humans Cannot Always Judge Actions as Wise or Foolish
Maturidi argues that humans cannot definitively classify actions due to the multiplicity of possible aspects and limitations of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human observers cannot decisively classify an action as wise or foolish, just or unjust, based on reflection alone.

Core Idea
Human judgment is limited because both aspects are possible and hidden causes may exist.

Maturidi concludes his argument by noting that a single action can have two aspects (just and unjust, wise and foolish) from different perspectives. This makes it impossible for humans to definitively judge an action's moral status through reflection alone, because they lack knowledge of all the causes. This lesson explains that limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on two aspects
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the claim that one thing can be divided into two aspects.
See how Maturidi says both sense and knowledge cannot determine which aspect applies.
2
Understand the epistemological point
Maturidi says that because two aspects are possible, and we cannot know all causes, our judgment is invalid.
Think about examples where we cannot tell if an action is wise.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that humans cannot know the true nature of the two matters in a thing. How does this affect our understanding of God's actions?
This reinforces that we should trust in God's wisdom even when we don't understand.

He extends this to eating, drinking, and destroying or preserving objects, which can be for needs, recompenses, or rights. Then he applies this to God: since God is established as generous, bountiful, self-sufficient, and knowing, every act of His must be described as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence; the descriptions of injustice and foolishness cannot apply to Him because their cause is ignorance and need, which are absent in God. Maturidi then notes that a single action can have two aspects (just and unjust, wise and foolish) depending on the context, and this is due to ignorance (of the observer). It is possible that the true aspect is hidden from the observer, or he tries to know it through sense perception but cannot. Since both aspects are possible, sense perception and the observer's knowledge cannot decisively attribute wisdom or foolishness, justice or injustice to an action. Therefore, any human judgment in his own law (shari'atihi) based on indicating the action as wise or foolish is invalid. Consequently, humans cannot know the true nature of the two matters (wisdom vs foolishness, justice vs injustice) in a thing through mere reflection, because they lack knowledge of all the causes that change the states of sensed things.

Key Terms
الجهل
الحاجة
الحكمة
السفه
العدل
الجور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think they can always determine if an action is wise or foolish by reasoning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that because of hidden causes, humans cannot definitively judge an action's moral status.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the possibility of two aspects in an action?
A) Only one aspect is real
B) Both aspects are possible but sense and knowledge cannot determine which
C) The observer can always know which aspect is correct
D) Only God knows the aspects

Key Takeaway
Human reason alone cannot definitively classify actions as wise or foolish due to limited knowledge of all causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you judge others' actions as foolish or unjust without considering that you might be ignorant of hidden causes? Will you commit to pausing before making such judgments?
?
If true wisdom and justice can only be known by Allah, how does that affect your reliance on your own reasoning in matters of faith?
?
When you are certain you know the wisdom behind an event, does this lesson remind you of your limited perspective? How can you remind yourself daily of your epistemic humility?
