Definition of Foolishness and God's Transcendence
Maturidi defines foolishness in human actions and affirms God's transcendence above it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of foolishness in human actions and why it cannot be attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Foolishness is transgression or disobedience; Allah is above both.

Maturidi begins by establishing a clear, rational definition of foolishness (safah) based on observable human actions. He then applies this definition to Allah, showing why such a description is impossible for the Creator. This passage is foundational for understanding Islamic theology's use of reason to affirm divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: definition, negation, distinction from lying, and start of proof.
The final sentence is incomplete; focus on the complete argument.
2
Identify the two causes of foolishness
Find the phrase 'أحد أمرين' and note the two conditions that make an action foolish in the witnessed world (al-shahid).
These are: transgression of dominion without permission, and acting against a command.
3
Understand the negation for Allah
See how Maturidi states that both causes are negated for Allah (منفي). Reflect on why Allah cannot be described by either.
Because He is the absolute owner and no one commands Him.
4
Distinguish between foolishness and lying
Notice the distinction: lying is always unacceptable, while actions can be wise or foolish depending on context. Allah is above both.
This clarifies that not all negative actions are equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي يجعل الفعل في الشاهد سفها أحد أمرين: إما تعدى الملك لا بإذن من له الملك لذلك الفعل، أو لما فيه ركوب النهي ومخالفة الأمر ممن له الأمر والنهي؛ وكل ذلك الله جل ثناؤه منفي. ثبت أنه يتعالى عن احتمال لحوق هذا الوصف فعله. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وليس ذلك كالكذب، لأنه لا يصلح بحال أن يكون كالفعل الذي ينقسم على الحكمة والسفه، والعدل والجور. وهذا من حيث الجملة لا انقلاب له، ومن حيث الوقوع في شيء على الإشارة إليه ممكن فيه الأمران باختلاف الأحوال والأسباب. لذلك لزم وصف الله تعالى في الجملة بالتعالي عن فعل السفه والجور وفي الإشارة أيضاً. لكن لا يجوز أن يوصف فيما ظهر فعله بالسفه والجور، بما لا يبلغه علم البشر ولا يدركه عقل ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم جملة ما به يعلم فساد الوصف بالجور والسفه والكذب وجهان. أحدهما قبح ذلك في العقول بالبديهة والفكر جميعا، حتى لا يزداد عند التأمل والبحث عنه إلا قبحا، ولا عند طول
Then the principle that makes an action foolish in the witnessed world is one of two things: either transgression of dominion without permission from the owner of the dominion for that action, or because it involves riding the prohibition and opposing the command of the one who has command and prohibition. And all of that is negated for Allah, majestic is His praise. It is established that He is exalted above the possibility of this description attaching to His action. There is no power except with Allah. And that is not like lying, because lying is not at all suitable to be like the action that is divided into wisdom and foolishness, and justice and injustice. And this, in terms of general principle, has no reversal, and in terms of occurrence in a thing, by indication it is possible that both aspects exist in it due to differing circumstances and causes. Therefore, it is necessary to describe Allah, the Exalted, in general as exalted above the action of foolishness and injustice, and also in particular. But it is not permissible to describe what appears of His action as foolishness and injustice, because human knowledge does not reach it nor does intellect perceive it. There is no power except with Allah. Then the summary of what is known about the corruption of describing [Him] with injustice, foolishness, and lying is two ways. One of them is the ugliness of that in the intellects, both by intuition and reasoning, such that it only increases in ugliness upon reflection and investigation, nor upon length [of time].
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines foolishness (safah) in the witnessed world (al-shahid) and then affirms God's transcendence. Maturidi states that an action is considered foolish for one of two reasons: (1) exceeding one's dominion without permission from the rightful owner, or (2) engaging in what is prohibited and opposing the command of one who has authority. Both of these are negated for God, as He is the absolute owner and no one commands Him. Therefore, God is exalted above having foolishness or injustice attributed to His actions. Maturidi distinguishes this from lying, which is never permissible, while actions that can be either wise or foolish depending on context are different. In general, the principle of foolishness (in the sense of transgression or disobedience) does not apply to God. However, when looking at particular actions, it is possible that both aspects (wisdom/foolishness) could be present due to differing circumstances, but God is described in general as transcending both. Yet, we cannot describe any particular action of God as foolish or unjust because human knowledge and intellect cannot fully comprehend the wisdom behind it. Finally, Maturidi begins to list the two ways to know the corruption of describing God with injustice, foolishness, and lying: first, their ugliness is known by reason, both intuitively and through reflection, and it only increases in ugliness when examined. The text cuts off.

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shahid)
السفه (al-safah)
تعدى الملك (ta'addi al-mulk)
ركوب النهي (rukub al-nahy)
الكذب (al-kadhib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any action that seems purposeless to humans is foolish, even for Allah.
Correction: Maturidi defines foolishness specifically as transgression of authority or disobedience to a command, both of which are impossible for Allah. Lack of perceived benefit does not constitute foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two causes of foolishness in human actions?
A) Ignorance and need
B) Transgression of dominion and disobedience to command
C) Lack of benefit and harm
D) Weakness and desire

Key Takeaway
Foolishness is rationally defined as transgression or disobedience; Allah is absolutely transcendent above both, and human intellect cannot judge His particular actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you recognize it as an act of foolishness according to Maturidi's definition? Or do you downplay it by thinking it's not 'that bad'? What would it take for you to see every sin as an act of foolish transgression?
?
Have you ever questioned Allah's wisdom in a difficult situation, attributing to Him in your heart what He is exalted above? If you are honest, when have you doubted that His actions are perfectly wise?
?
The passage says that the ugliness of describing Allah with foolishness increases upon reflection. Do you actively reflect on Allah's attributes to cultivate awe and reverence? What specific practice can you start this week to deepen your conviction that Allah is above all imperfection?

If God Could Be Unjust, Promises Become Untrustworthy
Maturidi refutes the possibility of divine injustice by showing its consequences for trust.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine justice is essential for trust in God's promises and threats, and how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili position that God could potentially act unjustly.

Core Idea
Justice is not merely possible but necessary for God to be trustworthy.

Maturidi begins by showing the dangerous consequences of allowing even the possibility of injustice in God. If God could act unjustly, then His promises and threats become unreliable, which contradicts the very purpose of revelation. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why the Mu'tazili view undermines faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening conditional
Maturidi starts with 'If His action were such'—meaning if God's actions could include injustice. Notice the immediate consequences: promise not trusted, threat not feared, goodness not desired, evil not safe from. That's a cascade of ruin.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the Mu'tazili claim leads to absurd outcomes.
2
Identify the attributes mentioned
Maturidi then lists God's attributes: All-Knowing, All-Wise, Self-Sufficient, nothing hidden from Him, no matter difficult. These attributes make injustice impossible. Connect each attribute to why injustice cannot occur.
Consider: how does being Self-Sufficient (غنى بنفسه) eliminate the motive for injustice?
3
Examine the Mu'tazili consequence
The Mu'tazila claim most things come out of God's will, yet some exist against His will in His own dominion. Maturidi says this implies God has no authority to remove what He does not will—a contradiction. Understand why this is impossible for the All-Powerful.
This shows the Mu'tazili position inadvertently diminishes God's sovereignty.
4
Understand the promise-breaking scenario
Maturidi gives an example: God promises people lifetimes and provisions, but then allows others to kill them before the term. This would make God break His promise, leading to foolishness and injustice. Trace how this scenario follows from the Mu'tazili premise.
The example illustrates that the Mu'tazili view forces God into a position of needing to fulfill a promise but being prevented—which is impossible.
5
Conclude with the necessity of trust
The passage ends this section by asking: with such a God, can there be safety? Maturidi affirms that God is described with generosity, nobility, pardon, and beneficence—so injustice cannot be attributed to Him.
This wraps the argument: God's known attributes rule out the possibility of injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتمل فعله ذلك لا يوثق لوعده ولا يخاف وعيده، ولا يرغب في خيره ولا يؤمن شره. ذا شأنه وعمله فمحال احتمال إضافة مثله إلى العليم الحكيم بذاته الغنى بنفسه مع الوصف بأن لا يخفى عليه شيء، ولا يصعب عليه أمر فيها أراد بل على قول المعتزلة لا يؤمن منه هذا ، إذ قد تخرج أكثر الأشياء عن إرادته، ويوجد ما لا يريده في سلطانه فيه بلا سلطان له في الإخراج عنه إذ لم يرده ؛ ويريد زيادة سلطانه ويتولى ذلك أن يكون، فيُمنع عن نحو ما يريد أن يكون جميع خلقه مطيعين ويكون له في سلطانه وملكه الطاعة لا المعاصي فلا يكون . ثم قد كان وعد لقوم مُدَدًا لأعمارهم ، وهو المبقى لهم إليها، وكان في وعده أن يرزقهم إلى تلك المدد أنواع الرزق ويسوق إليهم أنواع [الخيرات]، فيأتي خلق من خلائقه فيقتلوهم قبل مضي المدة، فيمنعه عن إنجاز ما وُعِدُوا الوفاء بالفعل الذي أخبرهم أن يفعله من إبقاء حياتهم إلى تلك المدة. وفي ذلك إيجاب الحاجة ولحوق الكذب اللذين يحققان السفه والجور؛ مع تحقيقهم له القدرة على الظلم والجور والسفه والكذب وكل فعل لو كان لأسقط الربوبية وأزال الإلهية، فأدخلو إلهيته وربوبيته تحت القدرة والتدبير . فمتى يكون مع مثله أمن البقاء، حاله سكون القلب بالوفاء بالذي وعد ولا قوة إلا بالله مع ما كان موصوفا بالجود والكرم والعفو والإحسان، وفي الفعل بالوصف الذي ذكرنا زواله جل عن ذلك وتعالى. والوجه الثاني أن الذي يدعو إلى تلك الأفعال ويبعث عليها الحاجة والجهل. وقد ثبت الأمرين، إذ هما يسقطان الربوبية ويزيلان التدبير وفي وجود العالم على ما [هو] دلالة غنا صاحبه وعلمه بإعطاء كل شيء حقه دليل إحالة هذا الوصف، لذلك بطل أن يوصف في شيء من فعله بذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If His action were such, then His promise would not be trusted, nor His threat feared, nor would one desire His goodness nor be safe from His evil. For one of such character and action, it is impossible to attribute the like to the All-Knowing, All-Wise by essence, the Self-Sufficient, with the description that nothing is hidden from Him, and no matter is difficult for Him when He wills. Rather, according to the Mu'tazila, this cannot be safe from Him, since most things come out of His will, and what He does not will exists in His dominion without authority to remove it because He did not will it; and He wills the increase of His dominion and undertakes that it be, yet He is prevented from what He wills that all His creatures be obedient and that in His dominion and kingdom there be obedience and not disobedience, so it is not. Then He had promised people fixed terms for their lifetimes, and He is the one who preserves them until them, and in His promise was that He would provide them with various types of provision until those terms and send to them various [good things]; then a creation of His creatures comes and kills them before the term passes, preventing Him from fulfilling what they were promised—the fulfillment of the act which He had informed them He would do, of preserving their lives until that term. In that is necessitating need and incurring lying, which realize foolishness and injustice; along with their affirming for Him the power to commit injustice, oppression, foolishness, and lying, and every act which, if it were, would nullify lordship and remove divinity, so they entered His divinity and lordship under power and governance. When would there be with one like this safety of permanence, his state being tranquility of heart in fulfilling what He promised? And there is no power except with God, along with His being described with generosity, nobility, pardon, and beneficence. And in acting with the description we mentioned, His exaltation above that and His transcendence. The second aspect is that which calls to those actions and impels them is need and ignorance. Both have been established, for they negate lordship and remove governance. And in the existence of the world as it is, there is evidence of the self-sufficiency of its Owner and His knowledge by giving everything its due, proof of the impossibility of this description. Therefore, it is invalid that He be described with that in any of His actions. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili claim that God could potentially act unjustly or break His promises. Maturidi begins with a conditional: if God's actions were unjust, His promises would be unreliable, which contradicts His attributes of knowledge and wisdom. He then explains that the Mu'tazila's position implies that things can occur against God's will in His own dominion, which is impossible. A second argument is that only need and ignorance motivate unjust acts, and God is free from both. The existence of the world in order proves God's self-sufficiency and knowledge, negating any possibility of foolishness in His actions.

Key Terms
الجور (injustice)
الوعيد (threat)
السفه (foolishness)
الربوبية (lordship)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power includes the ability to commit injustice, even if He never does.
Correction: Maturidi argues that injustice is not a matter of power but of character; God's attributes of wisdom, self-sufficiency, and justice preclude any possibility of injustice, so it is not even within His power in that sense.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what immediate consequence would follow if God could act unjustly?
A) People would still trust His promises because He is merciful.
B) His promises would not be trusted and His threats not feared.
C) Only the unjust would fear God.
D) Nothing would change because God never actually acts unjustly.

Key Takeaway
If God could be unjust, trust in His promises and threats collapses, contradicting His attributes of wisdom and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you actually live as if God will fulfill every promise He made in the Qur'an—or do you secretly doubt that His threat of punishment applies to you personally? Where in your life are you acting as if God's promise could fail?
?
When you see injustice in the world, do you ever question God's justice? How does this lesson expose that your question really doubts either His power or His wisdom? What would change in your heart if you truly internalized that God cannot be unjust?
?
Is there an area of your life where you feel God is not giving you what He 'promised'—like ease after hardship or provision? Could it be that you have misunderstood the promise, or that your impatience reveals a hidden belief that God 'owes' you something? How does this passage call you to trust His character rather than your expectations?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Only Need and Ignorance Motivate Injustice – God Is Free of Both
Maturidi argues that only need and ignorance cause injustice, which God lacks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument that only need and ignorance could drive unjust actions, and since God is self-sufficient and all-knowing, He cannot be unjust.

Core Idea
Injustice stems from need or ignorance; God has neither.

Maturidi presents a second, more direct argument: the only motives for injustice are need (deficiency) and ignorance (lack of knowledge). God is self-sufficient and all-knowing, so these motives are absent. This lesson examines that argument and how the order of the world confirms God's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two motives
Maturidi says that what calls to unjust actions are 'need' (الحاجة) and 'ignorance' (الجهل). Understand why these two are sufficient to cause any injustice. Consider: why would a perfect being have no need and no ignorance?
Need implies dependence; ignorance implies lack of wisdom. Both are impossible for God.
2
See how these negate lordship
Maturidi asserts that need and ignorance 'negate lordship and remove governance.' Reflect on why a lord who needs something or is ignorant cannot be truly sovereign.
True lordship implies absolute self-sufficiency and complete knowledge.
3
Examine the evidence from the world
The existence of the world in its orderly state is proof of the Owner's self-sufficiency and knowledge. 'Giving everything its due' shows both wisdom and lack of need. This empirical argument reinforces the rational one.
The world's perfect order reflects the Creator's perfect attributes.
4
Conclude the invalidity of injustice in God
Therefore, it is invalid to describe God with any form of injustice or foolishness in any of His actions. All His acts are just and wise.
This conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.

الحاجة (need)
الجهل (ignorance)
السفه (foolishness)
الغنى (self-sufficiency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be unjust if He wanted to, but He chooses not to.
Correction: Maturidi shows that injustice requires a motive (need or ignorance) that God lacks, so it is not a matter of choice but of impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what two motives drive unjust actions?
A) Pride and anger.
B) Need and ignorance.
C) Desire and power.
D) Habit and tradition.

Key Takeaway
Only need and ignorance cause injustice; God, being self-sufficient and all-knowing, cannot be unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act unjustly towards someone, what need or ignorance is really driving you? Are you willing to honestly identify and uproot it today?
?
This lesson says that God’s self-sufficiency and knowledge make injustice impossible for Him. How does that truth expose your own excuses for being unjust, and what specific step will you take to act justly even when it is hard?
?
If you truly believed that God’s perfect knowledge gives everything its due, how would that change the way you handle situations where you feel wronged or overlooked?

Human Limitation in Judging Divine Actions
Maturidi argues from human susceptibility to foolishness to the invalidity of judging God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human susceptibility to foolishness invalidates any judgment of God's actions.

Core Idea
Humans are inherently fallible; only God's attributes are perfect.

Maturidi begins by establishing that God's attributes are essential and unchanging, unlike human attributes which are contingent and susceptible to opposites. He argues that since every human sage is capable of foolishness, we cannot use human standards to evaluate divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's dense argument.
2
Identify the key attributes
Notice that God's attributes (knowledge, power, might, life) are by His essence, making Him immune to change. Humans, on the other hand, have these attributes only partially and can exhibit opposites.
3
Understand the analogy of the sage
Maturidi says no human sage is immune to foolishness. When we see an action as foolish, it may be due to our limited knowledge or perspective.
This refutes the notion that humans can objectively judge God's actions.
4
Reflect on human ignorance
Maturidi points out that we are aware of our ignorance, need, and incapacity. This awareness should prevent us from passing judgment on what God does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ كان الله جل ثناؤه موصوفًا بالعلم والقدرة والجبروت والحياة لذاته، لإحالة احتماله الأغيار، وإذ لم يوجد في الحكماء كذلك لم يجب تقديره في أفعاله من أفعال الحكماء في ظلم الشاهد وجملة هذا الأصل أنه لا حكيم في الشاهد إلا وهو محتمل للسفه، وكذلك الغنى والعليم والقدير محتمل لأضداد تلك الصفات، وكان بها موصوفا حتى أُكرم بأضدادها، فإنما له منها قدر ما أعطى منها . فهو - متى رأى السفه في شيء ـ بَين أن يكون قد أُعطى صفته علم حقيقة الحكمة في ذلك أو لا، أو بلغ علمه ما يدرك حكمته أو لا، أو مما كان من القديمة باقية فيه يمنع ذلك إياه عن الإحاطة بذلك. فلذلك يبطل وجه دعوى العبد في فعل الله أن ذا ليس بحكمة ولا كذا. والذي يوضح ذلك علمه بجهله بأكثر الأشياء، وعلمه بحاجته وعجزه في أكثر الأمور ، وإحاطته بسفهه في أغلب الأمر. ومن هذا وصفه في نفسه فخوضه فيما الله أن يفعله على الإشارة إليه دون لزوم الجملة - وليس اشترك فيها العقلاء، إذ ذلك حقيقة عمل العقل في الجملة وقد أعطى كل ذلك ـ عبث لا معنى له . وللذي بينا قال الله تعالى : لا يسأل عما يفعل وهم يسألون [الأنبياء، ٢٣/٢١]، إذ فعل كل أحد يحتمل السفه والحكمة، وفعله يَجلّ عن السفه ؛ وعلى كل أحدٍ أمر ونهى إذ هو لغيره في الحقيقة، والله يتعالى عن ذلك؛ ولأن كلاً إنما ملك قدرًا من الأشياء وجزءاً، والله المالك لها بكليتها ونحو ذلك مما يحيل معنى سؤال الرب. وإذا استحال ذلك فالجواب عنه تكلف.
Then since God, exalted be His mention, is described with knowledge, power, might, and life by His essence, due to the impossibility of His undergoing change, and since the wise (philosophers) are not found to be such, it is not necessary to judge His actions by the actions of the wise in the witnessed world. The summary of this principle is that no sage in the witnessed world is except that he is susceptible to foolishness, likewise the rich, the knowledgeable, and the powerful are susceptible to opposites of these attributes, and they are described by them until they are honored by their opposites. So he only has from them what he is given. So whenever he sees foolishness in something, it is either that he has been given the attribute of knowledge of the true wisdom in that or not, or his knowledge has reached what perceives its wisdom or not, or something from the old state remaining in him prevents him from encompassing that. Therefore, the claim of the servant regarding God's action that this is not wisdom or such is invalid. What clarifies this is his knowledge of his ignorance of most things, his knowledge of his need and incapacity in most matters, and his awareness of his foolishness in most cases. And from this description of himself, his delving into what God does—by way of pointing to it without adhering to the general statement—and not [something] in which the rational have participated, for that is the true work of reason in general, and he has been given all that—is nonsense with no meaning. And for what we have explained, God said: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned' (21:23), for the action of every person may involve foolishness and wisdom, but His action is exalted above foolishness; and every person has command and prohibition since he is in reality for another, and God is exalted above that; and because each only possesses a portion of things and a part, while God possesses them entirely, and similar things that render meaningless the questioning of the Lord. And if that is impossible, then answering about it is unnecessary.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a proof that humans cannot judge God's actions. Maturidi argues that any human sage is susceptible to foolishness, unlike God whose perfect attributes preclude it. He then explains that when a person sees something as foolish, it may be due to their ignorance or incapacity, not an actual defect in the action. The Quranic verse 'He is not questioned' confirms this, as only those who are fallible are questioned. God's complete ownership and humans' partial possession further negate the basis for questioning Him.

Key Terms
الحكيم (sage)
السفه (foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can rationally evaluate God's actions using human standards of wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human standards are tainted by susceptibility to foolishness, so such evaluation is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is a human's claim that an act of God lacks wisdom invalid?
A) Because humans cannot understand God's actions at all.
B) Because humans are inherently incapable of discerning foolishness in others.
C) Because a human's own susceptibility to foolishness and limited knowledge prevents him from being sure.
D) Because God's actions are always secret.

Key Takeaway
Human fallibility means we cannot use our limited perspective to judge the wisdom of God's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last felt that something in your life was unfair or meaningless, did you pause to consider that your own understanding might be limited by foolishness? How can this lesson change your reaction next time?
?
Think of a person whose judgment you dismissed as foolish. According to this lesson, are you not also susceptible to the same flaw? What step can you take today to humbly acknowledge your own limitations?
?
If you truly believe that God's actions are always wise, why do you still struggle to accept trials with contentment? What specific part of this lesson can you recite to yourself when doubt creeps in?

The Divine Imperative: 'He Is Not Questioned'
Maturidi cites Quran 21:23 and provides rational grounds for why God cannot be questioned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for why God is not questioned about His actions and how it refutes human judgment.

Core Idea
God's actions are inherently wise; questioning Him is meaningless due to His perfection and sovereignty.

After establishing the rational argument, Maturidi turns to scriptural proof. He cites the Quranic verse 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned' (21:23) to emphasize that because God's actions are free from foolishness and He is the absolute owner, there is no basis for questioning Him. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Read the Arabic verse and understand its placement in the passage.
The verse is central to the argument.
2
Analyze the comparison
Maturidi contrasts human actions, which can be foolish or wise, with God's actions which are exalted above foolishness.
3
Understand the basis for questioning
Maturidi explains that humans are subject to command and prohibition because they act for others, but God is above that. Also, humans own only a portion, while God owns everything.
This renders questioning meaningless.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since questioning is impossible, answering is unnecessary.

The Purpose of Creation: Trial and the Two Commands
Maturidi explains the purpose of creation and the two kinds of commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's explanation of why God created humans with discernment and imposed both hard and easy commands as a trial.

Core Idea
God created humans to be tested through commands suited to their nature.

Imam Maturidi begins his theological exposition by explaining the divine purpose behind human creation. He argues that God created humans for a trial (محنة), giving them the ability to discern good and evil. This passage lays the foundation for understanding why God's commands come in two forms: arduous and easy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Notice the phrase 'أما بعد' which signals the start of the main discourse.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms 'محنة' (trial), 'تمييز' (discernment), 'عقل' (intellect), and the two types of commands 'عسير' (arduous) and 'يسير' (easy). Consider how they relate to each other.
Maturidi emphasizes that trial is intrinsic to human nature.
3
Understand the role of intellect
Note how Maturidi describes God making some things 'ugly' and others 'beautiful' in the intellect, and how this leads to a struggle between inclination and reason.
Intellect is guided by outcomes (praiseworthy or blameworthy consequences).
4
Reflect on the two kinds of commands
The passage concludes that the trial consists of both arduous and easy commands. Ask yourself why both are necessary and how this relates to human nature.
Without trial, humans would undertake both equally; the diversity tests their choices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بعد، فإن الله تعالى لما خلق البشر للمحنة بما جعلهم أهل تمييز وعلم بالمحمود من الأمور والمذموم، وجعل ما يذم منها قبيحا في عقولهم، وما يحمد حسنا، وعظم في أذهانهم إيثار القبيح على الحسن والرغبة فيما يذم على ما يحمد، دعاهم - على ما عليه ركبوا وما به أكرموا - إلى إيثار أمر على أمر، وقبح في عقولهم احتمال أمثالهم [غيره]. جعل الله جميع ما لهم فيه متقلب بين ضرر يتقى ونفع يرغب فيه، ليكون ذلك لهم علما للموعود مما به الترغيب والترهيب وأنشأهم على طبائع تنفر عن أشياء وتميل إلى أشياء، وأراهم في عقولهم حسن بعض ما تنفر عنه الطباع بحمد العواقب، وقبح بعض ما تميل إليه بذم العواقب فصيرهم بحيث يحتملون المكروه على الطباع بلذيذ العاقبة، ويقهرونه عما يدعوهم إليه بشهى النهاية. ثم امتحنهم إذ أبت عقولهم احتمال أمثالها، ورغب في محاسن الأعمال ومكارم الأخلاق باختيار ما حسن من الأعمال واجتناب ما قبح من ذلك. ثم جعل ما فيه محنهم أمرين: العسير واليسير، والسهل والصعب؛ إذ هم بلا محنة يتعاطون الأمرين جميعا، لما إليه مرجع ما أقدموا عليه وامتنعوا.
To proceed: God Most High, when He created humans for trial, made them people of discernment and knowledge of praiseworthy and blameworthy matters. He made what is blameworthy ugly in their intellects, and what is praiseworthy beautiful, and He magnified in their minds the preference for the ugly over the beautiful and desire for what is blameworthy over what is praiseworthy. He called them – upon what they were constituted and with what they were honored – to prefer one matter over another, and He made ugly in their intellects the endurance of their likes [others]. God made all that in which they have fluctuation between harm to be avoided and benefit to be desired, so that this would be for them knowledge of what is promised regarding encouragement and intimidation. And He created them with natures that recoil from some things and incline to others, and He showed them in their intellects the beauty of some things that nature recoils from due to praiseworthy outcomes, and the ugliness of some things they incline to due to blameworthy outcomes. So He made them such that they endure what is disliked by nature for the pleasure of the outcome, and they overcome it from what calls them to it through the desire for the end. Then He tested them, since their intellects refused to endure their likes, and He made them desire the beauties of deeds and noble character by choosing what is beautiful among deeds and avoiding what is ugly thereof. Then He made what contains their trial two things: the arduous and the easy, the simple and the difficult; because without trial they would undertake both matters equally, given that the reference of what they undertake and abstain from is to it.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation. It begins with 'أما بعد' indicating the start of the main discourse. God created humans for trial (محنة) by giving them discernment (تمييز) and knowledge of praiseworthy and blameworthy things. He made blameworthy things ugly in their intellects and praiseworthy things beautiful, but He also made the inclination towards the ugly strong in their minds. Thus, He called them to prefer one thing over another, and made the endurance of similar things ugly in their intellects. He made all their circumstances oscillate between harmful things to avoid and beneficial things to desire, so that they would have knowledge of promised things. He created them with natures that dislike some things and incline to others, and showed them in their intellects the beauty of some disliked things due to good outcomes, and the ugliness of some liked things due to bad outcomes. Thus, they endure disliked things for pleasant outcomes. Then He tested them when their intellects refused to endure similar things, and made them desire good deeds. Then He made their trial consist of two things: the arduous and the easy, the simple and the difficult, because without trial they would engage in both types equally. So the trial lies in the diversity of commands.

Key Terms
المحنة
التمييز
العقل
الحسن
القبيح
العسير
اليسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's commands are arbitrary.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that commands are based on human nature and intellect; they are designed for trial and suited to human capabilities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did God make the trial consist of both arduous and easy commands?
A) Because without trial, people would equally engage in both
B) To make life difficult
C) To distinguish the believers
D) To show His power

Key Takeaway
God created humans with intellect and discernment, and tests them through both difficult and easy commands to guide their choices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an easy command, do you comply out of laziness or conscious obedience? What does your response reveal about your acceptance of the trial?
?
The passage says God magnified the desire for the ugly in our minds. Which ugly inclination do you still prefer, knowing its blameworthiness? How will you fight it today, starting now?
?
Are you more willing to obey God when the command is easy, and resentful when it is difficult? How does this lesson challenge your assumption that only hard commands are tests, and what will you change?

Divine Attributes

Deficient Power and Divine Transcendence
Maturidi argues against deficient power in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God must have power over all actions of His servants, and how this relates to trust in His promise and threat.

Core Idea
God's power cannot be like that of creatures; if it were, faith in divine justice would collapse.

Maturidi begins by defining 'deficient power' as the limited power characteristic of created beings. He then argues that attributing such deficiency to God would not only contradict His transcendence but also undermine the very foundation of trust in His promises and threats. This lesson unpacks his logical move from divine power to the necessity of God's complete authority over all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation side by side. Notice how Maturidi defines 'deficient power' and then applies it to God hypothetically.
The phrase 'على ما ليس يفعل لغيره' may seem ambiguous; try to grasp the meaning: each creature's power is over what its owner does not do for another.
2
Consider the hypothetical conclusion
Maturidi says: if God lacked power over His servant's actions, His power would be like that of every deficient being. Why does he draw this conclusion?
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage then asks: if actions could be outside God's power, how can one trust His promise and threat? Think about the link between omnipotence and divine justice.
This question shows the practical consequence of denying God's complete power.
4
Formulate the key argument
Summarize in your own words: God must have power over all human actions, otherwise His power would resemble creatures and divine justice would be uncertain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن القدرة الناقصة هي التي تكون لكل أحد من الخلق، ولكلّ قدرة على ما ليس يفعل لغيره؛ فإذا لم يكن الله قدرة على ما لعبده، فإذا قدرته نحو قدرة كل منقوص. جل الله عن صفة المخلوق. وبالله التوفيق. وأيضاً إنه لو جاز خروج شيء هو تحت القدرة عن أن يكون الله عليه قدرة - بل ليس هو شيئًا واحدا بل لعله أكثر من جميع الخلق - كيف يؤمن بوعده ووعيده، وكيف يطمئن؟
Concerning the deficient power: it is that which belongs to every one of the creation, and every power is over what it does not do for another. So if God did not have power over what belongs to His servant, then His power would be like the power of every deficient being. Exalted is God above the attribute of the creature. And with God is success. And also, if it were permissible for something that is under power to exit from being something over which God has power—but it is not one thing, rather perhaps it is more than all creation—how can one believe in His promise and threat, and how can one be assured?
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of deficient power (القدرة الناقصة). Maturidi defines it as the power characteristic of created beings: each creature has power over only what its owner does not do for another (meaning each being's power is limited to its own acts and does not extend to others). If God, on the other hand, lacked power over the actions of His servants, then His power would resemble the deficient power of creatures. That is impossible, so God must have power over everything, including human actions. Then Maturidi raises a rhetorical question: if it is possible for something that is under God's power (like human actions) to be outside His power, and note that these actions are not just one thing but perhaps more numerous than all creation, then how can one trust in God's promise (وعده) and threat (وعيده)? If God is not fully powerful over actions, then His promises of reward and punishment might not be fulfilled, leading to doubt. Thus, attributing independent power to humans undermines confidence in divine justice.

Key Terms
قدرة ناقصة
منقوص
صفة المخلوق
وعد
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can be limited to some things without affecting His divinity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if God lacked power over any action, His power would become like the deficient power of creatures, which is incompatible with His transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if God did not have power over His servants' actions?
A) God's power would be like the deficient power of creatures.
B) Humans would become gods.
C) God would still be all-powerful.
D) It would be impossible to conceive.

Key Takeaway
God must have power over all human actions; otherwise, His power would be deficient and trust in His promise and threat would be lost.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what area of your life have you been acting as though your choices are independent of Allah's power? Commit today to consciously affirm that Allah has power over that area, and ask Him to guide your actions.

God's Power Must Be Absolute Over All Actions
Maturidi defines imperfect power and argues that if God lacks control over human actions, His power would be deficient, undermining trust in divine promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if God lacked power over human actions, His power would be imperfect, and why this undermines trust in divine promises.

Core Idea
God's power is not like creature's power—it must be absolute over all actions.

Maturidi presents a concise argument defending God's absolute power over all actions, including human choices. He begins by defining imperfect power as that which belongs to creatures, then applies this to God to show that any limitation would reduce His power to the level of creation. This argument not only defends God's attribute of omnipotence but also grounds the believer's trust in His promises and threats. The passage then extends the logic to show the absurdity of saying God cannot even do a mosquito's act, emphasizing that all power is from Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of rhetorical questions towards the end.
2
Identify the definition of imperfect power
Maturidi begins by defining imperfect power. Find the phrase that describes who possesses such power and what it means.
The definition sets up the contrast with God's power.
3
Apply the definition to God
Consider what would follow if God lacked power over human actions. How would that affect His power?
Maturidi says His power would be 'like the power of every deficient one.'
4
Consider the consequences for belief
The passage asks how one can believe in God's promise and threat if His power is limited. Reflect on why trust in resurrection depends on God's omnipotence.
The logical link: limited power makes promises unreliable.
5
Reflect on the mosquito comparison
Maturidi contrasts God's ability to create all things with the inability to do a mosquito's act. How does this illustrate the absurdity of limiting God's power?
This reinforces the point that all power is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن القدرة الناقصة هي التي تكون لكل أحد من الخلق، ولكلّ قدرة على ما ليس يفعل لغيره ؛ فإذا لم يكن الله قدرة على ما لعبده ، فإذا قدرته نحو قدرة كل منقوص . جل الله عن صفة المخلوق. وبالله التوفيق. وأيضاً إنه لو جاز خروج شيء " هو تحت القدرة عن أن يكون الله عليه قدرة - بل ليس هو شيئًا واحدا بل لعله أكثر من جميع الخلق - كيف يؤمن بوعده ووعيده، وكيف يطمئن السامع إلى ما وعده من البعث أن يكون، وما أخبر أنه لو شاء لخلق مثل الذي خلق، وهو لا يقدر على فعل بَعُوض، فضلاً عن فعل ما هو أقوى منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
However, imperfect power is that which belongs to every one of the creation, and every power is over what it does not do for another; so if God has no power over what belongs to His servant, then His power is like the power of every deficient one. Exalted is God above the attribute of the created. And with God is success. And also, if it were permissible that something which is under power should come out from being under God's power — rather it is not one thing but perhaps more than all creation — how can one believe in His promise and threat, and how can the listener be reassured about what He promised of resurrection that it will be, and what He informed that if He willed He would create the like of what He created, while He is not able to do the act of a mosquito, let alone what is stronger than it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the nature of perfect power. Maturidi begins by defining imperfect power as that which belongs to creatures: it is limited and cannot produce acts for others. If God, therefore, lacked power over human actions, His power would resemble that of a deficient creature. The author then extends the argument: if any action were outside God's power, it would be independent, and that would undermine trust in God's promises and threats (e.g., resurrection). God's ability to create like things is contrasted with the inability to create a single mosquito, illustrating the absurdity of attributing limited power to God. The passage ends with a declaration that all power is from God.

منقوص (deficient)
وعد (promise)

Correction: Maturidi argues that such limitation would make God's power like that of a deficient creature, contradicting His perfection.

C) Absolute power

God's Ownership Necessitates Creation of Actions
Maturidi argues that God's absolute ownership necessitates creation of all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's absolute ownership as Creator implies that He creates all human actions.

Core Idea
To own is to create—God's ownership is not like ours.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam Maturidi. He starts from a simple premise: God is the owner of everything. But what does ownership mean for God? Maturidi distinguishes God's ownership from human ownership and draws a powerful conclusion about the nature of human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions carefully. Let the author's own phrasing sink in before analysing.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ملك' (ownership) and how it is used.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi contrasts God's ownership with human ownership. God is owner by essence as Creator; humans own derivatively. Find this distinction in the text.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The argument assumes God does not own actions, then shows this leads to absurd consequences, proving the original assumption false. Trace each step in the commentary.
The conclusion is that creation of actions necessarily follows from ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله إذ هو مالك كل شيء، وملكه الأشياء ليس بما أُوجب له فيه الملك كملك العبد ، بل هو بذاته مالك بما هو خالق كل شيء. فأما أن يكون غير مالك لفعل العباد ولا ربا لها فيجب به أن يكون للعباد ذلك، فيكون ربوبيته وملكه ملكاً ناقصا ، وذلك لكل مخلوق، [ فهو ] يملك أشياء بل هو أكثر ، لأنه يملك فعله وفعل غيره والله لا [ يملك ] . وإذا ثبت له الملك في كل شيء لزم القول بخلقه ؛ إذ لم يملكه العبد، وتملك الأشياء بالقدرة عليها أو بتمليك من له تلك. له تلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, since God is the owner of everything, and His ownership of things is not due to something that necessitated ownership for Him like the ownership of the servant, rather He is Owner by His Essence because He is the Creator of everything. So if He were not the owner of the actions of servants and had no lordship over them, then it would necessarily follow that servants own that [action], and then His lordship and ownership would be deficient ownership, and that belongs to every creature. [So] he owns things, even more, because he owns his own act and the act of another, while God does not [own]. And if ownership of everything is established for Him, then the statement of His creation [of actions] necessarily follows, since the servant does not own it, and things are owned by power over them or by grant from the one who has that. He has that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents an argument based on God's absolute ownership (mulk). Maturidi distinguishes God's ownership from that of servants: God is owner by His Essence as Creator, while servants own things derivatively. If God did not own human actions, then servants would own them, implying two lordships. This would reduce God's ownership to a deficiency, as even a creature can own his own act and that of another (e.g., a master's ownership of a slave's acts). Since ownership is either by power or by grant, and God's ownership is established over all things, human actions must be created by God. The passage concludes with the refrain that all power is from God.

Key Terms
مالك (owner)
ملك (ownership/lordship)
ربوبية (lordship)
خالق (creator)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ownership is merely a legal or conventional concept.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's ownership is ontological: to own something ultimately means to have created it, for true ownership is by power over the thing.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi characterize God's ownership compared to that of a servant?
A) God's ownership is granted by others
B) God is owner by His Essence as Creator
C) Both are the same
D) God's ownership is lesser

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership, rooted in His being Creator, requires that He creates all actions, for otherwise His lordship would be deficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' In what specific area of your life today have you been relying on your own power instead of His? What is one concrete step you can take within the next hour to acknowledge His ownership in that area?

From Divine Ownership to Creation of Actions
Maturidi argues from God's absolute ownership to the creation of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the concept of God's absolute ownership to prove that God creates human actions, and why this argument refutes the idea of independent human agency.

Core Idea
God's essential ownership of everything entails His creation of all actions.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument from divine ownership to the creation of human actions. He distinguishes God's ownership from human ownership and shows that denying God's ownership of actions would imply deficiency in His lordship. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the repetition of 'لا قوة إلا بالله' as a recurring theme.
2
Identify the key premise
Maturidi begins by asserting God's absolute ownership (ملك كل شيء) and distinguishes it from human ownership.
God's ownership is by essence (بذاته), not acquired.
3
Understand the hypothetical consequence
If God did not own human actions, then servants would own them, making God's lordship deficient.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Trace the conclusion from ownership to creation
Since God owns everything, including actions, He must be their creator. Ownership implies either power or transfer, and God has power.
5
Examine the enabling (iqdar) argument
Human ability comes from God's enabling; thus, God must have power over what He enables, leading to the conclusion that He creates actions.
This parallels the argument from knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السامع إلى ما وعده من البعث أن يكون، وهو لا يقدر على فعل بَعُوض، فضلاً عن فعل ما هو أقوى منه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن الله إذ هو مالك كل شيء، وملكه الأشياء ليس بما أُوجب له فيه الملك كملك العبد، بل هو بذاته مالك بما هو خالق كل شيء. فأما أن يكون غير مالك لفعل العباد ولا ربا لها فيجب به أن يكون للعباد ذلك، فيكون ربوبيته وملكه ملكاً ناقصا، وذلك لكل مخلوق، فهو يملك أشياء بل هو أكثر، لأنه يملك فعله وفعل غيره والله لا يملك. وإذا ثبت له الملك في كل شيء لزم القول بخلقه؛ إذ لم يملكه العبد، وتملك الأشياء بالقدرة عليها أو بتمليك من له تلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن العبد يقدر بإقدار الله إياه، فلا يجوز أن يقدر بإقدار من ليست له القدرة عليه، كما لا يجوز أن يعلم بإعلام من لا علم له به. أو لا يُرى أنه إذا لم يجز لأحد القدرة على إقدار غيره على شيء إذا لم يقدر هو عليه، ومن له علم يُعلم به غيره لم يجز أن لا يعلم هو، فمثله الذي بينا. وإذا ثبت قدرة الله عليه، وما يقدر الله عليه فهو محال وجوده بغيره، ثبت أنه خالق ذلك.
The listener to what He promised of resurrection that it will be, but he is not able to do a mosquito, much less doing what is stronger than it. And there is no power except with Allah. And also, indeed Allah when He is the owner of everything, and His ownership of things is not by something that necessitated ownership for Him like the ownership of the slave, but He is by essence owner as He is the creator of everything. So if He were not owner of the action of the servants nor lord over them, it would necessitate that the servants would have that, so His lordship and His ownership would be deficient ownership, and that is for every creature, so He owns things but rather He is more, because He owns His action and the action of others, and Allah does not own. And if ownership of everything is established for Him, the statement of His creation is necessary, since the servant does not own it, and owning things is by power over them or by ownership transfer from the one who has that. And there is no power except with Allah. And also, indeed the servant is able by Allah's enabling him, so it is not permissible that he be able by the enabling of one who does not have power over him, just as it is not permissible that he know by the teaching of one who has no knowledge of it. Or is it not seen that when it is not permissible for anyone to have power to enable another over something if he himself does not have power over it, and whoever has knowledge by which another is taught it is not permissible that he not know it, so its like is what we explained. And when the power of Allah over it is established, and what Allah is able over it is impossible in its existence by other than Him, it is established that He is the creator of that.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from divine ownership. It begins by noting that the servant is unable to create even a mosquito, so all power belongs to God. Maturidi then distinguishes God's ownership from that of a slave: God is owner by essence (bi-dhatihi) as Creator, not by acquisition. If God were not owner of actions, servants would be independent, making divine ownership deficient. Since God owns everything, including actions, He must be their creator. The servant's ability comes from God's enabling (iqdar), so it is impossible for the servant to act independently. The argument concludes that whatever God has power over cannot exist by another, hence God creates actions.

ملك
ربوبية
خلق الأفعال
إقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's ownership is like human ownership, acquired or limited.
Correction: Maturidi insists God's ownership is essential (بذاته) as Creator, not acquired, so it cannot be deficient.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's absolute ownership of everything, including human actions, entails that He is their sole creator, and human ability is entirely dependent on His enabling.

The Consequence of Denying Divine Creation of Actions
Maturidi presents a reductio ad absurdum on divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that denying God's creation of human actions leads to the absurdity of God needing assistance, which contradicts His wisdom and power.

Core Idea
If God didn't create actions, He'd need help — an impossibility for the Almighty.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum to defend the doctrine that God creates all actions, including human ones. He argues that if God were not the creator of our actions, then the very proofs He sent through prophets and the entire management of the universe would collapse, depending on creatures for completion. This would imply that God is not truly wise or powerful, which is impossible. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic original and its English translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if He were not creator... then...'
2
Identify the hypothetical and its consequence
Notice that the passage starts with a supposition (if God did not create actions) and then lists what would be invalid: proofs, management. Ask yourself: why would these be invalid?
3
Understand the role of creatures' help
The argument says that if God didn't create actions, His plans (permanence, non-existence, progeny) would depend on creatures' help. That would make God need assistance, which is incompatible with wisdom and power.
This is the core absurdity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that all proofs appear through God's creation on whom He wills. So the only consistent position is that God creates all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنه لو لم يكن خالقا لأفعال الخلق لكان عامة حججه التي أظهرها على أيدى رسله والتدبير الذي جرى عليه من أمر عالمه من أول ما أنشأ خلقه إلى آخر ما ينتهي إليه أمره منتقضاً فاسدا، لولا مساعدة خلقه له فيما دبر من البقاء وفيما جعل من العدم وفيها أنشأ من النسل، إن ذلك كله مما ظهر بأفعال خلقه وتم به وليس بحكيم ولا قادر من أراد أن يظهر حجة لا يقدر عليها إلا بالمعونة: بعلم غيره وفعله، بل هو جاهل عاجز. فثبت أنها كلها ظهرت بما خلقها على يدي من شاء كيف شاء على ما شاء، جل ثناؤه.
And also, if He were not creator of the actions of the creation, then most of His proofs which He manifested through His messengers and the management which proceeded from His command of His world from the beginning of His creation to the end of its affair would be invalid and corrupt, if not for the help of His creation to Him in what He planned of permanence and in what He made of non-existence and in what He originated of progeny, indeed all that is what appeared through the actions of His creation and was completed by it. And he is not wise nor powerful who intends to manifest a proof he is not able to do except with assistance: by another's knowledge and action, rather he is ignorant and incapable. So it is established that all of them appeared by His creating them on the hands of whom He willed, how He willed, as He willed, exalted is His praise.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum. Maturidi argues that if God did not create human actions, then His proofs through the prophets and His management of the universe would be invalid and corrupt, because they would depend on the help of creatures. For example, the perpetuation of the world and the continuation of progeny occur through human actions. If those actions are not created by God, then God would need assistance, which is incompatible with being wise and powerful. Therefore, God must be the creator of all actions that manifest His proofs.

Key Terms

تدبير
حكيم
قادر
معونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can be the creator of some actions but not others, or that humans independently create their own actions without divine involvement.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if any action is not created by God, then God's proofs and management would be dependent on creatures, which is incompatible with His wisdom and power. Hence, all actions must be created by God.

Checkpoint

A) His proofs and management would be invalid and depend on creatures.
B) Human actions would be self-generated and independent.
C) The prophets would be unnecessary.
D) God would still be all-wise and all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God creates all actions leads to the absurd conclusion that God needs help, contradicting His perfect wisdom and power.

?

Proof That Allah Creates All Actions
Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove Allah creates all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that Allah must create all actions of creatures, using a reductio ad absurdum based on the perfection of divine wisdom and power.

Core Idea
If Allah needed creation's help, He would be imperfect—impossible, so He creates all actions.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument that if Allah were not the creator of all actions, then His entire plan for creation would be flawed, dependent on His creatures. But such dependence would contradict His wisdom and power. Let's examine this logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
The Arabic is concise; take time with it.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if' statement: 'if He were not the creator of the actions of creatures...' This is the starting point of the reductio.
3
Follow the reductio: what would follow?
Maturidi then concludes that the proofs and planning would be invalid and corrupt, because they would depend on the assistance of creatures. This would imply Allah is ignorant and incapable.
This step shows the absurd consequence.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the consequence is impossible for Allah, the original premise is false. Therefore, Allah does create all actions. The passage ends by affirming that all actions appear by His creation.
The conclusion is positive and decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنه لو لم يكن خالقا لأفعال الخلق لكان عامة حججه التي أظهرها على أيدى رسله والتدبير الذي جرى عليه من أمر عالمه من أول ما أنشأ خلقه إلى آخر ما ينتهي إليه أمره منتقضاً فاسدا ، لولا مساعدة خلقه له فيما دبر من البقاء وفيما جعل من العدم [و] فيها أنشأ من النسل، إن ذلك كله مما ظهر بأفعال خلقه وتم به وليس بحكيم ولا قادر من أراد أن يظهر حجة لا يقدر عليها إلا بالمعونة : بعلم غيره وفعله ، بل هو جاهل عاجز. فثبت أنها كلها ظهرت بما خلقها على يدي من شاء كيف شاء على ما شاء، جل ثناؤه.
And also, if He were not the creator of the actions of creatures, then the majority of His proofs which He manifested through the hands of His messengers, and the planning which proceeded from Him concerning the affair of His world from the first of what He originated His creation to the last of what His affair ends to, would be invalid and corrupt—were it not for the assistance of His creation to Him in what He planned of permanence and in what He made of non-existence and [what] He originated of progeny. Indeed, all that is something which appeared through the actions of His creation and was completed by it. And He is not wise nor powerful who intends to manifest a proof which he cannot accomplish except with assistance: through the knowledge and action of another. Rather, he is ignorant and incapable. So it is established that all of them appeared by His creating them at the hands of whomever He willed, however He willed, as He willed—exalted is His praise.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that Allah is the creator of all actions of His creatures, using a reductio ad absurdum. Maturidi states the premise: if Allah were NOT the creator of the actions of creatures, then most of the proofs He manifested through His messengers, and the overall planning of His world from beginning to end, would be invalid and corrupt. The reason is that they would depend on the assistance of creation for what He planned (e.g., permanence, bringing into existence progeny). All these events are realized through the actions of creatures; if those actions are not created by Allah, then He would need the help of His creatures to accomplish His plans. But needing assistance for a proof or plan implies that He is not truly wise or powerful—rather, He would be ignorant and incapable. Since this is impossible for Allah (being ignorant and incapable), the original premise is false: Allah must create all actions. The conclusion: it is established that all these events appeared through His creating them at the hands of whomever He willed, however He willed, as He willed. Thus, the argument preserves divine wisdom and power.

Key Terms
خالق أفعال الخلق
حجج
تدبير
جاهل عاجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human free will requires that humans independently create their own actions.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if humans independently created actions, then Allah's plans would depend on them, contradicting His perfection. Thus, Allah creates all actions, including human actions, without negating human responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if Allah were not the creator of actions of creatures?
A) Creatures would be independent.
B) Allah's proofs and planning would be invalid and corrupt.
C) Prophets would not exist.
D) The world would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Allah's creation of all actions is necessary for His perfection in wisdom and power; any dependence on creation would imply deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If Allah creates all actions, including your sins, how does that affect your view of repentance and responsibility? How can you balance accountability with reliance on Allah in your daily life starting today?

Refuting the Objection of Evil and Foulness
Maturidi refutes the objection that God cannot create evil by analogy with physical impurities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's creation of morally evil acts is analogous to His creation of physical impurities, and why this refutes the Mu'tazila's objection.

Core Idea
God creating evil acts does not make Him evil, just as He creates filth without being filthy.

One of the strongest objections against divine creation of all actions is that God would then be the author of evil. Maturidi responds with a powerful analogy: if we accept that God creates physically foul substances like filth and stench without impairing His perfection, then we can accept that He creates morally foul acts without impairing His justice. This passage draws that parallel and undermines the Mu'tazila's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Maturidi's argument structure. Note the key terms: قبائح, فواحش, ربوبية.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The passage begins with 'ومما يقولون...' – identify what the Mu'tazila claim about attributing evil acts to God.
They claim it is ugly (قبيح) to ascribe such acts to God.
3
Understand the analogy with physical impurities
Maturidi compares the Mu'tazila's objection to a similar objection by dualists about physical foulness. See how he uses this comparison to show the argument fails.
The dualists also admit God creates filth but still affirm His lordship.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The final lines state that attributing evil acts to God does not negate His lordship. Ask yourself: what is the key distinction Maturidi is making?
He distinguishes between the act being ugly for the servant and the creation of that act being ugly for God.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument preserves God's sovereignty over all things while keeping moral responsibility with the servant. How does this fit into the broader Maturidi framework?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يقولون في فعل العباد مما فيه قبح الإضافة إلى الله تعالى في خلق ذلك من أن فيها فواحش ومناكير ونحو ذلك، فيه مثل ذلك للثنوية والمجوس في الجواهر: أن فيها قبائح وخبائث وأقذارا وأنتانا. ومع ما إضافة تلك الأشياء إلى الله فليست هي ـ عند التفسير بأن الله تعالى خلقها قبائح وفواحش من مرتكبيها، مخالفة للمحاسن والمصالح من أفعالهم ـ بأقبح ممن يقول: هو رب الأقذار وإله الخزى والنكال، وملك الشياطين والفجار. ثم لم يمنع هذا القول بتحقيق الربوبية له على كل شيء والإلهية، وإن كان على التفسير في الإضافة من الوجه الذي بينا قبيحا سمجا، فمثله جميع ما عليه وصف أفعال الخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And among what they say concerning the acts of servants, regarding the ugliness of attributing to God, may He be exalted, the creation of that which contains lewd acts and abominations and the like: there is the like of that for the dualists and Magians concerning [physical] substances: that they contain ugly things, filthy things, impurities, and stinks. And with the attribution of those things to God, it is not — on the interpretation that God, may He be exalted, created them as ugly acts and lewd acts from those who commit them, contrary to the good and beneficial things from their acts — uglier than the one who says: He is the Lord of filth and the God of disgrace and punishment, and the King of devils and wicked people. Then this statement is not prevented by the affirmation of lordship over everything and divinity, even if on the interpretation of attribution in the way we have explained it is ugly and foul. So the like of it is all that is described of the acts of creation.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage refutes the Muʿtazila's objection that attributing evil acts to God is ugly. Maturidi draws an analogy with physical impurities: the dualists and Magians also have to admit that God creates foul substances like filth and stench. Yet they still affirm God as Lord of everything. If it is not invalid for God to create physical foulness, then similarly, it is not invalid for God to create morally evil acts committed by people. The objection based on the ugliness of the act does not negate God's sovereignty, because the creation of the act is separate from the moral quality of the act. The act may be ugly from the servant's perspective, but that does not mean God's creation of it is ugly. Thus, the Muʿtazila's argument fails.

Key Terms
قبح
فواحش
قبائح
ربوبية
إلهية
أفعال الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is indifferent to evil and considers God the author of sin.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between God's creation of the act and the moral responsibility of the servant. The act is ugly for the servant, but God's creation of it is not ugly because He creates with wisdom and justice.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to refute the Mu'tazila's objection about God creating evil?
A) The dualists' belief in two creators
B) The existence of physical impurities like filth and stench created by God
C) The fact that God commands and forbids
D) The servant's power to choose

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defends God's creation of all acts, including evil, by showing that if physical foulness does not diminish God's perfection, neither does the creation of moral foulness.

Command and Prohibition Are Not Derived from Action's Attributes
Maturidi argues against basing command/prohibition on action attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that divine command and prohibition are not determined by the inherent attributes of actions, and that the creation of an action does not automatically attach moral or legal rulings to it.

Core Idea
Actions are created; command and prohibition come from a different consideration.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a subtle theological question: Are God's commands and prohibitions based on the intrinsic qualities of actions themselves? He argues that they are not. Instead, he shows that many attributes of actions, such as being larger or smaller, are morally neutral, and that creation itself does not imply a command. This lesson unpacks his dialectical reasoning and its implications for understanding divine justice and the nature of moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the dialectical structure: the author poses a question and then refutes a possible answer.
The passage uses conditional reasoning ('If he says yes...').
2
Identify the core argument
Maturidi argues that command and prohibition cannot be based on the aspects (wujuh) of an action, because those aspects are not inherently moral. Look for the example of disbelief (kufr) and the attributes of relations (awṣāf al-iḍāfāt).
3
Understand the refutation
If command/prohibition were based on an action's attributes, then for an act like kufr, one of its aspects (evidence of foolishness) is true, so forbidding it from that aspect would be impossible. Also, many people are ignorant of those attributes, so command/prohibition would not be universal.
4
Grasp the positive position
Maturidi states that an action can be created (khulq) without God commanding its creation. Relational attributes like larger/smaller are not described as good/evil, so the act itself is morally neutral in terms of its creation.
5
Apply to threat and promise
The same principle applies to reward and punishment: once the reality of the action is established, command/prohibition follow, and similarly reward/punishment follow. The act's creation does not determine these rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بد من بلى، لما فيهما هذه الوجوه كلها. فيقال: هل الأمر والنهي بالفعل موجبا الأمر والنهي بهذه الوجوه التي في فعله ذلك ؟ فإن قال : نعم، أحال لما في كفره دليل سفهه، وهو من حيث الدلالة صدق، ومحال النهي عنه من ذلك الوجه ، ولأن كثيرا منهم لا يعرفون تلك الصفات له ، لم يجز الأمر لذلك من ذلك الوجه ولا النهي فلا بد من المساعدة لهم في ذلك. فيقال له : ما منع أن يكون ذلك خُلق، وليس في ذلك أمر لنفسه بالخلق ولا نهى ؟ ثم استقام في العقل الجهات التي بينا . مع ما أوصاف الإضافات أن ذا أصغر من ذا وأكبر، وأخير وأشر، وأقبح وأحسن من ذلك وأعظم في الحجة وأوضع، وأضعف وأقوى، وأنه حدث وموجود، وغير ذلك مما يكثر وصفه، و مع ذلك لا يوصف شيء من ذلك بالشر والخير من جميع الوجوه ولا بالطاعة والمعصية، فجائز خلقها، ولا يوصف من ذلك الوجه بطاعة ولا معصية، ولا خير ولا شر، ولا أمر ولا نهى، ولا شيء مما له الفعل .. والله الموفق. وعلى مثل ذلك أمر الوعيد والوعد إنا حققنا الفعل، فلزم فيه الأمر والنهي، فمثله يلزم فيه الثواب والعقاب.
There is no escape from yes, because these all aspects are in them. So it is said: Are command and prohibition of the action the cause of command and prohibition by these aspects that are in that action? If he says: Yes, he would consider impossible because in his disbelief there is evidence of his foolishness, and it is from the perspective of evidence true, and it is impossible to forbid it from that aspect, and because many of them do not know those attributes for it, then command is not permissible from that aspect nor prohibition, so there must be assistance for them in that. So it is said to him: What prevents that it be created, and there is in that no command for itself to create nor prohibition? Then the aspects we explained become established in reason. Along with the attributes of relations that this is smaller than that and larger, and better and worse, and uglier and more beautiful than that, and greater in proof and clearer, and weaker and stronger, and that it is originated and existent, and other such things that are frequently described, and with that nothing of that is described as evil or good from all aspects, nor as obedience or disobedience, so its creation is permissible, and it is not described from that aspect as obedience or disobedience, nor good or evil, nor command or prohibition, nor anything of what belongs to the act.. And God is the grantor of success. And similar to that is the matter of threat and promise: we have realized the act, so command and prohibition become necessary in it, so similarly reward and punishment become necessary in it.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage expands on a kalam argument about the relationship between God's command/prohibition and the attributes of actions. The author begins by stating that there is no escape from affirming that actions have various aspects. He then poses a question: Does the command or prohibition of an action arise from those specific aspects (e.g., the act being good or bad in itself)? If the interlocutor answers yes, then the author argues that this leads to absurdity because, for example, an act of disbelief (kufr) contains evidence of the agent's foolishness, and that aspect is true, so one could not forbid it from that aspect, and many people do not recognize those attributes. Therefore, command and prohibition cannot be based on such aspects. The author then offers his own position: there is no objection to calling the action 'created' (khulq) without implying a command or prohibition regarding its creation. He goes on to explain that in reason, there are many relational attributes (awṣāf al-iḍāfāt) such as larger/smaller, better/worse, etc., and yet these attributes are not described as good or evil, nor as obedience or disobedience. Thus, it is permissible to attribute the creation of such acts to God without them being subject to moral or legal judgment from that perspective. Finally, the author applies the same principle to the matters of threat (wa'id) and promise (wa'd): once the reality of the action is established, the rulings of command and prohibition follow, and similarly reward and punishment follow. The key point is that the act itself, as a created entity, is not inherently good or bad from all aspects; evaluation comes from a different consideration.

Key Terms
الأمر والنهي
الخُلق
أوصاف الإضافات
الوعيد والوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that if God creates an action, He is automatically commanding or prohibiting it.
Correction: Maturidi argues that creation (khulq) does not entail command or prohibition; it is permissible for an action to be created without these rulings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can an action be created without being subject to command or prohibition?
A) Because the action's attributes are inherently good or evil
B) Because relational attributes like larger/smaller are not moral categories
C) Because God only creates obedient acts
D) Because command and prohibition are based on the act's inherent nature

Key Takeaway
Divine command and prohibition are not derived from the inherent attributes of actions; rather, actions are created without entailing moral or legal judgment, which comes from a separate consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone commit a sin, do you immediately label the act as evil? Maturidi teaches that actions are created without intrinsic moral value. How does this truth challenge your tendency to quickly judge others? Will you now pause and remember that moral rulings come from God's command, not from your own assessment?
?
Think of a sin you committed that still weighs on your conscience. This lesson says that God creates the action but does not intrinsically command or prohibit it. How does that reshape your understanding of His mercy? Does it help you separate your guilt over the act from your trust in His forgiveness?
?
If actions are not inherently good or evil, what does that mean for your personal accountability? How can you cultivate a deeper sensitivity to God's commands and prohibitions, knowing that He alone determines moral value? What practical step will you take this week to align your actions with His revealed law rather than your own judgments?
