Three Reasons for Denying God's Creation of Actions
Maturidi enumerates three stances on denying God's creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main reasons why some theologians deny that God creates human actions according to Maturidi's classification.

Core Idea
Denial of God creating actions stems from impossibility, lack of evidence, or fear of compulsion.

Imam Maturidi begins by laying out the possible stances one might take regarding the doctrine of khalaq al-af'al. He identifies three distinct reasons for rejecting it, each carrying different theological implications. This classification clarifies the landscape before moving into refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi lists the three reasons in a concise manner.
The passage uses 'إما أن ينكر... أو... أو...' for listing.
2
Identify the three reasons
Find each reason: impossibility (الإحالة), lack of evidence (لا دلالة), and necessitation of compulsion (إيجاب الضرورة وارتفاع الإمكان). Understand the wording.
3
Reflect on the third reason
Notice that the third reason includes a consequence: command, prohibition, promise, threat become unreasonable. Ask why that might be a concern.
This sets the stage for the later refutation of the first reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في هذا أن يكون القول بخلق الأفعال: إما أن ينكر للإحالة، أو لما لا دلالة على القول بذلك، أو لما في القول به إيجاب الضرورة وارتفاع الإمكان، ويقبح في العقول الأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد فيما كان هذا سبيله. فمن أبي القول به للإحالة كُلف دليله على ذلك، ولن يجد إلا على التقدير بفعل العباد: أن لا يكون فعل واحد في الحقيقة لاثنين، أو يظن أن القول يوجب الشركة. فجواب الحرف الأول في تقسيم القول لما اختلف فيه. فعندنا أن فعل الله تعالى في الحقيقة غير فعل العبد، وفعل العبد مفعوله لا فعله ووجود مثله في الشاهد غير عسير؛ نحو مد اثنين شيئًا ينقطع، وإزالة اثنين شيئًا عن مكان، وفعلهما واحد يصيران به شريكين في أنه مفعولهما في الحقيقة، وكذلك المزال والمنقطع واحد؛ وكذلك الحمل فيه جزء لا يتجزأ، حمله اثنان قواهما واحد إن حقيقة فعلهما وإن اختلفت فالمفعول واحد لهما، فمثله الذي نحن فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the principle in this is that the statement about the creation of actions: either it is denied due to impossibility, or due to no evidence for that statement, or because in the statement there is necessitation of compulsion and removal of possibility, and command and prohibition and threat and promise become ugly in reason in what is of this way. So whoever refuses the statement due to impossibility is required to provide evidence for that, and he will not find except on the assumption of the action of servants: that there cannot be one action in reality for two, or he thinks that the statement necessitates partnership. So the answer of the first part in the division of the statement for what is differed about. For with us, the action of God Almighty in reality is other than the action of the servant, and the action of the servant is His effect not His action, and the existence of its like in the witnessed world is not difficult; like the pulling of two people of something that breaks, and the removal of two people of something from a place, and their action is one, they become partners in that it is their effect in reality, and likewise the removed and the broken are one; and likewise the carrying of a thing that is indivisible, two people carry it, their strength is one, for the reality of their action, even if they differ, the effect is one for them, so the like of that is what we are in. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of opinions regarding the doctrine of God's creation of human actions (khalaq al-af'al) and then refutes the first position. The author states that the affirmation of God's creating actions can be denied for three reasons: (أ) because it is impossible (iḥāla), (ب) because there is no evidence for it, or (ج) because it would entail compulsion (ḍarūra) and negate possibility, thus making command, prohibition, promise, and threat unreasonable. The author then addresses the first category: those who deny due to impossibility. He argues that such a denier is required to provide proof, and the only possible proof would be based on assuming that one action cannot really be from two agents, or that the doctrine implies partnership (shirka) with God. The answer is that God's action and the servant's action are different in reality: the servant's action is God's creation (maf'ul) not the servant's own act. The author provides analogies from common experience: two people pulling a rope that breaks—their pulling is a single action in terms of the effect (the break), and they become partners in the effect. Similarly, two people removing an object or carrying an indivisible particle—their combined action results in a single effect. Thus, it is possible for God to create the action while the servant acquires it, without implying partnership or impossibility. The conclusion is that the analogy supports the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال
الإحالة
الضرورة
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think there is only one possible reason to deny God's creation of actions.
Correction: Maturidi shows there are three distinct reasons, each requiring a different response.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three reasons Maturidi lists for denying God's creation of actions?
A) Impossibility
B) Lack of evidence
C) Partnership with God
D) Entails compulsion

Key Takeaway
Maturidi classifies three reasons for denying God's creation of actions, each requiring a distinct response in theological debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by thinking, 'God created this action, so it wasn't really my fault'? How does this passage's third reason challenge that excuse and call you to repentance?
?
When you feel trapped in a bad habit, do you truly believe that God's creation of actions still leaves room for your genuine choice and accountability? What concrete step will you take tonight to exercise that choice?
?
Which of the three reasons do you find yourself using most often to avoid responsibility for your actions? How will you remind yourself tomorrow that God's justice and your moral obligation remain intact?

Refuting Denial Due to Impossibility with Analogy
Maturidi refutes the impossibility claim with analogies of shared effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the denial of God's creation of actions based on impossibility, using analogies from everyday experience.

Core Idea
One action can be the effect of two agents without partnership, as seen in common examples.

Having listed the three reasons, Maturidi now responds to the first: those who claim it is impossible for God to create human actions because one action cannot belong to two agents. He argues that this objection relies on a mistaken assumption and provides everyday analogies to show that a single effect can proceed from two agents without implying partnership in the full sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic from 'فمن أبي القول' to the end, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer, analogy, conclusion.
2
Understand the objection
The denier argues that one action cannot truly be from two agents, otherwise it would imply partnership (shirk) with God. Identify this claim.
3
Follow the analogies
Maturidi gives three analogies: two people pulling a rope, removing an object, carrying an indivisible particle. Note how each shows a single effect from two agents.
These examples are from witnessed reality (fi al-shāhid).
4
Grasp the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God's action is different in reality from the servant's action; the servant's act is God's creation (maf'ul), not the servant's own independent act. The analogies support this distinction.
The key: God is the sole creator, but human actions are real effects with human acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the principle in this is that the statement about the creation of actions: either it is denied due to impossibility, or due to no evidence for that statement, or because in the statement there is necessitation of compulsion and removal of possibility, and command and prohibition and threat and promise become ugly in reason in what is of this way. So whoever refuses the statement due to impossibility is required to provide evidence for that, and he will not find except on the assumption of the action of servants: that there cannot be one action in reality for two, or he thinks that the statement necessitates partnership. So the answer of the first part in the division of the statement for what is differed about. For with us, the action of God Almighty in reality is other than the action of the servant, and the action of the servant is His effect not His action, and the existence of its like in the witnessed world is not difficult; like the pulling of two people of something that breaks, and the removal of two people of something from a place, and their action is one, they become partners in that it is their effect in reality, and likewise the removed and the broken are one; and likewise the carrying of a thing that is indivisible, two people carry it, their strength is one, for the reality of their action, even if they differ, the effect is one for them, so the like of that is what we are in. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of opinions regarding the doctrine of God's creation of human actions (khalaq al-af'al) and then refutes the first position. The author states that the affirmation of God's creating actions can be denied for three reasons: (أ) because it is impossible (iḥāla), (ب) because there is no evidence for it, or (ج) because it would entail compulsion (ḍarūra) and negate possibility, thus making command, prohibition, promise, and threat unreasonable. The author then addresses the first category: those who deny due to impossibility. He argues that such a denier is required to provide proof, and the only possible proof would be based on assuming that one action cannot really be from two agents, or that the doctrine implies partnership (shirka) with God. The answer is that God's action and the servant's action are different in reality: the servant's action is God's creation (maf'ul) not the servant's own act. The author provides analogies from common experience: two people pulling a rope that breaks—their pulling is a single action in terms of the effect (the break), and they become partners in the effect. Similarly, two people removing an object or carrying an indivisible particle—their combined action results in a single effect. Thus, it is possible for God to create the action while the servant acquires it, without implying partnership or impossibility. The conclusion is that the analogy supports the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
خلق الأفعال
الإحالة
الشركة
المفعول
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates my actions, then I have no role and am like a puppet.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human actions are God's creation (maf'ul) but humans acquire them; the action belongs to God in terms of creation and to the human in terms of acquisition, preserving responsibility.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to show that a single effect can come from two agents?
A) A carpenter and a saw
B) Two people pulling a rope that breaks
C) A teacher and a student
D) A seed and a tree

Key Takeaway
The objection that God cannot create human actions because one action cannot come from two agents is refuted by everyday examples showing that one effect can result from multiple agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you truly believe that your power is from God alone? How does this change the way you approach even the smallest daily tasks?
?
The analogies show that two people can produce one effect. In your life, can you recall a situation where you shared credit for an achievement? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of attributing success—or failure—to yourself versus Allah?
?
If every action you take is ultimately created by Allah and only acquired by you, what motivation remains for you to strive? How can this truth increase your sense of accountability instead of diminishing it?

No Empowerment to Create
Maturidi asserts that no one can empower another to create, and warns against equating God's power with human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that no one can empower another to create an action, and that equating God's power with human power is a form of ignorance.

Core Idea
Only Allah creates; no creature can empower another to create.

Maturidi begins by establishing a fundamental principle: no creature can empower another to perform its action or to create its own action. This sets the stage for understanding the unique nature of divine power. In this lesson, we explore this principle and why comparing God's action to human action is misguided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's concise style come through.
The passage is only two sentences but dense.
2
Understand the principle
The passage states it is not permissible for anyone to empower another to perform his action or to create his own action. No one can act outside their own locus and state. Grasp this denial of creaturely creative power.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is equating God's action with human action ignorance? Because human actions subsist in creation, while God's power is transcendent. The author calls such estimation ignorance and confusion.
Consider how this protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لا يجوز أن يملك أحد تقوية آخر على فعله ولا خلق فعل نفسه، ولا أحد يقدر أن يفعل فعلاً في غير حيزه وغير حال في نفسه. ففي تقدير فعل الله بالموجود من فعل الخلق جهل وشبهة من جهة القدرة وقيام الفعل بالخلق، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. والقول الآخر قول من يقول: إن خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء، فقد بينا اختلاف الجهات في ذلك. فجائز القول بالخلق من جهة هي غير جهة القول بالكفر، على ما بيّنا من الشيئية. وقد زعم المعتزلة في حركة المفلوج أنها الله خلقا وللعبد حركة؛ وهي شيء لنفسها، إذ الشيئية عندهم في المعدوم، وهي دلالة حدث الجسم، وفي الكفر حجة الله على العبد في التعذيب ودلالة سفهه في التحقيق على أنا بينا أنه يُحيل من حيث لا يكون مثل ذلك في الخلق، وقد أوضحنا الفصل بين الأمرين، وأن من قاس أحد الوجهين بالآخر فهو مُغفّل. على أن المعتزلة إذ لا يجعلون من الله إلى الخلق سوى أنه أوجدهم بعد أن لم يكونوا. ولا ذلك معنى فعل العباد، إنما هو معالجات وعناء وجهد، والموجود فيما نحن فيه مع المعنى الذي من العباد واقعان جميعا، فلا وجه لإنكاره.
Moreover, it is not permissible that anyone should empower another to perform his action, nor to create his own action, nor can anyone act in a place other than his own locus and other than his own state. So in estimating God's action by the existing from the action of creation there is ignorance and confusion from the aspect of power and the subsistence of the action in creation; exalted is God above that. And the other view is the view of those who say: the creation of a thing is that thing itself; we have already explained the difference of aspects in that. So it is permissible to speak of creation from an aspect other than the aspect of speaking of disbelief, according to what we have explained about thingness (al-shay'iyya). And the Mu'tazila claimed concerning the movement of the paralyzed person that it is God's creation, and for the servant there is a movement; and it is a thing in itself, since thingness according to them is in the non-existent, and it is an indication of the originatedness of the body, and in disbelief, it is God's proof against the servant in punishment and an indication of his foolishness in reality, according to what we have explained that it becomes impossible from where there is no like of that in creation, and we have clarified the distinction between the two matters, and that whoever compares one of the two aspects with the other is deluded. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila, since they do not make from God to the creation anything except that He brought them into existence after they had not been, and that is not the meaning of the actions of servants, for they are only treatments, labor, and effort, and the existing in what we are discussing together with the meaning that is from the servants are both occurring, so there is no reason to deny it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage contains several interconnected arguments. First, the author asserts that no one can empower another to perform his own action or create his own action, nor can anyone act outside his own locus (ḥīza). Therefore, equating God's power with human power is ignorance. Second, the author addresses an alternative view: some say the creation of a thing is identical to the thing itself. The author replies that the aspects differ—one can speak of 'creation' from one aspect and 'disbelief' from another, as explained earlier regarding 'thingness' (shay'iyya). Third, the Mu'tazila claim that in the movement of a paralyzed person, the movement is God's creation while the servant has a movement; they consider it a thing in itself because they hold thingness for non-existents (ma'dum). The author refutes this by pointing out that such a view leads to impossibility (iḥāla) because no similar instance exists in creation. He emphasizes the distinction between the two aspects and warns against conflating them. Finally, the author notes that the Mu'tazila limit God's relation to creation to mere origination (ījād), and that human actions involve treatments and effort; since both the divine and human aspects coexist in the act, there is no justification to deny one aspect. The overall thrust is to affirm that God creates the act while the human acquires it, without contradiction.

Key Terms
خلق فعل نفسه
الحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That one creature can empower another to create or that human power is comparable to God's power.
Correction: No one can empower another to create, and equating God's power to human power is ignorance; God is exalted above such comparison.

Checkpoint
What is the result of estimating God's action by human action, according to the passage?
A) Wisdom
B) Ignorance and confusion
C) Clarity
D) Empowerment

Key Takeaway
No creature can empower another to create, and comparing God's power to human power is ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a success or failure in your life to your own effort or someone else's help, do you unconsciously imagine that either you or that person 'created' the outcome? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for you to truly believe that only Allah creates every event, even those involving your choices?
?
Think of a moment you felt proud of empowering someone — a student, a child, a colleague. Does this passage strip that achievement of its meaning? Or does it refine it? If no creature can truly empower another, how should you understand your role in helping others? What internal shift does this demand from you?
?
The passage ends with 'exalted is God above that.' Is there a part of your heart that still resents God's absolute power — perhaps because it seems to diminish your autonomy? Can you honestly say that you are at peace with being a creature who cannot create even a single atom? If not, what will you do to realign your heart with this truth starting today?

Creation and Human Action: The Mu'tazila View Refuted
Maturidi critiques the view that creation equals the thing, and refutes the Mu'tazila on the paralyzed person, affirming two real aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the view that creation of a thing is identical to the thing itself, and the Mu'tazila claim about the paralyzed person, as well as the coexistence of divine creation and human effort.

Core Idea
God creates the act, while the human acquires it through effort; both aspects are real.

In the previous lesson, we established that no creature can empower another to create. Now, Maturidi addresses a second view: some say the creation of a thing is that thing itself. He then discusses a specific Mu'tazila argument about the movement of a paralyzed person. This lesson unpacks these arguments and the crucial distinction between divine creation and human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. It contains multiple parts: a second view, a Mu'tazila claim, and a final point. Note the logical flow.
This passage is longer; take your time.
2
Analyze the second view and response
Some say creation of a thing is that thing itself. Maturidi responds that aspects differ, so it is permissible to speak of creation from a different aspect than disbelief, referencing thingness (shay'iyya). Ask yourself: how does this avoid contradiction?
3
Examine the Mu'tazila claim and its refutation
The Mu'tazila claim about the paralyzed person: the movement is God's creation yet the servant has a movement. They see it as a thing in itself because thingness applies to non-existents. Maturidi refutes by saying this leads to impossibility (iḥāla) since no like exists in creation. Notice the warning against conflation.
4
Understand the final point on coexistence
Maturidi concludes that Mu'tazila limit God to mere origination (ījād), but human actions involve treatments and effort. Both the divine and human aspects are real and occur together, so there is no reason to deny the human role.
This is the core of the acquisition (kasb) doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Moreover, it is not permissible that anyone should empower another to perform his action, nor to create his own action, nor can anyone act in a place other than his own locus and other than his own state. So in estimating God's action by the existing from the action of creation there is ignorance and confusion from the aspect of power and the subsistence of the action in creation; exalted is God above that. And the other view is the view of those who say: the creation of a thing is that thing itself; we have already explained the difference of aspects in that. So it is permissible to speak of creation from an aspect other than the aspect of speaking of disbelief, according to what we have explained about thingness (al-shay'iyya). And the Mu'tazila claimed concerning the movement of the paralyzed person that it is God's creation, and for the servant there is a movement; and it is a thing in itself, since thingness according to them is in the non-existent, and it is an indication of the originatedness of the body, and in disbelief, it is God's proof against the servant in punishment and an indication of his foolishness in reality, according to what we have explained that it becomes impossible from where there is no like of that in creation, and we have clarified the distinction between the two matters, and that whoever compares one of the two aspects with the other is deluded. Furthermore, the Mu'tazila, since they do not make from God to the creation anything except that He brought them into existence after they had not been, and that is not the meaning of the actions of servants, for they are only treatments, labor, and effort, and the existing in what we are discussing together with the meaning that is from the servants are both occurring, so there is no reason to deny it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage contains several interconnected arguments. First, the author asserts that no one can empower another to perform his own action or create his own action, nor can anyone act outside his own locus (ḥīza). Therefore, equating God's power with human power is ignorance. Second, the author addresses an alternative view: some say the creation of a thing is identical to the thing itself. The author replies that the aspects differ—one can speak of 'creation' from one aspect and 'disbelief' from another, as explained earlier regarding 'thingness' (shay'iyya). Third, the Mu'tazila claim that in the movement of a paralyzed person, the movement is God's creation while the servant has a movement; they consider it a thing in itself because they hold thingness for non-existents (ma'dum). The author refutes this by pointing out that such a view leads to impossibility (iḥāla) because no similar instance exists in creation. He emphasizes the distinction between the two aspects and warns against conflating them. Finally, the author notes that the Mu'tazila limit God's relation to creation to mere origination (ījād), and that human actions involve treatments and effort; since both the divine and human aspects coexist in the act, there is no justification to deny one aspect. The overall thrust is to affirm that God creates the act while the human acquires it, without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel paralyzed—whether physically, emotionally, or spiritually—do you truly believe that both God's decree and your own efforts are real? Or do you slip into either complete resignation or self-reliance? Take one concrete step today to affirm both: seek God's help while exerting your full effort.
?
The Mu'tazila reduced human action to mere metaphor, denying its reality. Is there an area of your life where you have unconsciously done the same—blaming God for your failures or crediting yourself for successes without recognizing His creation? Write down one responsibility you have been avoiding by attributing it entirely to divine will, and commit to acting on it today.
?

Have you ever compared your own efforts to God's creation, leading either to arrogance (thinking you did it alone) or despair (feeling your effort is meaningless)? How can you hold both realities together without confusing them, especially in your next challenge?

Divine Attributes

Distinguishing Creator from Speaker
Maturidi distinguishes between 'Creator of everything' and 'Speaker of every speech'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God is called 'Creator of everything' but not 'Speaker of every speech' and how this distinction is supported by the Mu'tazila's own inconsistency.

Core Idea
Creation and speech are not analogous; the title 'Creator' is more comprehensive.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi begins by drawing a clear distinction between God's attribute as Creator and His attribute as Speaker. He points out that while it is permissible to say God is the Creator of all things, it is not permissible to say He is the Speaker of every speech. This distinction, as we will see, is rooted in the nature of these attributes and the Mu'tazila's own inconsistent position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Read the section where Maturidi establishes the distinction: God is Creator of everything, but not Speaker of every speech.
Notice the contrast: 'خالق كل شيء' vs 'قائل كل قول'.
2
Identify the implication
Maturidi concludes that one is not the counterpart of the other. Reflect on why that might be.
3
Observe the Mu'tazilite inconsistency
They accept that God's power underlies all actions but not that His speech underlies human speech. This shows they treat creation and speech differently.
This inconsistency is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق ولا يجوز أن يقال هو قائل كل قول ولا مخبر كل خبر ولا هو مخبر وقائل وما سواه خبر وقول فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير للآخر مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل الله تعالى ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه بقدرة هي قول الله تعالى وخبره ويقال له إذا لم يسم هو متحركا بما حرك غيره فقل أيضا إنه لا يسمى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره وإذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما به على أن المعنى الذي به سمي في فعل كل أحد والمعنى الذي به سمي قائلا لم يوجد لذلك اختلفا والله أعلم وأيضا إن القول بالخالق يخرج مخرج التعظيم فكل ما هو أعم فهو أبلغ وبقائل لا لذلك اختلفا فنذكر معاني إنكاره أيضا ثم الأصل أن إنكار المعتزلة هذا بما لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود وهو الأصل الذي له أنكر من أنكر خلق الأعيان بامتناعه في الشاهد عن الوجود في الحقيقة بفعل أحد بل لا يوجد فيه غير جمع وتفريق فأبوا أن يكون خلق أعيان الأشياء بذلك وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال فلذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك مع ما قولهم في التحقيق ذلك لأنهم حققوا الأشياء في القدم وجعلوا من الله إيجادها لا إحداث شيئيتها وكانت الشيئية لا به فيكون العالم عندهم في التحقيق حدثا عن أشياء لا أنه أحدث عن غير شيء ثم ذكروا في الكفر والإيمان أنهما شيئان كان من الفاعل إيجادهما
Then it is permissible to say that God is the Creator of everything, and He is the Creator, and other than Him is created. And it is not permissible to say that He is the Speaker of every speech, nor the Informer of every report, nor is He an Informer and Speaker while other than Him is report and speech. So this indicates that one of them is not the counterpart of the other. Additionally, they allow that every individual's act is by a power that is the act of God, but they do not allow that every individual's speech and report is by a power that is the speech of God and His report. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'moving' by what He moves others with, then also say that He is not called 'Creator' by what He creates others' movements with. And if you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or by whatever you wish, then differentiate between them by that. But the meaning by which He is named in the act of everyone, and the meaning by which He is named 'Speaker', does not exist; therefore they differ. And God knows best. Also, the expression 'Creator' is used for exaltation, so whatever is more general is more emphatic, but 'Speaker' is not like that; therefore they differ. So we mention meanings of His denial as well. Then the principle is that the Mu'tazila's denial of this is because they did not find any human action that brings something from non-existence to existence, and that is the principle for which those who denied the creation of entities denied: because of its impossibility in the witnessed world to come into existence truly by anyone's action—rather, nothing exists in it except gathering and separating. So they refused that the creation of the entities of things could be by that. And similarly, the Mu'tazila denied the creation of actions. Therefore the early scholars attributed that to them, along with the fact that in reality their statement is that; because they investigated things in pre-eternity, and they made from God their bringing into existence, not the bringing about of their quiddity, and quiddity was not from Him. So the world according to them in reality is an occurrence from things, not that it occurred from nothing. Then they mentioned regarding unbelief and faith that they are two things that the agent brings into existence.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage contains several distinct arguments. First, Maturidi distinguishes between 'Creator of everything' and 'Speaker of every speech'—the former is permissible but not the latter, indicating that creation and speech are not analogous. He notes that the Mu'tazila accept that God empowers actions but not that He empowers speech in the same way. He then challenges them: if you do not call God 'mover' for the movements He creates in others, then you should also not call Him 'Creator' for the actions He creates in others. If you differentiate based on generality or another criterion, then apply it consistently. The reason they differ is that the meaning that justifies naming God 'Creator' for every act (i.e., bringing into existence from nothing) is absent in the case of speech. Additionally, 'Creator' implies exaltation and is more general, so it is more emphatic, while 'Speaker' does not have that connotation. Then Maturidi explains the root of the Mu'tazilite denial: they measure the unseen by the visible world, where no human can bring something from non-existence to existence; they only see the gathering and separating of pre-existing materials. Therefore they deny that God creates entities or actions in a similar way, effectively treating creation as the organization of pre-existent matter. Their view on faith and unbelief is also based on this: they are things that the agent brings into existence (i.e., the human creates them). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
خالق كل شيء
قائل كل قول
مخبر كل خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes like 'Creator' and 'Speaker' are analogous in scope and application.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'Creator' is more comprehensive and cannot be equated with 'Speaker'.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between 'Creator of everything' and 'Speaker of every speech'?
A) They are equivalent.
B) The former is permissible, the latter is not.
C) Both are permissible.
D) Neither is permissible.

Key Takeaway
God is called 'Creator of everything' because His creative act brings things from non-existence to existence, but He is not called 'Speaker of every speech' because speech originates from humans differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often call God 'Creator of everything.' Have you ever considered that you do not call Him 'Speaker of every speech'? What does this distinction reveal about your own understanding of God's transcendence? Will you now be more careful in how you attribute actions to God?
?
The Mu'tazila allowed that every human act is by a power that is God's act, but not that every speech is by God's speech. Are you consistent in how you attribute actions to God in your daily life? When you speak, do you recognize that your speech is not God's speech, yet it is still permitted? How does this affect your sense of accountability for your own words?
?
If you were to claim that God is the Creator of your every action, would you also claim that He is the Speaker of your every word? Where does your logic break down? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of analogy when describing God?

Challenging the Mu'tazila: Analogy and Generality
Maturidi challenges the Mu'tazila on consistency and argues that 'Creator' is more general and exalting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi’s challenge to the Mu'tazila that they should apply the same logic to 'Creator' as they do to 'Mover' and the argument that 'Creator' denotes greater generality and exaltation.

Core Idea
If you don't call God 'Mover' for others' movements, then don't call Him 'Creator' for others' actions either.

After establishing the initial distinction, Maturidi now advances a direct challenge to the Mu'tazila. He forces them to be consistent: if they refuse to call God 'Mover' for the movements He creates in others, then they should also refuse to call Him 'Creator' for the actions He creates in others. Then he adds that the term 'Creator' is used for exaltation and is more general, so it is more emphatic, unlike 'Speaker'.

Guided Path
1
Read the challenge from analogy
Maturidi argues: if God is not called 'moving' by what He moves others with, then He should not be called 'Creator' by what He creates others' movements with.
This forces consistency.
2
Understand the consistency requirement
If the Mu'tazila differentiate between 'Creator' and 'Speaker' by generality or some other criterion, they must apply it consistently.
3
Reflect on the meaning difference
The meaning that justifies naming God 'Creator' for every act (bringing into existence) does not exist for speech, so they differ.
This is a deeper reason.
4
Consider the exaltation argument
'Creator' is used for exaltation and is more general, thus more emphatic, while 'Speaker' is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق ولا يجوز أن يقال هو قائل كل قول ولا مخبر كل خبر ولا هو مخبر وقائل وما سواه خبر وقول فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير للآخر مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل الله تعالى ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه بقدرة هي قول الله تعالى وخبره ويقال له إذا لم يسم هو متحركا بما حرك غيره فقل أيضا إنه لا يسمى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره وإذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما به على أن المعنى الذي به سمي في فعل كل أحد والمعنى الذي به سمي قائلا لم يوجد لذلك اختلفا والله أعلم وأيضا إن القول بالخالق يخرج مخرج التعظيم فكل ما هو أعم فهو أبلغ وبقائل لا لذلك اختلفا فنذكر معاني إنكاره أيضا ثم الأصل أن إنكار المعتزلة هذا بما لم يجدوا فعل أحد يخرجه غيره من العدم إلى الوجود وهو الأصل الذي له أنكر من أنكر خلق الأعيان بامتناعه في الشاهد عن الوجود في الحقيقة بفعل أحد بل لا يوجد فيه غير جمع وتفريق فأبوا أن يكون خلق أعيان الأشياء بذلك وبمثله أنكرت المعتزلة خلق الأفعال فلذلك نسبهم الأوائل إلى ذلك مع ما قولهم في التحقيق ذلك لأنهم حققوا الأشياء في القدم وجعلوا من الله إيجادها لا إحداث شيئيتها وكانت الشيئية لا به فيكون العالم عندهم في التحقيق حدثا عن أشياء لا أنه أحدث عن غير شيء ثم ذكروا في الكفر والإيمان أنهما شيئان كان من الفاعل إيجادهما
Then it is permissible to say that God is the Creator of everything, and He is the Creator, and other than Him is created. And it is not permissible to say that He is the Speaker of every speech, nor the Informer of every report, nor is He an Informer and Speaker while other than Him is report and speech. So this indicates that one of them is not the counterpart of the other. Additionally, they allow that every individual's act is by a power that is the act of God, but they do not allow that every individual's speech and report is by a power that is the speech of God and His report. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'moving' by what He moves others with, then also say that He is not called 'Creator' by what He creates others' movements with. And if you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or by whatever you wish, then differentiate between them by that. But the meaning by which He is named in the act of everyone, and the meaning by which He is named 'Speaker', does not exist; therefore they differ. And God knows best. Also, the expression 'Creator' is used for exaltation, so whatever is more general is more emphatic, but 'Speaker' is not like that; therefore they differ. So we mention meanings of His denial as well. Then the principle is that the Mu'tazila's denial of this is because they did not find any human action that brings something from non-existence to existence, and that is the principle for which those who denied the creation of entities denied: because of its impossibility in the witnessed world to come into existence truly by anyone's action—rather, nothing exists in it except gathering and separating. So they refused that the creation of the entities of things could be by that. And similarly, the Mu'tazila denied the creation of actions. Therefore the early scholars attributed that to them, along with the fact that in reality their statement is that; because they investigated things in pre-eternity, and they made from God their bringing into existence, not the bringing about of their quiddity, and quiddity was not from Him. So the world according to them in reality is an occurrence from things, not that it occurred from nothing. Then they mentioned regarding unbelief and faith that they are two things that the agent brings into existence.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage contains several distinct arguments. First, Maturidi distinguishes between 'Creator of everything' and 'Speaker of every speech'—the former is permissible but not the latter, indicating that creation and speech are not analogous. He notes that the Mu'tazila accept that God empowers actions but not that He empowers speech in the same way. He then challenges them: if you do not call God 'mover' for the movements He creates in others, then you should also not call Him 'Creator' for the actions He creates in others. If you differentiate based on generality or another criterion, then apply it consistently. The reason they differ is that the meaning that justifies naming God 'Creator' for every act (i.e., bringing into existence from nothing) is absent in the case of speech. Additionally, 'Creator' implies exaltation and is more general, so it is more emphatic, while 'Speaker' does not have that connotation. Then Maturidi explains the root of the Mu'tazilite denial: they measure the unseen by the visible world, where no human can bring something from non-existence to existence; they only see the gathering and separating of pre-existing materials. Therefore they deny that God creates entities or actions in a similar way, effectively treating creation as the organization of pre-existent matter. Their view on faith and unbelief is also based on this: they are things that the agent brings into existence (i.e., the human creates them). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Correction: Maturidi shows they inconsistently treat 'Creator' and 'Speaker', as they accept God's power for actions but not His speech for speech.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to challenge the Mu'tazila?
A) If God is not called 'Creator' for others' actions, don't call Him 'Speaker'.
B) If God is not called 'Mover' for others' movements, don't call Him 'Creator' for others' actions.
C) If God is not called 'Speaker', don't call Him 'Creator'.
D) If God is not called 'Informer', don't call Him 'Creator'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila must be consistent: if they deny calling God 'Mover' for others' movements, they should also deny calling Him 'Creator' for others' actions; the difference lies in the meaning and the connotation of exaltation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'Creator' for every action in the universe, do you also accept that He is the 'Speaker' of every speech, including the Qur'an? Have you been inconsistent like the Mu'tazila, picking and choosing which attributes to affirm based on your own logic rather than submitting to revelation? Today, commit to affirming all of Allah's attributes as He has described them without making analogies that limit Him.
?
In your own life, do you apply one standard to your own actions and a different standard when judging others? The Mu'tazila's inconsistency here mirrors our own hypocrisy. Reflect on a specific area where you excuse yourself but condemn others. How will you align your standards today?
?
The name 'Creator' carries a weight of exaltation—do you truly live as if Allah is the sole Creator of every detail of your life? Or do you attribute your successes to your own efforts and your failures to external causes? Examine your heart: where do you give credit to yourself rather than to Allah, and repent from that subtle shirk.

Root of Mu'tazilite Denial: Measuring Unseen by Visible
Maturidi explains the Mu'tazilite denial as stemming from anthropocentric reasoning based on visible human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental error of the Mu'tazila as identified by Maturidi: they judge God's creation by human inability to bring things from nothing, leading them to deny that God creates entities and actions directly.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny God's creation of actions because they see only human acts of gathering and separating, not bringing things from non-existence.

In this concluding part, Maturidi delves into the root cause of the Mu'tazilite denial. He explains that they measure the unseen realm of divine action by the visible world of human action, where no one can bring something from nothing. They only see the gathering and separating of pre-existing materials, so they reject that God creates entities and actions in a true sense. This leads to their views on faith and unbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the root cause
Maturidi states the Mu'tazila deny because they do not find any human action that brings something from non-existence to existence.
This is the principle behind their denial.
2
Understand the error of analogy
They measure the unseen world by the visible world, where only gathering and separating occur.
3
Recognize the scope of denial
This denial extends to creation of entities (a'yan) and actions.
4
Examine their positive view
They hold that God brings things into existence (ijad) but does not create their quiddity (shay'iyya); the world is an occurrence from pre-existent things.
This leads to their view that humans create their own actions, including faith and unbelief.

Refuting the Analogy Between Creator and Speaker
Maturidi refutes the analogy between khaliq and qa'il using the witnessed/unseen distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God is Creator of all but not Speaker of all speech, using the distinction between witnessed and unseen.

Core Idea
God's names are not derived from His acts in creation the same way.

Maturidi engages a Mu'tazilite argument that since God creates all actions and speech, He should be called Speaker of all speech just as He is Creator of all. This passage presents his refutation, grounded in the fundamental distinction between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). By the end, you will see why the name 'Qa'il' is not applied to every speech, preserving God's unique attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
The passage is a refutation, so expect a dialectical structure.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
Notice that Maturidi begins by referencing an opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) who draws an analogy between 'Creator' and 'Speaker'. Understand the opponent's claim: if God is called Creator because He creates all actions, then He should be called Speaker because He creates all speech.
3
Trace Maturidi's refutation
Follow how Maturidi first points out a flaw in the analogy using the shahid/gha'ib distinction. Then he states the accepted position: God is Creator of everything, but not Speaker of every speech. Then he provides a further argument based on the term 'moving'.
Notice the logical progression: flaw → position → additional proof.
4
Grasp the underlying reason
Conclude with the key point: the meaning that makes one a 'Speaker' for every speech does not exist in God. Therefore, the two names are not analogous.
This is the core theological insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلان وفلان فلئن كان ذا أصله فيجب به جواز الآخر، إذ به يلزم الآخر . ولو كان [كل] ما يجوز في الشاهد هو دليل الغائب ليجب التفريق بين الفعل والقول في الغائب كما وجب في الشاهد، وهذا يبين وهمه. ثم جائز القول بأن الله خالق كل شيء، وهو خالق وما سواه مخلوق؛ ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو قائل كل قول، ولا مخبر كل خبر، ولا هو مخبر وقائل، وما سواه خبر وقول، فدل أن أحدهما ليس بنظير للآخر. مع ما يجوز عندهم فعل كل واحد بقدرة هي فعل الله تعالى، ثم لم يجز في قول كل أحد وخبره أنه بقدرة هي قول الله تعالى وخبره. ويُقال له : إذا لم يُسم هو متحركاً بما حرّك غيره، فقل أيضاً إنه لا يُسمّى خالقا بما خلق حركة غيره؛ وإذ فصل بينهما بالعموم والخصوص أو بما شئت فافصل بينهما [به]. على أن المعنى الذي به سمى في فعل كل أحد، والمعنى الذي به سمى قائلاً لم يوجد؛ لذلك اختلفا. والله أعلم.
So-and-so and so-and-so: if the origin of this is valid, then the permissibility of the other follows, because the other is necessitated by it. And if everything that is permissible in the witnessed world is evidence for the unseen, then it would be necessary to make a distinction between action and speech in the unseen as is necessitated in the witnessed, and this shows his error. Then it is permissible to say that God is the Creator of everything, and He is the Creator and what is other than Him is created; and it is not permissible to say: He is the Speaker of every saying, nor the Informer of every report, nor is He Informer and Speaker, and what is other than Him is report and speech. So this indicates that one of them is not analogous to the other. Along with the fact that according to them the act of each one is permissible by a power which is the act of God Almighty, yet it is not permissible regarding the speech and report of everyone that it is by a power which is the speech and report of God Almighty. And it is said to him: If He is not called 'moving' by what He moved of others, then say likewise that He is not called 'Creator' by what He created of the movement of others. And since you differentiate between them by generality and specificity or by whatever you wish, differentiate between them [by it]. However, the meaning by which He is named in the act of everyone, and the meaning by which He is named 'Speaker' does not exist; therefore they differ. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an analogy made by the Mu'tazilites (specifically al-Ka'bi) between the divine names 'Creator' and 'Speaker'. Maturidi begins by pointing out the flaw in the opponent's example: if the opponent's analogy were valid, then distinctions made in the witnessed world (between action and speech) would have to apply to God, which they do not. He then states the accepted position: God is Creator of everything, but not Speaker of every speech; speech belongs to creatures. The distinction is drawn by noting that actions can be attributed to a power that is God's act, but speech cannot be similarly attributed. Further, if God is not called 'moving' by the movement He creates in others, then likewise He should not be called 'Creator' of the movement of others—indicating that the name 'Creator' is not derived from the act in the same way. The underlying reason is that the meaning by which one is named 'Creator' in the case of everyone (i.e., God is Creator of all acts) is present, but the meaning by which one is named 'Speaker' for every speech does not exist in God. Therefore, the two names are not analogous.

Key Terms
خالق
قائل
غائب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God creates all speech, He should be called Speaker of that speech.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the meaning of 'Speaker' (eternal attribute) is not present in created speech; God is Speaker only of His own eternal speech.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not called 'Qa'il of every qawl'?
A) Because He does not create all speech
B) Because the meaning that makes one a Speaker does not exist in God for every speech
C) Because speech is not created by God
D) Because the term Qa'il is only for humans

Key Takeaway
God is Creator of all, but not Speaker of all speech, because the meaning that warrants the name 'Speaker' is not present in created speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time you described God using a name or attribute in a way that might have implied a similarity to creation. What was that name, and how does this lesson challenge that understanding? Are you willing to humble your intellect before revelation and let God define Himself?
?
If God is not called 'Speaker' of every speech, what does that mean for how you approach the Quran? Do you treat it as merely a created book, or do you experience it as the very speech of God, distinct from all other speech? Let this shift how you listen to it today.
?
The passage teaches that the meaning that warrants the name 'Creator' is present in God, but the meaning for 'Speaker' is not in created speech. This means there is a part of God's essence we cannot comprehend through His creation. Does your heart rest in the mystery of God, or do you try to contain Him in human categories? Commit to one specific way you will trust His uniqueness today.

The Absurdity of Demanding 'Huwa' in Every Name
Maturidi points out the absurdity of demanding 'Huwa' in every name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why demanding the explicit pronoun 'Huwa' in universal names is logically absurd, as shown by Maturidi's analogy.

Core Idea
Requiring 'Huwa' in every name would lead to absurdities, as shown by scholars, lords, and kings.

Maturidi begins his refutation by calling the objection 'strange'. He points out that if one demanded the pronoun 'Huwa' in every absolute name, one would have to mention it when referring to scholars, doers, lords, and kings—which is clearly nonsensical. This lesson examines that initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic phrase where the objector claims that since 'Huwa' is not mentioned in 'Lord of everything', not everything is created by God.
2
Maturidi's first response
Notice how Maturidi calls this 'strange' and asks when 'Huwa' is ever mentioned in absolute names.
This is a rhetorical question highlighting the impracticality.
3
The absurd analogy
See how Maturidi extends the logic: if the objector were consistent, he would also require 'Huwa' when mentioning scholars, doers, lords, and kings.
This shows the objection is self-defeating.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the objection is based on a flawed expectation that does not hold in normal language or reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And his saying: 'He (Huwa) has not entered into it' is strange. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the doers, the guardians, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. Moreover, even if it were mentioned—though that is impossible in reason—in the thing, it would not permit the exclusion of other than it by its exclusion, for reasons: First, His saying: 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'; it is not permissible to exclude anything from that and specify it in creation so that the understanding of what is meant would be nullified because 'Huwa' has not entered into it. Second, it is praise, and in its exclusion is its fall; for it is praise by making everything under power, and actualizing in everything servitude, and actualizing that nullifies that. And Allah is the grantor of success. Third, the statement directed to another regarding the act, lordship, and the like returns to that other. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'except me', and specification would not be like it, so the first is like it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector (likely al-Ka'bi) claimed that the verse 'Lord of everything' does not include the pronoun 'Huwa' (He), implying that maybe not everything is created by God. Maturidi responds by calling this claim 'strange'. He asks: when is 'Huwa' used in absolute names of things? If that were allowed, it would also have to be used when mentioning scholars, doers, guardians, lords, and kings—which is senseless. Then he gives three reasons why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned (though impossible), it would not permit exclusion: First, the verse itself already says 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'—so nothing can be excluded even if 'Huwa' is not repeated in the first part. Second, the verse is praise (imtidaah); if anything were excluded, the praise would fall because it is praise for bringing everything under power and servitude. Third, any statement that attributes an act or lordship to another person returns in its meaning to that person; thus if God said 'except me', that would not justify specification, so the first argument stands. The conclusion is that the universality of God's creation remains intact.

Key Terms
هو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absence of the pronoun 'Huwa' in a verse limits its meaning and allows for exceptions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that demanding 'Huwa' in every universal statement leads to absurdity and is not required by reason or language.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi call the objector's claim that 'Huwa' has not entered into it?
A) Strange
B) Reasonable
C) Praise
D) Clear

Key Takeaway
The objection that the omission of 'Huwa' allows exclusion is refuted by showing its logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a theological objection that seems small or linguistic, do you instantly reject it or examine its premises? How does Maturidi’s response teach you to handle doubts that seem trivial but could mislead your understanding of God?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Allah that you have never tested with logical consistency. Are you willing to apply the same reductio method to your own reasoning to see if it leads to absurdity? If it does, will you abandon it?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's argument as 'speech of one who does not understand' without first considering whether your own position holds up under logical extension? How can you ensure you are not the one failing to understand?

First and Second Reasons: Universal Verses and Praise
Maturidi gives the first two reasons for the non-exclusionary effect of omitted 'Huwa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first two reasons for why omitting 'Huwa' does not permit limiting God's creation: the universal verses and the praise aspect.

Core Idea
The universal verses exclude any specification, and the verse is praise that would collapse if anything were excluded.

In this lesson, we examine the first two reasons Maturidi gives for why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned (though impossible), it would not allow exclusion. The first reason appeals to other verses where 'Huwa' is explicitly mentioned universally. The second reason shows that the verse is praise, and excluding anything would negate that praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the first reason
Read where Maturidi quotes the verse: 'He is over everything a guardian, He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'. Notice the universality.
2
Understand the implication
Because these verses explicitly state 'He is over everything', no thing can be excluded even if 'Huwa' was omitted in the previous phrase.
3
Read the second reason
Maturidi says the verse is praise (imtidaah). If anything were excluded, the praise would be lost.
Praise requires encompassment.
4
Connect the reasons
Both reasons lead to the same conclusion: the universality of God's lordship and creation is intact.

Third Reason: The Implication of 'Except Me'
Maturidi gives the third reason and concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third reason: that any statement attributing an act or lordship to another implicitly returns to that other, so when God says 'except me', it does not justify specification.

Core Idea
If God said 'except me', it would not create a specification, so the first argument stands.

Maturidi's third reason is more subtle. He argues that when a statement directs an act or lordship to another, the meaning ultimately returns to that other. Thus, if God were to say 'except me', it would not create a valid specification, and the universal lordship remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the third reason
Read Maturidi's statement: 'The statement directed to another regarding the act, lordship, and the like returns to that other.'
2
Understand the logic
If someone says 'except me' in a context of universal lordship, it would be a self-reference that does not create a true exception.
3
Final conclusion
Maturidi concludes that specification is not possible, so the first argument (the universal verses) stands.

Source Text

The Impossibility of Anything Outside God's Creation
Maturidi establishes the principle that any exception to God's creation would destroy divine praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that if anything were outside God's creation, divine praise would be invalidated.

Core Idea
Every exception to God's creation makes Him a partner in His own praise.

Maturidi begins by warning against any thought that something could exist independently of God's creative act. He shows that such an exception would unravel all divine titles of praise, because every title would then be shared.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage from the start to 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Notice the logical chain: if one exception is allowed, then the same exception applies to all names of praise.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Connect to the concept of praise
Maturidi says that if God had partners in the meaning of His attributes, He would have no exclusive praise. Think about why divine praise requires that God alone be the true possessor of those attributes.
This principle is foundational for tawhid.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Consider the vast implication: the very possibility of something being outside God's creation would erode all divine praise and majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز خروج شيء من أن يكون هو له خالقا لجاز مثله عن الملك والربوبية ونحو ذلك من أسماء الامتداح، فيبطل أن يكون له مدح بشيء، لما في كل شيء له شركاء في حقيقة معناه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول.
And if it were permissible for anything to come out from being such that He is its Creator, then the like would be permissible concerning [the names] King, Lord, and similar names of praise, and thus it would invalidate that He has praise through anything, because in everything He has partners in the reality of its meaning. And there is no power except with God. And his saying: 'He did not enter 'Huwa' ('He') into it' is amazing. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the agents, the deputies, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. Moreover, if it were mentioned – even if that is impossible in reason – in the thing, it would not be permissible for others to come out by its coming out, for several reasons: first, His saying: 'And He is over all things a Guardian, and He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'; it is not permissible for anything to come out from that and be specified in creation, so as to invalidate the knowledge of the intended because 'Huwa' did not enter into it. Second, that it is praise, and in its coming out is its downfall; for it is praise for having placed everything under power and realized servitude in everything, and realizing that entails nullifying that. And God is the Granter of success. Third, that the saying directed by the act to another, and lordship and the like, refers back to that other. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'other than Me', and specification is not like that, so it is like the first.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument against an opponent who claims that some things (like acts of disobedience) are outside God's creation because the word 'Huwa' (He) is not explicitly mentioned in descriptions of those things. Maturidi first states a general principle: if it were possible for anything to be outside God's creative power, then the same exception could be made for God's names like King and Lord, which would destroy all divine praise because everything would have partners in the meaning of those names. He then turns to the opponent's specific claim about 'Huwa', calling it absurd. He asks when 'Huwa' is ever used in naming things absolutely; if it were, it would also apply to human scholars, agents, lords, and kings—which is clearly nonsensical. Then he gives three reasons why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned in a thing, that would not allow excluding other things from God's power. First, the Quran explicitly says God is Guardian, Lord, and God of everything, so nothing can be specified out. Second, these names are praise, and excluding anything from them would negate that praise because they imply universal power and servitude. Third, if you attribute lordship and actions to something else, that attribution refers to that other entity, so if you say 'other than Me', specification fails. Thus the opponent's use of 'Huwa' does not help his case.

Key Terms
خالق (Creator)
ملك (King)
رب (Lord)
امتداح (praise)

C) They would apply only to the things within creation.
D) They would become metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Allowing any exception to God's creation would destroy the exclusive praise due to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Invalidity of the Opponent's Use of 'Huwa'
Maturidi refutes the opponent's use of 'Huwa' with a reductio and three logical points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three-fold refutation of the opponent's argument that the word 'Huwa' ('He') can be used to exclude things from God's creation.

Core Idea
The opponent's appeal to 'Huwa' is absurd and self-defeating.

Having established the general principle, Maturidi now addresses the specific argument of his opponent, who claims that because the word 'Huwa' is not explicitly mentioned in descriptions of certain things (like acts of disobedience), those things fall outside God's creation. Maturidi demolishes this claim by pointing out its absurd implications and then providing three logical reasons why even if 'Huwa' were used, it would not help.

Guided Path
1
Read the absurdity of 'Huwa'
Read the part from 'وقوله: لم يدخل هو فيه عجيب' to 'كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول'. Notice how Maturidi shows that using 'Huwa' absolutely would apply to humans as well, which is nonsensical.
This is a reductio ad absurdum on the opponent's own method.
2
Study the three reasons
Read the three reasons given after 'وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر'. Each reason shows why even if 'Huwa' were used in a thing, other things cannot be excluded.
The reasons are based on Quranic texts and logical consistency.
3
Summarise the argument
Maturidi concludes that the opponent's use of 'Huwa' fails to achieve his goal. Understand how each reason builds on the previous.
The third reason ties back to the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز خروج شيء من أن يكون هو له خالقا لجاز مثله عن الملك والربوبية ونحو ذلك من أسماء الامتداح، فيبطل أن يكون له مدح بشيء، لما في كل شيء له شركاء في حقيقة معناه. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله: «لم يدخل «هو» فيه عجيب. متى يُذكر «هو» في اسم الأشياء بالإطلاق؟ ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن يذكر في ذكر العلماء وذكر الفاعلين وذكر الوكلاء والأرباب والملوك، وذلك كلام من لا يعقل ما يقول. وبعد، فلو كان يُذكر - وإن كان ممتنعا ذلك في العقل - في الشيء لم يجز خروج غيره بخروجه لوجوه أحدها قوله: وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وهو رب كل شيء، وإله كل شيء؛ لم يجز خروج شيء من ذلك وتخصيصه في الخلق ليبطل معرفة المراد من حيث لم يدخل «هو» فيه. والثاني أنه امتداح، وفي خروجه سقوطه؛ إذ هو امتداح بما صير كل شيء تحت القدرة، وحقق في كل العبودة، وتحقيق ذلك فيه إبطال ذلك. والله الموفق. والثالث أن القول المصروف بالفعل إلى آخر والربوبية ونحو ذلك راجع إلى ذلك الآخر. وإذا كان كذلك فكأنه قال: «سواي»، ولم يكن بمثله التخصيص، فمثله الأول.
And if it were permissible for anything to come out from being such that He is its Creator, then the like would be permissible concerning [the names] King, Lord, and similar names of praise, and thus it would invalidate that He has praise through anything, because in everything He has partners in the reality of its meaning. And there is no power except with God. And his saying: 'He did not enter 'Huwa' ('He') into it' is amazing. When is 'Huwa' mentioned in the name of things absolutely? And if this were permissible, it would be permissible to mention it in mentioning the scholars, the agents, the deputies, the lords, and the kings, and that is the speech of one who does not understand what he says. Moreover, if it were mentioned – even if that is impossible in reason – in the thing, it would not be permissible for others to come out by its coming out, for several reasons: first, His saying: 'And He is over all things a Guardian, and He is the Lord of everything, and the God of everything'; it is not permissible for anything to come out from that and be specified in creation, so as to invalidate the knowledge of the intended because 'Huwa' did not enter into it. Second, that it is praise, and in its coming out is its downfall; for it is praise for having placed everything under power and realized servitude in everything, and realizing that entails nullifying that. And God is the Granter of success. Third, that the saying directed by the act to another, and lordship and the like, refers back to that other. And if that is so, it is as if He said: 'other than Me', and specification is not like that, so it is like the first.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument against an opponent who claims that some things (like acts of disobedience) are outside God's creation because the word 'Huwa' (He) is not explicitly mentioned in descriptions of those things. Maturidi first states a general principle: if it were possible for anything to be outside God's creative power, then the same exception could be made for God's names like King and Lord, which would destroy all divine praise because everything would have partners in the meaning of those names. He then turns to the opponent's specific claim about 'Huwa', calling it absurd. He asks when 'Huwa' is ever used in naming things absolutely; if it were, it would also apply to human scholars, agents, lords, and kings—which is clearly nonsensical. Then he gives three reasons why even if 'Huwa' were mentioned in a thing, that would not allow excluding other things from God's power. First, the Quran explicitly says God is Guardian, Lord, and God of everything, so nothing can be specified out. Second, these names are praise, and excluding anything from them would negate that praise because they imply universal power and servitude. Third, if you attribute lordship and actions to something else, that attribution refers to that other entity, so if you say 'other than Me', specification fails. Thus the opponent's use of 'Huwa' does not help his case.

Key Terms
هو (Huwa)
خالق (Creator)
ملك (King)
رب (Lord)
امتداح (praise)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the word 'Huwa' is a legitimate argument that some things are not created by God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'Huwa' is not used absolutely for anything, and even if it were, it would not allow excluding anything from God's universal lordship.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's first reason why even if 'Huwa' were used in a thing, it would not allow excluding other things?
A) Because the Quran says God is over all things a Guardian and Lord of everything.
B) Because 'Huwa' cannot be used in Arabic.
C) Because the opponent does not understand what he says.
D) Because the act refers to another.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's appeal to 'Huwa' fails because it is absurd and, even if conceded, does not support his claim.
