The First Aspect: Uniqueness of Power
Maturidi presents the initial objection and the first aspect: uniqueness of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the opponent's own argument against dualists to refute the claim that human power resembles divine power.

Core Idea
The argument of uniqueness (الانفراد) applies equally to the opponent.

Maturidi first presents the opponent's objection that affirming power for humans is tashbih. He then offers a preliminary reply, but his deeper analysis reveals a self-contradiction in the opponent's position. The opponent had previously used the uniqueness of divine power to refute the dualists; now that same argument returns against him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: objection, reply, then Maturidi's analysis.
2
Identify the first aspect
Find the phrase 'أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة' and understand its meaning: the uniqueness of power.
3
Recognize the self-contradiction
The opponent used the uniqueness argument against dualists; now he must face it regarding human power. The text says: 'وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا.'
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this argument work? Because if human power were truly independent, it would challenge God's unique power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر سؤال خصمه من وجه لا يحتمل خصمه أن يقول به، فقال: إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها فقال: لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به، وهو يقدر لا بغيره، كما يقال في العلم. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: لو قدرت بالله لم يجز أن تزول بقدرتك قدرة الله، كما إذ علمت به لم يزل بعلمك علم الله به وبعد، فإن السؤال من وجهين. أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة، وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا. والثاني أن ذلك يوجب الغنى عن الله في الفعل قبل وجوده، ولا يجوز أن يكون الله يُغنى أحداً عن نفسه. فإن قلت: يحتاج إليه في الإبقاء أحلت عندك، لأنها لا تحتمل. وإن قلت: يُحدث أخرى، فقد أغناه عنه في وقت، ولو جاز ذلك في الوقت مع قيام العبودة يجوز أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned his opponent's question in a manner that his opponent cannot say, and said: 'When you affirm power for yourselves, you have resembled Allah with it.' He said: 'It is not necessary, because you have power by which you act, while He (Allah) is powerful not by something other than Himself, as is said regarding knowledge.' Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'If you had power by Allah, it would not be permissible that your power removes Allah's power, just as when you know by Him, your knowledge does not remove Allah's knowledge of Him and after. Then the question has two aspects. One is the uniqueness of power, and by this you argued in refuting the statement of the Dualists, so it applies to you here. The second is that it entails independence from Allah in the act before its existence, and it is not permissible that Allah makes someone independent of Himself. If you say: He needs it for preservation, you would be stumped, because it does not persist. And if you say: He creates another (power), then He has made him independent of Him in that time, and if that is permissible in one time despite the establishment of servitude, it would be permissible forever. And there is no power except with Allah.'
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that affirming a power for humans is a form of tashbih (likening creation to the Creator). The opponent asserts that if humans have power, they resemble Allah. Maturidi first quotes a possible reply: divine power is not through another, whereas human power is. But then Maturidi gives a deeper answer: if human power were from Allah, it would not negate Allah's power, just as knowledge from Allah does not negate divine knowledge. He then analyzes the objection from two angles. First, the claim of uniqueness of power (الانفراد بالقدرة) – the opponent himself used this argument against the Dualists, so it now applies against his own position. Second, affirming an independent human power would imply independence from Allah in the act before its occurrence, which violates tawhid. Maturidi then considers two possible defenses: (1) that the power needs Allah for preservation – but powers are accidents (أعراض) and do not persist, so this fails; (2) that Allah creates a new power each time – but that would still grant independence in that moment, and if allowed once, it would be allowed always, destroying the concept of servitude (عبودية). The argument concludes with 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with Allah).

Key Terms
قدرة
انفراد
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human power is completely independent from God and thus resembles divine power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human power is not independent; it is given by God and does not rival His unique power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does the opponent's argument against the Dualists apply to the opponent himself?
A) The opponent used the uniqueness of power to refute dualists, now it applies to his own claim about human power.
B) The opponent denied human power, but dualists affirmed it.
C) The opponent claimed power is an accident.
D) The opponent used analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The uniqueness of divine power (الانفراد) is a key argument that the opponent cannot evade without contradicting himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when do you feel most in control—as if your power is entirely your own? How would your actions change if you constantly reminded yourself that your power is never independent of God?
?
Consider a moment when you took full credit for an achievement. Did you actually believe your effort alone caused it? What would it look like to genuinely attribute that success to God's power while still acknowledging your own role?
?
The opponent in this passage used an argument against others but failed to apply it to himself. Are there areas in your faith or practice where you hold others to a standard but make exceptions for yourself? Will you commit today to recognizing one such blind spot?

The Second Aspect: Independence from Allah
Maturidi discusses the second aspect: independence from Allah, and refutes two defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming an independent human power would imply independence from Allah in the act, and how Maturidi refutes two possible counterarguments.

Core Idea
If human power were independent even for a moment, it would destroy servitude.

Maturidi now turns to the second aspect of the objection: that an independent human power would make humans independent of Allah in their actions before those actions occur. This directly contradicts tawhid. Maturidi then anticipates two possible defenses and shows their failure, concluding that no power exists except with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the second aspect and the two defenses that Maturidi refutes.
2
Understand the second aspect
The claim is that affirming human power leads to independence from Allah in the act before its existence.
3
Analyze the first defense
The opponent says power needs Allah for preservation. Maturidi replies that power is an accident and does not persist.
4
Analyze the second defense
The opponent says Allah creates a new power each time. Maturidi replies that this grants temporary independence, and if allowed once, it would be allowed forever.
5
Conclude with the final declaration
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except with Allah), summarizing total dependence.

Then he mentioned his opponent's question in a manner that his opponent cannot say, and said: 'When you affirm power for yourselves, you have resembled Allah with it.' And there is no power except with Allah.'

The opponent asserts that if humans have power, they resemble Allah. Maturidi first quotes a possible reply: divine power is not through another, whereas human power is. Misconception: If Allah creates power in humans, humans can act independently without Allah's direct involvement.
Correction: Maturidi argues that any power, even if created, is not independent; it is always dependent on Allah for its existence and the act's occurrence. The idea of independence even for a moment is invalid.

The Opponent's Objection on Power Resembling God and the Two Aspects
Maturidi refutes the opponent's objection regarding resemblance of human power to God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical method in refuting the claim that human power resembles God's power, and the two aspects of the objection.

Core Idea
Human power is derived from God, so resemblance does not diminish God's unique power.

Maturidi presents an opponent's challenge: if humans have power, they resemble God. In this passage, he refutes the objection by distinguishing between derived and essential power, and exposes two problematic aspects of the claim. This lesson unpacks the dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed dialectical style.
2
Identify the opponent's objection and Maturidi's initial counter
Spot the objection ('إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها') and the initial response ('لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به'). Understand the analogy with knowledge.
3
Understand the two aspects of the objection
Maturidi says the question has two aspects: uniqueness in power (الانفراد بالقدرة) and independence from God (الغنى عن الله). See how each is argued.
The first aspect turns the opponent's own argument against dualists back on them.
4
Analyze the principle that power exists only for an act
Maturidi states the foundational principle: 'القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل' (power cannot exist except for an act). Understand how this refutes the idea of power preceding the act.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that reason forces abandonment of the doctrine of precedence. Consider how this argument defends divine uniqueness and the dependent nature of human power.
This lesson shows the sophistication of Maturidi's theological method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر سؤال خصمه من وجه لا يحتمل خصمه أن يقول به، فقال: إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها فقال : لا يجب ذا لما قدرت به، وهو يقدر لا بغيره، كما يقال في العلم. { قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } لو قدرت بالله لم يجز أن تزول بقدرتك قدرة الله ، كما إذ علمت به لم يزل بعلمك علم الله به وبعد، فإن السؤال من وجهين. أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة، وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية، فيلزمك في هذا . والثاني أن ذلك يوجب الغنى عن الله في الفعل قبل وجوده ، ولا يجوز أن يكون الله يُغنى أحداً عن نفسه. فإن قلت: يحتاج إليه في الإبقاء أحلت عندك ، لأنها لا تحتمل. وإن قلت: يُحدث أخرى، فقد أغناه عنه في وقت، ولو جاز ذلك في الوقت مع قيام العبودة يجوز أبدا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . والأصل أن القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل، وكذلك العجز لا عن فعل. ثم قد يجوز أن يكون قادرًا في وقت للفعل، يعجز في الوقت الثاني، إذ معلوم وجود مثله؛ فيكون الله تعالى معطيّا القوة لشيء يستحيل كونه ، وفي ذلك فساد كون القوة للفعل. فألزم ما أوجبه العقل أنها لا تكون إلا للفعل إحالـة القول بالتقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned the question of his opponent in a manner that his opponent could not possibly say, so he said: Since you affirm for yourselves power, you have resembled God by it. He said: This is not necessary because of what you are empowered by, and He empowers not by other than Himself, just as is said regarding knowledge. {Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} If you were empowered by God, it would not be possible for God's power to be removed by your power, just as when you know by Him, God's knowledge of it is not removed by your knowledge, and afterwards, the question is from two aspects. One is uniqueness in power, and by it you argued in refuting the dualists, so it applies to you here. The second is that it requires independence from God in the act before its existence, and it is not permissible for God to make anyone independent of Himself. If you say: He needs Him for preservation, you are inconsistent according to you, because it does not persist. And if you say: He creates another, then He has made him independent of Him at a time, and if that is permissible at a time while servitude remains, it is permissible forever. And there is no power except through God. The principle is that it is impossible for power to exist not for an act, and likewise inability not from an act. Then it is possible for one to be powerful at a time for an act and be incapable at the second time, since the existence of its like is known; so God, exalted, would be granting power to something impossible for it to exist, and in that is the corruption of power being for the act. So what reason necessitated—that it cannot be except for the act—compels the abandonment of the doctrine of precedence. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. Maturidi first recounts an opponent's objection: if you (Muslims) affirm that humans have power (qudra), you make them resemble God, who is the ultimate powerful. The response is that this does not follow because human power is derived from God ('what you are empowered by' refers to the power granted by God) while God's power is unique. Maturidi then elaborates that the opponent's question has two aspects: (1) uniqueness in power—the same argument used against dualists can be turned against the objector; (2) the implication of independence from God in the act before existence—which is impossible because every creature needs God. He then examines possible replies from the opponent: if one says the creature needs God for preservation of power, that is inconsistent because power is an accident and does not persist; if the adversary says God creates a new power each time, then the creature is independent at that moment, which if allowed once could be allowed forever. Maturidi then states the foundational principle: power cannot exist except for an act, and inability cannot exist except regarding an act. He concludes that reason demands that power is only for the act, thereby refuting the theory of 'precedence' (taqaddum, likely the idea that power precedes the act).

Key Terms
القدرة
العلم
الانفراد
الغنى
الأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'power' when applied to humans implies a similarity in essence and reality to God's power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that human power is derived from God and does not diminish or resemble God's power in its essential attributes, such as uniqueness and independence.

Checkpoint
What are the two aspects of the opponent's question that Maturidi identifies?
A) Uniqueness in power and independence from God.
B) Resemblance to God and denial of prophecy.
C) Creation of power and its persistence.
D) Power before the act and after the act.

Key Takeaway
Human power does not resemble God's power because it is derived from Him and is only for an act, thus preserving divine uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you silently credit your own effort and ability, or do you truly feel that God's power was the sole enabler? What would change in your heart if you internalized that your power is merely a created, dependent gift? Today, before you start any task, consciously remind yourself: 'لا قوة إلا بالله'—and see if it shifts your sense of self-reliance.
?
The lesson shows that even a momentary feeling of independence from God is logically unsustainable and contradicts servitude. Where in your life have you nurtured a pocket of independence—perhaps in your skills, relationships, or daily routines—that you have not surrendered to God? What is one concrete step you can take today to acknowledge your utter need for Him in that area?
?
If your power exists only at the moment of the act and is directly given by God, then your very ability to perform a good deed is a fresh creation from Him. How should this reshape your gratitude and humility? Will you take time after your next prayer to thank God not just for the act, but for the very capacity to do it?

God's Power and Knowledge: Answering Objections
Maturidi presents and answers two objections about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between God's power to create and His power over His own knowledge, and how Maturidi refutes Husayn's argument about God's knowledge of hypotheticals.

Core Idea
God's power does not extend to changing His own knowledge — it is eternal and fixed.

Maturidi continues his defense of divine attributes by addressing two further objections. The first concerns a logical implication: if God has power to create the world, does that mean He could also nullify His own knowledge? The second objection, attributed to Husayn al-Najjar, challenges the relationship between God's knowledge and free human acts. This lesson unpacks both arguments and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the two objections introduced by 'ثم عارض'.
The first objection is about God's power, the second is from Husayn.
2
Identify each objection and its refutation
Find where Maturidi presents the objection and where he implies the answer. The first objection is refuted by analogy with the first case; the second by affirming that God's knowledge covers all possibilities.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Why can't God's power nullify His knowledge? Because divine attributes are distinct and eternal. Understanding this strengthens the broader kalam framework.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليُحال ، من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه، فمثله الأول. ثم عارض حسينًا بالإطلاق، إنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان . أتقول في أنه أطلق إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال: الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله .
Then he objected with God's power to create the world, that it would lead to [something] impossible, without it being permissible to describe [God] with power to nullify His knowledge — so it is like the first. Then he objected regarding Husayn's general statement: he made a general statement between him and belief. Do you say that he made a general statement of nullifying God's knowledge? Then he said: God knows how it would be if it were, so if it were, it would not leave God's knowledge.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage contains two further objections. The first objection is about God's own power: if God has power to create the world, does that imply He also has power to nullify His own knowledge? The answer is that it is similar to the first case — God's power to create does not entail power over His knowledge. The second objection is attributed to Husayn (likely Husayn al-Najjar). He made a general statement linking Pharaoh and belief, arguing that if God knows how something would be if it occurred, then it would still be within His knowledge even if it never occurs. The answer is implicit: God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; what He knows will not occur cannot be changed.

Key Terms
قدرة الله
إنشاء العالم
علم الله
حسين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power to create implies He could also change His knowledge.
Correction: God's power does not extend to His own attributes; His knowledge is eternal and unchanging, as shown by analogy with the first objection.

Checkpoint
What does the objection about God's power to create the world imply?
A) That God can nullify His knowledge.
B) That God's power is limited.
C) That God's knowledge is not eternal.
D) That God cannot create.

Key Takeaway
God's power and knowledge are distinct attributes; power to create does not imply power over knowledge, and God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities without change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly wish that God's knowledge of your past sins could be erased or changed, as if He didn't know? How does this passage's insistence that His knowledge is fixed and unchanging confront that desire? What does it mean for you to trust that His knowledge is perfect and you cannot escape it, nor do you need to?
?
The passage says God knows how things would be if they were—even things that never happen. When you look at your own life, what 'if only' scenarios do you hold onto? Are you willing to release them, knowing that God's knowledge of what could have been is already complete and good? What do you need to surrender today?

Refutation of the Foreknowledge Objection
Maturidi refutes an objection about God's foreknowledge of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's foreknowledge of belief compels belief, by exposing the dilemma and affirming the immutability of divine knowledge.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge does not compel; the objection collapses on a false dilemma.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi responds to a common objection against predestination: if God already knows who will believe, then belief is compelled. Maturidi dismantles this objection by showing it leads to absurdity, either making God ignorant or creating a contradiction. He also emphasizes that God's knowledge is unchangeable, so the objection is misplaced. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the gist of the argument before diving into commentary.
Pay attention to the structure: dilemma, responses, conclusion.
2
Identify the dilemma presented.
Find where Maturidi asks: does God know that the person will not believe or not? This is the core fork of the argument.
The two horns are 'Yes' or 'No'.
3
Follow the refutation of each horn.
See how each answer leads to a problem: saying 'No' implies ignorance, saying 'Yes' creates a contradiction because God's knowledge says he won't believe, yet belief is demanded.
The phrase 'سفّهه' (makes him foolish) is key for the first horn.
4
Understand the immutability point.
Notice the final clause: 'if he believed, he would not exit from His knowledge'. This shows that God's knowledge is unchanging and encompasses all possibilities, so the objection fails.
The phrase 'ولقد ذكرت' refers back to an earlier argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: يعلم أنه آمن كيف يكون، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه، لأنه مع علمه بذلك يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، سفّهه، وإن قال: نعم، قيل: في ذلك وقعت المطالبة، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه، فكيف لم يخرج، وعلمه أنه لا يكون، وقد كان؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And his statement: He knows that he believed, how is that? This is a meaning with no benefit, because with His knowledge of that, does He know that he will not believe or not? If he says: No, he makes him foolish; if he says: Yes, it is said: in that fell the demand, and I mentioned that if he believed, he would not exit from His knowledge, so how did he not exit, while His knowledge that it will not be had already existed? There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about God's foreknowledge. The objection is that if God knows that a person will believe, then that belief is inevitable. The response is that this kind of knowledge is meaningless because God knows whether that person will believe or not. If the objector says God does not know whether he will believe, then that makes God ignorant; if he says God knows, then the problem is that God knows that he will not believe, so the demand that he should believe is contradictory. The last part mentions that if he were to believe, he would not leave God's knowledge, so how could he leave it? So the argument is that God's knowledge is unchangeable, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
علم
إيمان
علمه
سفّه
مطالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge of a person's belief means that belief is compelled and the person has no free will.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's knowledge does not compel; rather, the objection is based on a false dilemma. God's knowledge is immutable and encompasses what will happen, but that knowledge does not cause the action.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma Maturidi presents to refute the foreknowledge objection?
A) God does not know whether the person will not believe, which would imply ignorance.
B) God knows that the person will believe, so belief is compelled.
C) The objector is foolish for raising the objection.
D) Belief is beneficial regardless of God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the objection from foreknowledge is incoherent: God's knowledge is perfect and immutable, so it does not create compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for sin by saying 'Allah already knows what I will do,' how does Maturidi's argument expose the fallacy in that thinking? What will you tell yourself next time that thought arises?
?
If you truly internalized that God's foreknowledge does not compel your actions, which area of your life would you immediately stop treating as 'fate' and start taking responsibility for?
?
The objectors in this passage used God's knowledge to justify their refusal to believe. Are there any truths you avoid acting on because you feel 'it won't make a difference'? How can Maturidi's refutation push you to act now?

Refuting the Claim that Powerlessness Removes Blame
Maturidi rejects the claim that lack of power removes blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that lack of power does not remove blame but rather increases it, and why the objection fails.

Core Idea
Lack of power does not remove blame; it amplifies it.

Maturidi responds to an objection that if a person had no ability, they wouldn't be blameworthy. In this passage, he turns the argument on its head, showing that the person is even more blameworthy for misusing the power they possess. He also clarifies why the distinction between power and faith does not save the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find the statement that is being refuted: 'If he had no power over it, he would not be blameworthy.' Notice how Maturidi frames it as similar to a previous argument.
The objection is about power (qudra) and blame (lawm).
2
Understand Maturidi's core response
Maturidi argues that the person is actually more blameworthy because he wasted the power he had. Focus on the phrase: 'بل عليه أعظم اللائمة، لما هو ضيّع القدرة'.
This reverses the objector's logic.
3
Examine the secondary refutations
Maturidi addresses two further points: the objector's claim that the conclusion doesn't follow because power is not faith, and the reliance on command (amr). Note how he turns each argument against the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله: «لولم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما» فهو مثل القول سواء، ودليله أنه قوله. بل عليه أعظم اللائمة، لما هو ضيّع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذي به يأتيه. وقوله «لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الإيمان يُدفع أيضاً، فما فيه ما يمنع هذا اللزوم؛ بل إنما لزم ذلك لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد لأنه استعباد به يظهر ذله وعبوديته، والقوة هي الغنى والعلو والرفعة؛ فهو الوجه الذي به يبطل ربوبية غير الله، وليس في الأمر ذلك. على أنه لو لم يكن أمر ولا نهى كان القول بـيؤمن ويكفر ويقدر ولا يقدر» لا معنى له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for his statement: 'If he had no power over it, he would not be blameworthy', it is like the statement (previously considered), and his proof is his statement. Rather, upon him is greater blame, because he wasted the power when he turned away from that by which it comes to him. And his statement: 'It does not follow us because power is not faith' is also rebutted, for there is nothing in it to prevent this following; rather, it follows precisely because power is not faith. Then his consideration of command is corrupt because it is an enslavement by which his lowliness and servitude appear, while power is self-sufficiency, highness, and elevation. So it is the aspect by which the lordship of other than Allah is invalidated, but it is not in command. Moreover, if there were no command or prohibition, the statement 'he believes and disbelieves and is able and not able' would have no meaning. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that if a person had no power (ability) to do an act, he would not be blameworthy. The author argues that, on the contrary, the person is more blameworthy for wasting his power and turning away from what is required. The objector claims that the conclusion does not follow because power is different from faith, but the author replies that it is precisely because power is different from faith that the blame follows. Then the author critiques the reliance on command (أمر) as a basis for obligation, arguing that command implies subjugation, whereas power implies glory and lordship. Finally, he notes that without command and prohibition, the categories of belief/disbelief and ability would be meaningless.

Key Terms
قدرة
ملوما
لائمة
إيمان
أمر
استعباد
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a person lacks the power to do an act, they cannot be blamed for not doing it.
Correction: Maturidi argues that lack of power does not remove blame; on the contrary, blame is greater when one wastes the power they have. The blameworthy act is turning away from the source of power (Allah).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does lack of power not remove blame?
A) Because blame is based on intention, not ability.
B) Because the person is more blameworthy for wasting the power they had.
C) Because power is irrelevant to moral responsibility.
D) Because command alone determines blame.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that blameworthiness increases when one misuses the power granted by Allah, and that the objection fails by conflating power with faith and misinterpreting command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How have you wasted the power Allah gave you—your time, health, intellect—by turning away from the One who granted it? Will you repent today for every ability misused?
?
When you say 'I can't' to an act of obedience, are you really unable, or have you simply chosen to turn away? What would it cost you to acknowledge that all power is from Allah and act accordingly?
?
If command and prohibition were removed, would your life still have meaning? Do your actions reflect that you are a servant, or do you live as if you are autonomous? What change must you make to live in constant reliance on 'there is no power except with Allah'?

Power Produces Action, Not Command
Maturidi defines the relationship between power, action, and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Islamic theology, divine power directly produces action, while command and empowerment have different roles.

Core Idea
Power's fruit is action; command alone does not create.

This lesson begins with a fundamental principle: power (qudra) brings about action, not command. Maturidi clarifies that empowerment (iqdar) elevates one to a position of strength and vicegerency, contrasting with the nature of command. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how divine attributes relate to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first paragraph of the source in Arabic. Notice the key terms 'قدرة' (power), 'فعل' (action), and 'أمر' (command).
The phrase 'ثمرتها الفعل' means 'its fruit is action'.
2
Understand the core claim
Maturidi states that power produces action, not command. Command is for that which is mentioned, but empowerment makes one a powerful vicegerent.
This refutes any notion that God's command alone suffices without power.
3
Reflect on the exception clause
The passage ends with 'كما إذا تم كان ربًا إلا' (as if when completed he is a lord except). This hints at the uniqueness of God's lordship.
The final phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' affirms all power belongs to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فإن القدرة ثمرتها الفعل، وبها يكون الذي ذكر، لا بالأمر؛ لذلك الأمر أمرًا بالذي ذكرت، فبالإقدار يصير مسلطا غنيا مستخلفًا، كما إذا تم كان ربًا إلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وبعد، فإنا عارضنا بالذي طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل، ولولا الأمر كان المؤول بالعقل وحشيا. فإما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه فلم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثاني. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته، ولا يُؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء. ثبت أن في الأمر ذلة واستعبادا، فهو لا يوجب ذلك، وفي الإقدار رفعة وعلو فهو يوجب. والله الموفق.

وبعد، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تُضَيَّع إلا فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يُقصد، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجود الفعل. وكم من أمر به لا ائتمار هنالك؛ فلذلك لم يجب به. وما ذكر في الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجري سلطانه فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلوّ الرتبة، وفي إيجاب ذلك لغيره نقض. ألا ترى أنه قد نقض قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت، ولا يُقبل قوله بمعارضة مثله في الله: إنه يقدر على ما علم أنه لا يفعل، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته، وبمثله
After that, the fruit of power is action, and by it occurs that which was mentioned, not by command; therefore the command is a command for that which was mentioned, and through empowerment one becomes powerful, rich, and a vicegerent, as if when completed, he is a lord except. There is no power except with Allah.

After that, we countered with that whose way of knowledge is the intellect from the aspect I mentioned, and command does not contradict what the intellect necessitates, and were it not for command, the interpretation by the intellect would be isolated. So either He knows the aspect of wisdom in the command or He does not know it; by that which we knew, it was not necessary to reject it because the second aspect is impossible for Him. There is no power except with Allah. And the matter of witnessed things supports what I mentioned: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic and great one is not commanded with anything. It is established that there is lowliness and enslavement in command, so it does not necessitate that; while in empowerment there is elevation and highness, so it necessitates. And Allah is the grantor of success.

After that, since there is no power that is wasted except that its existence necessitates the intended action, and the right of command is obligation, not existence of action. How many a command there is with no compliance; therefore, it is not necessitated by it. And what is mentioned regarding Allah—that He does it by His power, the execution of His will, and the flow of His authority—so His lordship is complete, and He merits majesty and elevation by His essence. It is not permissible that there be in Him what was mentioned of fear; rather, through it is the completion of wisdom and high rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a refutation. Do you not see that it has refuted the statement of the Dualists by what I mentioned, and his statement is not accepted by a similar objection regarding Allah: that He is able over what He knows He will not do, so He is able to nullify His lordship, and by similar to it.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This lesson is a complex argument comprising several points. First, it asserts that power (قدرة) produces action, not command (أمر). Empowerment (إقدار) makes one powerful and a vicegerent. Then, it argues that the intellect knows something about command, and command does not contradict reason; without command, rational interpretation would be isolated. The witness of observable things shows that the powerful are not commanded; command implies lowliness and subjection, while empowerment implies elevation. Therefore, in God, command cannot imply deficiency. Finally, it refutes the dualists by pointing out that God's power is over what He knows He will not do, and that does not nullify His lordship. The objection that God could nullify His own lordship is rejected.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
أمر
إقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'fruit of power' according to Maturidi?
A) Command
B) Action
C) Empowerment
D) Vicegerency

Key Takeaway
Power directly produces action; command and empowerment are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a task you feel powerless to complete, do you rely on your own imagined 'command'—your intention or verbal affirmation—or do you seek the actual power from Allah that alone produces action? Will you pause today to ask Allah for qudra before acting?
?
The passage says empowerment turns one into a vicegerent, but only 'as if' a lord. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the independent lord, forgetting that your authority is merely a gift and that 'there is no power except with Allah'? Write down one specific situation where you will consciously remind yourself of this dependence.
?
If power's fruit is action, then what does your lack of action in certain duties reveal about where you are actually placing your trust? What is the first step you will take today to align your reliance with Allah's power rather than your empty commands?

Intellect, Command, and the Witness of Creation
Maturidi defends the compatibility of command and reason, and uses observable creation to show the powerful are not commanded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how intellect and observed reality support that command does not contradict reason and that the powerful are not commanded.

Core Idea
Command does not oppose reason; the powerful are never commanded.

Maturidi now addresses a possible objection: does command contradict intellect? He argues that command aligns with reason, and without command, rational interpretation would be isolated. He then brings evidence from the witnessed world: the powerful are not commanded, for that implies lowliness, while empowerment indicates elevation. This reinforces the earlier point about God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Follow the argument structure
Read the second paragraph. Notice it has two parts: a rational argument about intellect and command, and a witnessed argument from the world.
The phrase 'عارضنا' indicates a counter-argument.
2
Understand the role of intellect
Maturidi says command does not contradict what intellect necessitates. If there were no command, rational interpretation would be 'وحشيا' (isolated/unconnected).
This shows command is not irrational.
3
Examine the witnessed evidence
He points to the fact that the powerful (قوى) are elevated by their power and are not commanded. Command implies lowliness (ذلة) and enslavement (استعباد), while empowerment implies elevation (رفعة).
This analogy supports that God, the Most Powerful, is not subject to command.

Commentary
This lesson is a complex argument comprising several points. First, it asserts that power (قدرة) produces action, not command (أمر). Empowerment (إقدار) makes one powerful and a vicegerent. Then, it argues that the intellect knows something about command, and command does not contradict reason; without command, rational interpretation would be isolated. The witness of observable things shows that the powerful are not commanded; command implies lowliness and subjection, while empowerment implies elevation. Therefore, in God, command cannot imply deficiency. Finally, it refutes the dualists by pointing out that God's power is over what He knows He will not do, and that does not nullify His lordship. The objection that God could nullify His own lordship is rejected.

Key Terms

أمر
شاهد
ذلة
رفعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think command contradicts reason or that God is subject to command like humans.
Correction: Maturidi shows command is not irrational and that in creation, the powerful are not commanded, so God, the Most Powerful, is beyond command.

Checkpoint
What does the witnessed world show about powerful beings and command?
A) They are commanded frequently.
B) They are elevated and not commanded.
C) They are both commanded and commanding.
D) Command brings them honor.

Key Takeaway
Command does not contradict reason; the powerful are not commanded, so God's command is not a sign of lowliness.

Will you now embrace submission as a sign of your lowliness before Him, rather than a loss of dignity?
?

Are you tempted to elevate your own will above His command? What would it take today for you to genuinely submit to His authority in an area you've been resisting?
?
Maturidi says without command, reason would be isolated (وحشيا). In what area of your life are you relying solely on your own reasoning, ignoring God's command? What would it cost you to trust His wisdom over your own? Will you take one concrete step this week to act on a command you've been neglecting?

Divine Attributes
beginner

Power, Will, and Refutation of Dualism
Maturidi argues that power necessitates action, and refutes dualist objections about God's power and lordship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power necessarily produces action, and that God's ability to not do something does not nullify His lordship, refuting dualist objections.

Core Idea
Power necessitates action; God's lordship is proven, not threatened.

In this final section, Maturidi deepens the argument: power, by its nature, produces its intended action. Command only obligates, but does not create action—many commands go unheeded. Regarding Allah, His power, will, and authority complete His lordship. He then refutes the dualists who argue that if God can do something He knows He will not do, then He could nullify His own lordship. Maturidi rejects this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the third paragraph
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the key point: 'لا توجد قدرة لا تُضَيَّع إلا فوجودها يوجب الفعل' — no power is wasted; its existence necessitates action.
This is a philosophical principle about power.
2
Distinguish power from command
Maturidi states that command only requires obligation (لزوم), not existence of action. Many commands are not obeyed.
This reinforces that God's power directly creates.
3
Apply to Allah's attributes
God's power, will, and authority make His lordship complete. He is free from fear, and His wisdom is perfect.
The phrase 'لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف' denies any deficiency.
4
Understand the dualist refutation
Dualists object that if God can do what He knows He won't, He could nullify His lordship. Maturidi says this is rejected because God's power does not contradict His knowledge.
The refutation uses the same principle of power necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فإن القدرة ثمرتها الفعل، وبها يكون الذي ذكر، لا بالأمر؛ لذلك الأمر أمرًا بالذي ذكرت، فبالإقدار يصير مسلطا غنيا مستخلفًا، كما إذا تم كان ربًا إلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وبعد، فإنا عارضنا بالذي طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل، ولولا الأمر كان المؤول بالعقل وحشيا. فإما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه فلم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثاني. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته، ولا يُؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء. ثبت أن في الأمر ذلة واستعبادا، فهو لا يوجب ذلك، وفي الإقدار رفعة وعلو فهو يوجب. والله الموفق.

وبعد، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تُضَيَّع إلا فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يُقصد، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجود الفعل. وكم من أمر به لا ائتمار هنالك؛ فلذلك لم يجب به. وما ذكر في الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجري سلطانه فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلوّ الرتبة، وفي إيجاب ذلك لغيره نقض. ألا ترى أنه قد نقض قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت، ولا يُقبل قوله بمعارضة مثله في الله: إنه يقدر على ما علم أنه لا يفعل، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته، وبمثله
After that, the fruit of power is action, and by it occurs that which was mentioned, not by command; therefore the command is a command for that which was mentioned, and through empowerment one becomes powerful, rich, and a vicegerent, as if when completed, he is a lord except. There is no power except with Allah.

After that, we countered with that whose way of knowledge is the intellect from the aspect I mentioned, and command does not contradict what the intellect necessitates, and were it not for command, the interpretation by the intellect would be isolated. So either He knows the aspect of wisdom in the command or He does not know it; by that which we knew, it was not necessary to reject it because the second aspect is impossible for Him. There is no power except with Allah. And the matter of witnessed things supports what I mentioned: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic and great one is not commanded with anything. It is established that there is lowliness and enslavement in command, so it does not necessitate that; while in empowerment there is elevation and highness, so it necessitates. And Allah is the grantor of success.

After that, since there is no power that is wasted except that its existence necessitates the intended action, and the right of command is obligation, not existence of action. How many a command there is with no compliance; therefore, it is not necessitated by it. And what is mentioned regarding Allah—that He does it by His power, the execution of His will, and the flow of His authority—so His lordship is complete, and He merits majesty and elevation by His essence. It is not permissible that there be in Him what was mentioned of fear; rather, through it is the completion of wisdom and high rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a refutation. Do you not see that it has refuted the statement of the Dualists by what I mentioned, and his statement is not accepted by a similar objection regarding Allah: that He is able over what He knows He will not do, so He is able to nullify His lordship, and by similar to it.

Commentary
This lesson is a complex argument comprising several points. First, it asserts that power (قدرة) produces action, not command (أمر). Empowerment (إقدار) makes one powerful and a vicegerent. Then, it argues that the intellect knows something about command, and command does not contradict reason; without command, rational interpretation would be isolated. The witness of observable things shows that the powerful are not commanded; command implies lowliness and subjection, while empowerment implies elevation. Therefore, in God, command cannot imply deficiency. Finally, it refutes the dualists by pointing out that God's power is over what He knows He will not do, and that does not nullify His lordship. The objection that God could nullify His own lordship is rejected.

The Harmony of Divine Command and Reason
Maturidi establishes harmony between divine command and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine command does not contradict reason and that rejecting what one does not comprehend is unjustified.

Core Idea
Divine command never contradicts reason; ignorance of wisdom is no grounds for rejection.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: the divine command (al-amr) is consistent with rational knowledge. He argues that if the wisdom behind a command is unknown, that does not warrant its dismissal. This lesson introduces the relationship between revelation and reason in Islamic theology, preparing you to read the passage where Maturidi counters those who might see a conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key claim about reason
Find where Maturidi says that the command does not contradict what reason necessitates. Highlight that sentence.
3
Reflect on the wisdom argument
Maturidi presents two possibilities: either one knows the wisdom or one does not. Consider why this leads to the conclusion that the command should not be rejected.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر أمرًا بالذي ذكر، فبالإقدار يصير مسلطا غنيا مستخلفًا، كما إذا تم كان ربًا إلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإنا عارضنا بالذي طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل، ولولا الأمر كان المؤول بالعقل وحشيا. فإما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه فلم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثاني. ولا قوة إلا بالله وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته، ولا يُؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء. ثبت أن في الأمر ذلة واستعبادا، فهو لا يوجب ذلك، وفي الإقدرا رفعة وعلو فهو يوجب. والله الموفق. وبعد، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تُضَيَّع إلا فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يُقصد، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجود الفعل. وكم من أمر به لا ائتمار هنالك؛ فلذلك لم يجب به. وما ذكر في الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجري سلطانه فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلوّ الرتبة، وفي إيجاب ذلك لغيره نقض. ألا ترى أنه قد نقض قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت، ولا يُقبل قوله بمعارضة مثله في الله: إنه يقدر على ما علم أنه لا يفعل، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته، وبمثله جعلوه في غيره معارضاً موجبا ذلك، فمثله الذي نحن فيه. ووجه آخر أنه لا يوجب الله قدرة ولا علمًا بقوله: «له قدرة بكذا وعلم بكذا» بلا معنى له، وذلك متحقق في غيره، فالمعارضة له لازمة. وأيضًا إن الله إذ هو قادر بذاته، عالم بذاته، فمحال وصفه بالذي ذكر، إذ بذلك تمت ربوبيته وجل سلطانه وألوهيته. وقد يكون الشيء الموصوف بذاته مما إذا ثبت لغيره عليه سلطان تجري قدرته عليه كالأعراض التي من مختلفات لأنفسهن، والأجسام لهن، أو الأجسام التي من قائمات بأنفسهن والأعراض بهن. ثم الله عليها سلطان وملك، فلو جعلنا على الله قدرة في إبطال تقديره ونقض تدبيره وإزالة علمه ونفي الحقيقة عن خبره لكان تحت قدرة غيره وفي سلطان آخر.. فتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا. مع ما الإطلاق يرجع إلى الأمر، وقد بيناه ومسألتان في القدرة على القدرية توجبان أن الله ليس بقادر بذاته. أحدهما أنهم قالوا: يقدر الله جل ثناؤه على حركات العباد وسكونهم، فلما أقدرهم على تلك الحركات والسكون
The command as a command by that which was mentioned, so by granting power he becomes empowered, independent, a viceregent, as if when completed he was a lord except. And there is no power except by Allah. Afterwards, we have countered by that whose path of knowledge is reason from the aspect which you mentioned, and the command does not contradict what reason necessitates, and were it not for the command, that which is interpreted by reason would be wild. Either one knows the aspect of wisdom in the command or one does not know it by that which we have known, so it was not necessary to repel it by what is difficult for him concerning the second aspect. And there is no power except by Allah, and supported what you mentioned the matter of the witnessed world: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic great one is not commanded with anything. It is established that in the command there is humiliation and enslavement, so it does not necessitate that, and in granting power there is elevation and highness, so it necessitates. And Allah is the giver of success. Afterwards, since there is no power that is wasted except that its existence necessitates the action which is intended, and the right of the command is obligation, not the existence of the action. How many a command is there with which there is no compliance there? Therefore it was not necessitated by it. And what was mentioned concerning Allah, it is by His power and the execution of His will and the flow of His sovereignty, so His lordship is completed and He merits majesty and highness by His essence, it is not permissible that there should be in Him what was mentioned of fear, rather by it is the perfection of wisdom and the highness of rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a contradiction. Do you not see that He has contradicted the saying of the Dualists by what you mentioned, and His saying is not accepted with a counter-argument like it concerning Allah: that He is able over what He knows He will not do, for then He would be able to contradict His lordship, and by its like they made in other than Him a counter-argument necessitating that, so its like is what we are in. And another aspect: that He does not necessitate power nor knowledge by His saying: 'He has power such-and-such and knowledge such-and-such' without meaning for Him, and that is actualized in other than Him, so the counter-argument is necessary for him. Also, Allah, since He is powerful by His essence, knowing by His essence, it is impossible to describe Him with what was mentioned, for by that His lordship is completed, His sovereignty is majestic, and His divinity. And sometimes the thing described by its essence, when for other than Him authority is established, His power runs over it, like the accidents which are of different kinds for themselves, and the bodies for them, or the bodies which are self-subsistent and the accidents are by them. Then Allah has authority and dominion over them. So if we were to establish over Allah power to nullify His decree and revoke His plan and remove His knowledge and negate the reality of His report, He would be under the power of another and under a different authority.. Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation. With what the absoluteness returns to the command, and we have explained it. And two issues concerning power against the Qadariyya which necessitate that Allah is not powerful by His essence. One of them: that they said: Allah, exalted be His praise, has power over the movements and stillness of the servants, and when He empowered them over those movements and stillness
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a sustained theological argument refuting the idea that God can be subject to command or that His power can be limited or compared to created powers. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by stating that the command (al-amr) is as previously mentioned: through granting power (al-iqdar), one becomes empowered and a vicegerent, but this does not apply to God. He then counters an opponent (likely Abu Ali al-Jubba'i or the Mu'tazila) by asserting that reason is the path to knowledge, and the divine command does not contradict reason. Without divine command, rational interpretation would be 'wild' (wahshiyyan). Maturidi argues that one either knows the wisdom in the command or does not; if one does not, he should not reject it based on what he cannot grasp. From the witnessed world, it is observed that the powerful are elevated by their power and cannot be commanded; command implies humiliation and enslavement (dhilla wa isti'bad), which does not apply to God, whereas granting power (iqdar) implies elevation ('uluww). Therefore, command does not necessitate action, as many commands go unheeded. What is mentioned of God is by His power, will, and sovereignty; His lordship is completed and He merits majesty by His essence. Fear cannot apply to Him; rather, in Him is perfect wisdom and high rank. To necessitate fear or limitation for another is a contradiction (naqd). Maturidi then uses the example of the Dualists (al-thunawiyya) to show that such a contradiction invalidates their view. He rejects the counter-argument that God has power over what He knows He will not do, as that would imply power to negate His own lordship—an impossibility. He then presents another aspect: God does not necessitate power or knowledge through a statement like 'He has power and knowledge' without meaning, as that is realized in others. For God, being powerful and knowing by essence, it is impossible to describe Him with such attributed limitations, for by His essence His lordship, sovereignty, and divinity are complete. Maturidi draws an analogy from accidents and bodies: accidents are different in themselves and bodies are self-subsistent with accidents, yet God has authority over them. If we were to posit a power over God to nullify His decree or remove His knowledge, He would be under another's power—impossible. The passage concludes by noting that absoluteness (al-itlaq) returns to the command, and then introduces two issues against the Qadariyya that would imply God is not powerful by His essence. The first issue is their claim that God has power over the movements and stillness of servants, but when He empowers them, those actions become theirs—leading to a contradiction if God's power is then considered limited.

Key Terms
الأمر
العقل
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine command may contradict reason, leading to irrationality in faith.
Correction: Maturidi argues that command does not contradict reason; it guides reason. If wisdom is unknown, rejection is not warranted.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between divine command and reason?
A) Divine command contradicts reason.
B) Divine command does not contradict reason.
C) Reason is unnecessary for understanding command.
D) Command is only for those without reason.

Key Takeaway
Divine command is in harmony with reason, and ignorance of its wisdom is not grounds for rejection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a divine command that you have found difficult to understand because its wisdom was hidden from you. Has that difficulty ever led you to hesitate or resent obeying? How does Maturidi's argument about the dilemma of knowledge confront your attitude today? Will you now commit to obedience even when understanding is absent, trusting that your ignorance is not a valid excuse for rejection?
?
When you read the phrase 'without the command, rational interpretation would be wild,' do you recognize areas in your life where your own reasoning has strayed because it was not anchored by divine command? What specific step can you take this week to bring your thinking under the authority of a command you once found puzzling?
?
Maturidi presents a stark choice: either know the wisdom or admit you do not. Which camp are you in regarding a command you currently struggle with? If you are in the 'do not know' camp, are you willing to stop seeking loopholes and instead surrender, believing that God's wisdom is greater than your comprehension? What concrete act of submission will you perform today to show that?

Command, Power, and the Transcendence of God
Maturidi distinguishes command from granting power using witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why command implies humiliation and cannot be attributed to God, while granting power implies elevation.

Core Idea
Command entails humility; God is too exalted to be commanded, but He grants power.

Maturidi now turns to the witnessed world to demonstrate that command and power have opposite implications. In creation, the powerful are not commanded, and command implies servitude. This lesson examines the distinction between command (al-amr) and granting power (al-iqdar), showing why only the latter befits God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the shift from theoretical reasoning to an argument from the witnessed world (al-shahid).
2
Analyze the witnessed world argument
Identify how Maturidi uses the example of powerful beings in creation to support his point.
He says the powerful are elevated and not commanded.
3
Understand the distinction
Focus on the contrast between command (containing humiliation) and granting power (containing elevation).
Command (al-amr) implies dhilla and isti'bad; iqdar implies rif'a and 'uluww.
4
Apply to God's attributes
See how Maturidi concludes that God's power, will, and sovereignty complete His lordship, and that fear cannot be ascribed to Him.

الأمر أمرًا بالذي ذكر، فبالإقدار يصير مسلطا غنيا مستخلفًا، كما إذا تم كان ربًا إلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإنا عارضنا بالذي طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل، ولولا الأمر كان المؤول بالعقل وحشيا. فإما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه فلم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثاني. ولا قوة إلا بالله وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته، ولا يُؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء. ثبت أن في الأمر ذلة واستعبادا، فهو لا يوجب ذلك، وفي الإقدرا رفعة وعلو فهو يوجب. والله الموفق. وبعد، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تُضَيَّع إلا فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يُقصد، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجود الفعل. وكم من أمر به لا ائتمار هنالك؛ فلذلك لم يجب به. وما ذكر في الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجري سلطانه فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلوّ الرتبة، وفي إيجاب ذلك لغيره نقض. ألا ترى أنه قد نقض قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت، ولا يُقبل قوله بمعارضة مثله في الله: إنه يقدر على ما علم أنه لا يفعل، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته، وبمثله جعلوه في غيره معارضاً موجبا ذلك، فمثله الذي نحن فيه. ووجه آخر أنه لا يوجب الله قدرة ولا علمًا بقوله: «له قدرة بكذا وعلم بكذا» بلا معنى له، وذلك متحقق في غيره، فالمعارضة له لازمة. وأيضًا إن الله إذ هو قادر بذاته، عالم بذاته، فمحال وصفه بالذي ذكر، إذ بذلك تمت ربوبيته وجل سلطانه وألوهيته. وقد يكون الشيء الموصوف بذاته مما إذا ثبت لغيره عليه سلطان تجري قدرته عليه كالأعراض التي من مختلفات لأنفسهن، والأجسام لهن، أو الأجسام التي من قائمات بأنفسهن والأعراض بهن. ثم الله عليها سلطان وملك، فلو جعلنا على الله قدرة في إبطال تقديره ونقض تدبيره وإزالة علمه ونفي الحقيقة عن خبره لكان تحت قدرة غيره وفي سلطان آخر.. فتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا. مع ما الإطلاق يرجع إلى الأمر، وقد بيناه ومسألتان في القدرة على القدرية توجبان أن الله ليس بقادر بذاته. أحدهما أنهم قالوا: يقدر الله جل ثناؤه على حركات العباد وسكونهم، فلما أقدرهم على تلك الحركات والسكون
The command as a command by that which was mentioned, so by granting power he becomes empowered, independent, a viceregent, as if when completed he was a lord except. And there is no power except by Allah. Afterwards, we have countered by that whose path of knowledge is reason from the aspect which you mentioned, and the command does not contradict what reason necessitates, and were it not for the command, that which is interpreted by reason would be wild. Either one knows the aspect of wisdom in the command or one does not know it by that which we have known, so it was not necessary to repel it by what is difficult for him concerning the second aspect. And there is no power except by Allah, and supported what you mentioned the matter of the witnessed world: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic great one is not commanded with anything. It is established that in the command there is humiliation and enslavement, so it does not necessitate that, and in granting power there is elevation and highness, so it necessitates. And Allah is the giver of success. Afterwards, since there is no power that is wasted except that its existence necessitates the action which is intended, and the right of the command is obligation, not the existence of the action. How many a command is there with which there is no compliance there? Therefore it was not necessitated by it. And what was mentioned concerning Allah, it is by His power and the execution of His will and the flow of His sovereignty, so His lordship is completed and He merits majesty and highness by His essence, it is not permissible that there should be in Him what was mentioned of fear, rather by it is the perfection of wisdom and the highness of rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a contradiction. Do you not see that He has contradicted the saying of the Dualists by what you mentioned, and His saying is not accepted with a counter-argument like it concerning Allah: that He is able over what He knows He will not do, for then He would be able to contradict His lordship, and by its like they made in other than Him a counter-argument necessitating that, so its like is what we are in. And another aspect: that He does not necessitate power nor knowledge by His saying: 'He has power such-and-such and knowledge such-and-such' without meaning for Him, and that is actualized in other than Him, so the counter-argument is necessary for him. Also, Allah, since He is powerful by His essence, knowing by His essence, it is impossible to describe Him with what was mentioned, for by that His lordship is completed, His sovereignty is majestic, and His divinity. And sometimes the thing described by its essence, when for other than Him authority is established, His power runs over it, like the accidents which are of different kinds for themselves, and the bodies for them, or the bodies which are self-subsistent and the accidents are by them. Then Allah has authority and dominion over them. So if we were to establish over Allah power to nullify His decree and revoke His plan and remove His knowledge and negate the reality of His report, He would be under the power of another and under a different authority.. Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation. With what the absoluteness returns to the command, and we have explained it. And two issues concerning power against the Qadariyya which necessitate that Allah is not powerful by His essence. One of them: that they said: Allah, exalted be His praise, has power over the movements and stillness of the servants, and when He empowered them over those movements and stillness
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a sustained theological argument refuting the idea that God can be subject to command or that His power can be limited or compared to created powers. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by stating that the command (al-amr) is as previously mentioned: through granting power (al-iqdar), one becomes empowered and a vicegerent, but this does not apply to God. He then counters an opponent (likely Abu Ali al-Jubba'i or the Mu'tazila) by asserting that reason is the path to knowledge, and the divine command does not contradict reason. Without divine command, rational interpretation would be 'wild' (wahshiyyan). Maturidi argues that one either knows the wisdom in the command or does not; if one does not, he should not reject it based on what he cannot grasp. From the witnessed world, it is observed that the powerful are elevated by their power and cannot be commanded; command implies humiliation and enslavement (dhilla wa isti'bad), which does not apply to God, whereas granting power (iqdar) implies elevation ('uluww). Therefore, command does not necessitate action, as many commands go unheeded. What is mentioned of God is by His power, will, and sovereignty; His lordship is completed and He merits majesty by His essence. Fear cannot apply to Him; rather, in Him is perfect wisdom and high rank. To necessitate fear or limitation for another is a contradiction (naqd). Maturidi then uses the example of the Dualists (al-thunawiyya) to show that such a contradiction invalidates their view. He rejects the counter-argument that God has power over what He knows He will not do, as that would imply power to negate His own lordship—an impossibility. He then presents another aspect: God does not necessitate power or knowledge through a statement like 'He has power and knowledge' without meaning, as that is realized in others. For God, being powerful and knowing by essence, it is impossible to describe Him with such attributed limitations, for by His essence His lordship, sovereignty, and divinity are complete. Maturidi draws an analogy from accidents and bodies: accidents are different in themselves and bodies are self-subsistent with accidents, yet God has authority over them. If we were to posit a power over God to nullify His decree or remove His knowledge, He would be under another's power—impossible. The passage concludes by noting that absoluteness (al-itlaq) returns to the command, and then introduces two issues against the Qadariyya that would imply God is not powerful by His essence. The first issue is their claim that God has power over the movements and stillness of servants, but when He empowers them, those actions become theirs—leading to a contradiction if God's power is then considered limited.

الذلة
الاستعباد
الإقدرا
الرفعة
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can be commanded like a created being, implying subordination.
Correction: Maturidi argues that command entails humiliation, which is impossible for God. Only granting power (iqdar) befits Him.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about command in the witnessed world?
A) Command elevates the one who commands.
B) Command contains humiliation and enslavement.
C) Command is the same as granting power.
D) Command applies to all beings.

Key Takeaway
Command implies humiliation and cannot be attributed to God; He grants power which implies elevation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear God's commands, do you ever feel a sense of burden or resentment? How does Maturidi's distinction between command (implying humiliation) and granting power (implying elevation) reshape your understanding of why God commands—and how should it change your response?
?
The passage says 'there is no power except by Allah.' In what specific area of your life today are you acting as if your power comes from yourself, forgetting that all power is from Him? What would it look like to acknowledge that today?
?
You likely believe in God's majesty, but do you ever subconsciously attribute limitations to Him—like thinking He needs your obedience for His benefit? How does Maturidi's argument that command implies humiliation and cannot apply to God challenge such thinking, and what will you do to correct it?

Divine Attributes
advanced
15 min
Refuting the Idea of Power Over God
Maturidi refutes Dualists and the notion of power over God via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for any power to be exercised over God, and how Maturidi refutes the Dualists and analogous arguments.

Core Idea
No power can nullify God's decree; He is above all subjugation.

Maturidi extends his argument by refuting the Dualists and any claim that God might have power over what He does not do. He provides an analogy from accidents and bodies to illustrate that if another power could affect God, He would be under that power—an impossibility. This lesson covers the intricate philosophical reasoning defending God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the structure: refutation of Dualists, another aspect about God's essence, and the analogy of accidents and bodies.
2
Follow the Dualist refutation
Identify how Maturidi uses the Dualists' own logic against them, showing that God cannot have power over what He will not do without contradiction.
Key phrase: 'فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته' (then He would be able to nullify His lordship).
3
Understand the 'another aspect' argument
Maturidi argues that describing God with 'power over such-and-such' without meaning is not applicable to God, as He is powerful by essence.
4
Analyze the accident-body analogy
See how Maturidi says that even though accidents and bodies are under God's power, we cannot posit a power over God to change His decree, as that would make Him under another's dominion.
Conclusion: 'فتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا' (Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation).

The first issue is their claim that God has power over the movements and stillness of servants, but when He empowers them, those actions become theirs—leading to a contradiction if God's power is then considered limited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God has power over what He knows He will not do, implying a limitation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such a power would allow God to nullify His lordship, which is impossible. God's power is by essence, not relative.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would happen if we established a power over God to nullify His decree?
A) It would complete His lordship.
B) He would be under the power of another.
C) It would prove His omnipotence.
D) It would be consistent with His essence.

Key Takeaway
It is impossible for any power to be exercised over God; He is absolutely transcendent and sovereign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever harbored the thought that your sincere dua could somehow force God to change His decree against His will? How does Maturidi's argument that no power can nullify God's decree challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your trust in God's wisdom?
?
In which area of your life do you most often act as if God's authority is limited or negotiable—your finances, relationships, or future plans? What would change today if you truly believed that His lordship is absolutely sovereign and none can revoke His decree?
?
Consider a recent disappointment where you wished God's plan had been different. How does the truth that no being can exercise power over God transform your perspective on that situation? Will you surrender that area to His perfect decree, or will you continue to resist?
